THE RED PAGE
THE COLISEUM.
Over the Coliseum watched the moon
Like a forgotten lover who returns
Faded with years, and, still remembering,
Yearns from afar over a loveliness
Long faded, too.
So through the dying years
The wan old moon is drawn again to her
Whom once he loved, and drapes her in the gre
And silver of his memories ; so through
All Time these two white things keep helple
tryst,
And gaze and gaze blankly at a blank face,
And with a pang almost forgotten pass
Heedlessly into forgetfulness again.
Dumb as Eternity, Home drowsed outside.
So many histories she had outwatched ;
So many crimes and splendors folded down,
And underneath new ruins long forgot;
Over so many grandeurs softly drawn
Her grey oblivion ; and of Time so much
Had vaguely obliterated, that slie slept,
Sated and tired of Empire, sick of sway,
Content among her palaces to dream ;
Beneath her old, tremendous memories
Bewitched, like drowned Atlantis, from tl
world.
And in the quiet of the Coliseum,
In that wide crater of dead passions—l,
In all my arrogant modernity !
Only the night lived there. Arch upon arch,
Tier over tier, the Coliseum seemed
Built of white silence, or a dream thing drawn
From waning memories by the wizard-moon.
And on these quiet galleries, that once
With the quick pulse of life had richly throbbed,
A throng of mute, incurious stars looked down ;
And on the edge where once the sailors stood
To roof the universe with painted silk
A swirl of pigeons fluttered distantly.
And as I stood and watched, Antiquity
With all her centuries whirled in on me
Like a swift ruthless rain ! Within this pile
Of buried passions drowsed Eternity !
I felt Time like a weight that crushed me down
And held me breathless and impersonal.
'Beneath that vast and pitiless regard
My frail modernity was stripped from me,
And I lay naked in the dust of Time.
And all the years—impassive lookers-on—
Sat in their high seats, tier on tier, at gaze !
I knew I did not live—had never lived—
This transient life that jealously I called
My one, and clung to as,a child her doll,
And draped it with a thousand lordly dreams.
For in my body dead souls stirred and waked ;
And I was but the grave of unquiet ghosts
That moaned and wondered, twitched and stirred
and fought,
And maimed and murdered endlessly. This soul
Was but the desolated battleground
For issues undecided, ancient feuds
Begotten in the ape, revenges vowed
Before my earliest self strangely erect
Stood balancing uncouthly. Centuries
On centuries of strife were summed in me.
What fierce vendettas cunningly bequeathed
Down the long generations snarling went !
And in this self were sometimes victories,
With their fierce slaughters and their fiercer
paeans,
Sometimes defeats, despairs unmitigaHle
But never silence, nightfall... never death!
And all the years—impassive arbiters—
Sat in their high seats, tier on tier, at gaze !
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Ibsen
IT was an uncouth and unprecedented
blossom that suddenly flowered from
the cactus-stem of Henrik Ibsen’s
mean and unpretentious ancestors.
Genius works that way. Life takes
generations of the undistinguished and
the conventional to produce the revolt
that js called genius. It is from an
unimaginative and obscure stock that the
exotic blossom comes, and after its
strange flowering the plant goes peaceably
on again with its normal' life. It has
exhausted itself with its one miraculous
effort.
Henrik Ibsens life was a dour and ter-
rible one; and Ibsen’s genius would
have probably died unacknowledged had
not the State—more than once—come to
his aid with a pension. His was a
nature that developed late, and only
with extraordinary difficulty. Hone
of us |would wish to have had the
youth of this ugly duckling— the un-
gainliest 'in literary history. “ A surly
dunce,’ he had a kind of schooling in
his bleak Norwegian home, and at the
age of sixteen he was apprenticed to an
apothecary for five years. As Edmund
G-osse says in his altogether admirable
life of Ibsen (Hodder and Stoughton,
per Dympck’s Library, Sydney), it
was the-atmosphere of Grimstad and of
its drug-store which moulded the
character of Ibsen. He hated this re-
mote Norwegian coast-town as he had
hated his birthplace. He was savage
in manners, short in stature, truculent,
and “not quite nice.” The estimable
girls of Grimstad did not like him
“ because he was so spectral .”
\f>
At the age of 21 this rumbling volcano
was shaken into activity by. the upheaval
of the French Revolution. His youth
had been one ineffectual struggle to
express himself, to find his tongue. But
Grimstad had set its seal deep upon him,
and though he ultimately became a
medal-decorated citizen of Europe, an
exile from his family and his hated Nor-
way, in his art he could never escape
from Grimstad. An understanding of
Ibsen can only be acquired when Ave re-
member that every one of his social plays
was set in Grimstad.
He escaped from the chemist’s shop to
Christiania, where, dreadfully poor, he
yet managed to exist on journalism. At
last a little play of his was produced.
It failed. He founded a neAvspaper. It
failed. Then the Avay opened for him—
an insignificant and depressing way. For
Norway had, what Australia has not yet,
national theatres. To the newly-opened
national theatre at Bergen Ibsen went
as manager at an annual salary of less
than £7O.
This (says Gosse) was the turning-point of
Ibsen’s life ; this blind step in the dark,
taken in the magnificent freedom of youth,
was Avhat made him what he became. No
Bergen in 1851, Ave may say, and no Doll's
House or Hedda Gabler ultimately to follow.
For five years he remained at Bergen,
making no friends, doggedly managing
his little theatre, uncertain of himself,
himself unripe. Now, “in his dark
way,” he fell in love. He was offered
the directorship of the Norwegian
Theatre at Christiania. He married.
The theatre was badly supported, his
slender salary seldom paid. There his first
acknowledged masterpiece, The Pretenders,
was at last written and produced. But
still Ibsen, as director of the theatre,
. starved on his pound a week. He appealed
in vain to the Government for a dichter-
gage—“'a pension as poet,” such as (O,
Australians !) is freely given to talent in
the Northern countries. In despair, he
applied to the King, and was granted a
pension of £9O a year. The Christiania
press announced the news with despicable
insults. But Ibsen Avas enabled by this
help to leave his narrow native North
for the Avorld. He found it narrower.
He Avent to Italy, and there, at last, he
wrote Brand.
Brand placed Ibsen at a bound among the
greatest European poets of his age. The
advance over the sculptural perfection of
The , Pretenders and the graceful wit of
Love’s Comedy was so great as to be start-
ling. Nothing but the veil of a foreign
language, which the best translations are
powerless to tear aAvay from noble verse,
prevented this mastery from being perceived
at once. . . It bears some remote likeness
to Faust, but Avith that exception there is
perhaps nothing in the literature of the
world which can be likened to Brand, except,
of course, Peer Gynt.
But the literary success was not a
pecuniary one; and Ibsen, at his pence’s
end, appealed to the King of Norway
and Sweden for help. The Storthing,
almost unanimously, voted him a small
“poet’s pension.” Then Ibsen folloAved
lip his initial success Avith the equallv
remarkable Peer Gynt. Of this bewilder-
ing drama Gosse says :
It is, I think, no exaggeration to say that
nowhere in the dramatic literature of the
world, not .by Shakespeare himself, is there
introduced into a play so much loveliness of
scenery, and such varied and exquisite appeal
to the eyes, as there is in Peer Gynt.
. a g e of 50, Ibsen was recog-
nised as the greatest living poet of the
North.' But he was moving slowly on to
prose. He had outgrown the metrical
form, the legends of his youth ; he saw a
stronger, sterner work ahead. He was
ready to treat psychologically of modern-
ism, and the only vehicle for that was prose.
Incidentally, he was also re-making him-
self. He developed from the poet into
the man of business. The velveteen jacket
was discarded for the frock-coat.
V ith his gold spectacles, his Dundreary
whiskers, his broadcloth bosom, and his
quick, staccato step, he adopted the pose of
a gentleman of affairs, very positive, and
with no nonsense about him.
At the age of 30 he had been a
commonplace-looking little man ; but
with middle-age he began to dress the
part. W illiarn Archer remarks upon
“ the high and massive forehead, crowned
with a mane of hair, the tight-shut, thin-
lipped mouth.” And in 'Christiania, in
his old age,
He looked quite small under the blaze of
crosses, stars and belts which he displayed
when he unbuttoned the long, black over-
coat. His little hands were always folded
away as he tripped upon his tiny feet. . . .
He must sit day after day in the same chair,
at the same table, in the same corner of the
cafe. ... It was a pain to him to have the
chain of his thoughts suddenly broken, and
he could not bear to be addressed by chance
acquaintances. He never went to a theatre,
except on the very rare occasions when he
could be induced to be present at the per-
formance of his own plays. He possessed
very little knowledge of literature.
As for his change from poet to prose-
writer, he justified himself when re-
proached for not having written Emperor
and GaliUeau in verse :
We are no longer living in the days pf
Shakespeare. I wished to produce the im-
pression qn the reader that wdiat he was
reading Ivas something that had really
happened. . . . What 1 desired to depict
were human beings, and therefore I would
not let them talk “ the language of the
gods.”
vl/
And now we come to Ibsen’s little love
affair. Of course he was married; and
his wife survived him, and nursed him
with devotion all through those last
silent years of the dramatist’s life. But
when he was over 60 he fell in love with
a Viennese girl of 18. She used to sit on
a bench and watch the poet stalk past,
and one day she smiled at him. And
Ibsen smiled'back, and she made room
for him on the bench. And when they
parted Ibsen wrote to a friend, “ Oh, you
can always love, but I am happier than
the happiest, for lam beloved.” It was
great fun—for the girl; but for the old
man it was “ the most beautiful and the
most harmonious portion of my whole
existence.” And—a characteristic femi-
nine touch—the world was quite ignorant
of the episode until the correspondence
was published in 1906, “at the desire of
the lady herself.”
By this time his fame filled Europe,
and everywhere his progress was a
triumph. He grew rich, for he was a genius
and also an excellent business man. He
died the richest private citizen of Chris-
tiania, wholly in consequence of the care
he had taken in protecting his copyrights
and administering his receipts.
And now the celebrated afternoons at the
cafes had begun. In Rome, Ibsen had his
favorite table, and he would sit obliquely
facing a mirror in which, half hidden by a
newspaper and by the glitter of his gold
spectacles, he could command a sight of the
whole restaurant, and especially of the door
into the street. Every one who entered,
every couple that conversed, every move-
ment of the scene, gave something to those
untiring eyes. The newspaper ancl the cafe-
mirror—these were the books which, for the
future, Ibsen was almost exclusively to study.
\f>
Not the least valuable portion of this
altogether excellent biography is Gosse’s
criticism of the dramas and his descrip-
tion of Ibsen’s method of writing them.
Ibsen’s conception of a drama may be
summed up as a clash of temperament. His
play is an avalanche which has begun to
move, and with a movement unaffected by
the incidents of the plot, long before the
curtain rises. The later plays of Ibsen
unlike almost all other modern dramas’
depend upon nothing that happens while
they are being exhibited, but rush down
wards to their inevitable close, in obedience
t° a series of long precedent impulses
order to gain this effect, the dramatist
to be acquainted with everything that 1
ever happened to his personages; and
are informed that Ibsen used to build un
his own mind, for months at a time them
history of his puppets. . . . Nothin* w
unknown to him of their experience” a
for nearly two years, like a coral-insect'
was building up the scheme of them
silence.
As to the crop of “ problems” whic
has sprung from these remarkable play
Gosse aptly remarks :
This obsession of the critic to discov
“ problems ” in the works of Ibsen has bee
one. of the main causes of that impatien
and even downright injustice with which h
writings have been received by a lar<
section of those readers who should ha'
naturally enjoyed them. He is a poet
fantastic wit and often reckless imagination
and he has been travestied in a lon* blue
coat and white choker, as though he wer
an embodiment of the Nonconformis
conscience.
But, as Gosse admits,'
In Ibsen, unquestionably, time will creat
profundities, as it has in Shakespeare. T 1
greatest works grow in importance, as trees
do after the death of the mortal men wh
planted them.
And now the end—or the beginnin
of the end—came in an apoplectic stroke
and his intelligence was gradually, bu
surely, overclouded. During his long
six years of illness he was troubled b
aphasia. Manfully, pitifully, he strug
gled with his mental disease.
At the table where all the works of hi
maturity had been written, the old man sa
persistently learning and forgetting the
alphabet. “ Look ! ’ he said, pointing to
his mournful pot-hooks, “ See what fan
doing ! I am sitting here and learning my
letters—my letters! I who was once
Writer.
He died in 1906, in his seventy-ninth
year.
Every one of us should be grateful to
whatever gods there be that he was not
born a genius.
There is good stuff, rather thinly spread,
in Sydney Partrige’s Life’s Wallaby, printed
and published at the author’s press, 90
Edward-street, Norwood, S. Australia. The
little book is composed of short stories
reprinted from The Bulletin and other papers.
If a little weak in theme, these sketches are
touched with some literary quality. Par-
trige is the author of that amateurish, yet
quite promising, novel, Rocky Section. A
book such as this is merely marking time.
The recent exhibition in Sydney of The
Society of Arts and Crafts shows this pat-
riotic movement commendably purged of
many excrescences that in the hands of
women simulate art. Still within this band
of craftswomen are to be found alleged
artists who disfigure nice, smooth wood
by making it take the forms of waratahs
and other curly, knobby things. Still
there are alleged handiworkers who carve
rough and dust-collecting surfaces upon tops
of tables and chairs, which should be smooth.
And still there is to be found the pseudo-
artist who disfigures wood by burning black
marks on it. But, with these trifling excep-
tions, these women workers are effecting
much in the direction of simplicity and
beauty for the furnishing of the Australian
home. Excellent stencil designs, leather-
work, brass-work, painting on china,
enamelling and jewellery, weaving on the
hand loom, the construction of Australian
toys, and bookbinding were to be found in
the recent exhibition; and the society,
which has pow permanent headquarters in
Bull’s Chambers, Moore-street, is valiantly
at work beautifying and simplifying the
Australian house.
In back-blocks Australia there should be a
clamant demand for Harold Seeney’s Home
Doctoring of Animals (Macdonald and Martin,
London ; 7s. (yd). This valuable work is a plain
statement of practical suggestions for the treat-
ment of all diseases of animals ; it is not meant to
oust the vet., but to enable owners of stock to
treat their patients if expert assistance cannot be
immediately procured. It is strongly recom-
mended by Harry Stockdale.
The Federal-Houlder-Shire Lines have brought
out an admirably written and well illustrated
handbook, by Wilson le Couteur, entitled The
Great Outposts of the Empire. It is packed with
interest and information.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sealed.
No matter if the way we chose no penances could
e’er make right,
Somewhere, when star to moon-heart glows, we
climbed the way to Heaven last night.
And you leaned down from out the years that,
mocking, had not let us meet,
And with the tenderness of tears set on the road
my anxious feet.
Oh ! there, between the dawn and dark, between
the star-rise and the grey,
Was lit the fervor of a spark no earthly winds can
waft away.
Though we should say good-bye to-day, knowing
we looked last each on each, '
No miles between can steal away the something
that is more than speech.
And should you follow other eyes, find other hands
can fondly cling,
From its low grave the wraith will rise of that
which was no purchased thing;
And if as strangers we must part, bound by no
law, held by no vow,
My fingers leave lon your heart... my seal I
leave upon your brow !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The New Souls.
Against the door that hath no key—
The doorway of the outer pale
Through which no mortal eye may see,
Within the wall that none may scale—
I leaned my ear. Beyond the gate
I heard strange voices in debate.
Strange voices in the dark I heard,
That sighed like some HColian breath ;
At first I could not catch the word
That hovered in the deeps of death —
Where death and new-born life abide
Like dreams, upon the other side.
At last I followed that dim speech ;
An inward understanding woke ;
I heard souls speaking, each to each,
And in dark fear and doubt they spoke;
They were about to enter in
The world, and feared its grip of sin !
“ What body shall I have to guide ? ”
A trembling, tender spirit said,
“ And shall its strength be over-tried
Ere I be free, and it be dead ?
Is it ordained that I shall fall
To endless torments, after all ?”
“ Nay,” said another, “ surely not!
We go from immemorial peace.
No scheme of Justice could allot
A punishment that did not cease,
Whatever our frail garment did
Before they closed its coffin-lid !
“Yet we that walk in human ways,
Though wholly sprung from innocence,
Are doomed to suffer many days
When in the flesh ; both then and thence ;
Set round with changing circumstance,
And made the sorry sports of chance.”
“ I hear, upon the other side,”
A third cried out with subtle pain,
“ The roaring of that pulsing tide
That surges through the human brain ;
Wherein are drowned a thousand creeds ;
And death alone shall end its needs !
“ And we that take our place therein,
And struggle through the mazing round,
Shall not escape some taint of sin;
To erring flesh we shall be bound.
Though strong as ten we yet shall fall,
And go, like weakness, to the wall!”
“Yon life,” another voice replied,
“ Is but a twinkling in the deep—
‘ He woke to consciousness, and died ! ’
Shall sum that nightmare of our sleep.
We there shall some brief moment burn,
Then evermore to sleep return ! ”
And from the door that hath no key—
The doorway of the Outer Pale
Through which no mortal eyes may see,
Within the wall that none may scale—
I drew. The voices passed, to bide
Their time upon the other side.
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.
The Witness.
We sit in the twilight, your head on my knee—
But one whom you know not sits opposite me.
Each tender endearment, each intimate word,
Is known to another—a shadowy third.
She stood by the altar, a witness unseen.
What time you were kneeling where she should
have been ;
And I looked in the eyes of a soul that had fled.
While a judgment was done ’twixt the quick and
the dead.
Though your love may obscure it, the judgment is
plain,
And a love that is greater shall sever us twain.
While the eyes of my lost love accuse nor con-
demn,
I read, as they hold me, her message in them.
I learn till it haunts me the triumph afresh
Of the bride of my soul o’er the wife of my flesh.
“ The spirit accounts not the clay in the shrine ;
For Time thou art hers—for Eternity, mine ! ”
And thus for Earth’s lifetime, whatever if be,
That other you know not sits opposite me.
VIC.
KETTLEDRUM.

Correspondents to Tiik Bulle tin ivould oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods o f address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Entice..
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor ivill carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
twc columns in length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Neivspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that, it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in lllacle and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or icash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 14, 1908.
THE STATE DEBTS AND THE FEDERAL
PROPOSALS.
The proposals of Prime Minister Deakin and
Treasurer Lyne for taking over the debts of
the States are the simplest yet put before the
State Premiers, and, on the whole, vastly the
most favorable to the States. George Turner,
looking at the matter just as he would if he were
acting for a client in his plain little family-lawyer’s
office, demanded very much more. “If I take
over the debts,” he said, “what about the security?
Please pass over your railway revenues. I may
not want all of them ; and if I don’t I’ll hand the
balance back to you.” He knew that, acting for
a client, he would be required to do that; and he
knew that the other party wouldn’t think of seri-
ously objecting. Deakin and Lyne, though they
have less reason to trust the State authorities than
George Turner had, stop long before they reach
the point that Turner reached. They ask that,
if a State refuses to pay to the Commonwealth its
proportion of interest and sinking fund, a Council
of Finance shall have power to refuse authority to
that State to borrow for 10 years ; and 10 years’
compulsory absence from the loan market is a fairly
small penalty for default. “ We back your bill,”
says the Commonwealth, “and if you dishonor it
then we require two things—(lj that we shall
have the right to say that you shall not borrow
any more for 10 years ; and (2) that we shall have
the right to put a special tax upon you in order to
get the money in.” No honest State could object
to that; the most amazing thing about the pro-
posal is its moderation.
Boiled down the scheme amounts to this : The
States now have to pay about £8,750,000 a year as
interest. The Commonwealth undertakes to pay
£6,000,000 of that annual liability out of
Customs and other revenue, and requires the
States to undertake to pay the other £2,750,000.
That is for the first five years after the
Braddon clause runs out in 1910. After the
five years the amount payable by the States will
be reduced by one-thirtieth every year; and at
the end of 30 years the States’ indebtedness, so
far as existing debt is concerned, will be abso-
lutely wiped out. The States will go on owning
the railways, the tramways, the waterworks,
and all the other now-existing permanent and
reproductive undertakings, and there won’t be
so much as the shadow of a mortgage on any of
them. In 35 years from the starting of the
scheme the Commonwealth is to take the whole
responsibility on its own shoulders, without any
security whatever—apart from the security it has
under the Constitution in the shape of powers of
taxation. Yet, in order to get clear of debt in
this way, the States are not required, so far as ex-
isting loans are concerned, to pay a sinking fund.
There is to be a sinking fund, but the
Commonwealth is to provide it in two
ways—(l) by paying its own revenue into it,
though the amount is not specified; (2) by
paying into it all savings of interest. Thus
if there is a loan now carrying 3f per cent., and
the Commonwealth converts it into one carrying
3J per cent., the salvaged per cent, will be put
into the sinking fund, which will be invested in
the most suitable Australian public stocks.
* * * *
Up to that point there doesn’t appear to be
much that even a professional grievance-monger
could twist into a wrong to the States. Yet it is
grumbled that the Commonwealth ought to under-
take to pay more than £6,000,000, and thus make
less demand on the States. G. Reid and others
point out what an awful discrepancy there is
between the £6,000,000 and the three-fourths of
the revenue to which the States are entitled
under the Braddon “Blot.” Certainly
there is a discrepancy; last year the three-
fourths amounted to £7,037,573. “We ought
to have that much paid for us,” say some of
the State Premiers —“anyhow, £7,000,000.” In
other words, the Commonwealth should enter
into an undertaking which would most likely pre-
vent it doing any more in the future than it has
done in the past. There are the transcontinental
railways to be built; there is the Asiatic peril
threatening, and a large outlay on Defence is im-
perative (and the Commonwealth alone has the
power to carry out Defence preparations); there
are the State Premiers themselves clamoring for
more lighthouses ; there is a Federal old-age
pension system crying for establishment; there is
a persistent State-frights demand for more postal
accommodation —and in the face of all that
the demand is made upon the Commonwealth
that it shall undertake to pay in interest on the
State debts practically the full amount to which
the States were entitled last year under the
Braddon “Blot.” That “Blot” was useful to
tide the States over the early years of Federation.
Two things were realised —one, that in the early
years of Federation the Commonwealth’s activi-
ties would be limited, and it would only enter
upon its complete work by degrees, wherefore it
wouldn’t at first want as much money as it would
later on ; the other, that the States would be doing
some work which they would be relieved of
later, and that, anyhow, it would take some
little time to make the financial adjustments
rendered necessary by the loss of the Cus-
toms. So the Braddon “ Blot ” was a
temporary convenience. The Commonwealth
practically offers to extend that temporary con-
venience for 35 years—a convenience that it is not
bound to extend for one day. It says that, in
spite of the imperative demand for a large
Defence, railway, pension, and other expenditure,
it will hand over to the States (or to the States’
creditors, which amounts to the same thing)
£6,000,000 a year for the first five years, and there-
after something more; and, beyond all that, it
will pay contributions into a sinking fund to
extinguish, not the Commonwealth’s, but the
States’ debts. Yet the Commonwealth, which
proposes to do all that, is accused of making a
niggardly offer to the States !
• • * *
The one tolerable argument used by those who
say that the Commonwealth’s offer in this respect
isn’t good enough is that m years to come the
Commonwealth revenue from Customs may be
enormously larger than it is to-day ; in 35 years, it
is suggested, the population may be twice what it is
to-day. But no enormous increase of Common-
wealth revenue—especially Customs revenue —is
likely even with increased population. Australia
has adopted Protection, and effective Protection
means reduced Customs revenue. In the United
States the Customs collections amount annually to
less than four dollars (19s. 4d.) a head; and for
the present the Commonwealth proposes to pay
the States no less than 30s. a head, besides pro-
viding a sinking fund to wipe out the States’ debts.
And, anyhow, supposing the Commonwealth,
after paying £6,000,000 or upwards on behalf of
the States, and after providing sinking fund,
and after building transcontinental railways, and
carrying out Defence schemes, and relieving the
States of the payment of old-age pensions, and
providing the extra lighthouses that the State
Premiers demand, and undertaking quarantine
and other obligations—supposing, after doing all
these things, that it has something left over, the
States may reasonably assume that the Common-
wealth won’t burn its own people’s money. Last
year all that the States had a right to under the
Braddon clause was £7,037,573. The Common-
wealth had something over, and actually paid
£7,845,574 to the States. From the beginning of
things the States had a right to £43,191,357 ; the
Commonwealth has actually paid them £48,919,706.
Out of the difference—£s,72B,349 —State Govern-
ments have made their surpluses, and State
Treasurers have made their haloes ; and all the
time they have snapped at the hand which has
been voluntarily providing the money.
Then there is the matter of future loans ; and it
is at this point that the State Frighters collapse
into a shapeless heap, overcome with the awful-
ness of the proposal. Nevertheless, the unmen-
tionable thing which Deakin and Lyne propose
is so mild that there isn’t a sane banker or other
money-lender in the country who, during business
hours, wouldn’t reject it with the laugh of
scorn on the ground of utter inadequacy.
“ If I’m going to take over your debt,” every
banker would say, “I must have command over
your future borrowings—unless you hand over to
me ample security for my loan.” In other
words, so far as the States are concerned,
the railways, the waterworks, the public build-
ings, and the unsold lands —a great proportion of
the lands, anyway. That would be the ordinary
business proposition. The Commonwealth doesn’t,
however, ask for anything of the sort. It doesn’t
even ask for the right to restrict further borrow-
ing except in the one event of a State’s default in
payment of its small direct contribution towards
the interest on debts taken over by the Common-
wealth. All it does require is the appointment
of an expert Council of Finance, which is to
fix the order in which loans are to be raised so as
to prevent clashing. Further, if it thinks that in
any one year the States want more loans than
Cohen is likely to stand, the Council of Finance is
to be empowered to settle, in consultation with the
State Treasurers, the proportion to be raised by
each individual State. A State may propose to
spend a loan in any wild-cat scheme that its
Ministers may conceive, and the Council of
Finance will have no say in the matter. No
State Treasurer’s cat can be so wild as to justify
the Council’s interference. It will have other
duties—the care of the sinking fund, for instance
—but so far as the flotation of new loans is con-
cerned, it is about the mildest sort of a check that
could be conceived. The idea carries all over it
the brand of Alfred Deakin’s own excessive
affability. Notwithstanding all that, N.S.W.
State Premier Wade says he can hardly trust
himself to speak, and Waddell says the scheme
is a ravening tiger, and the Sydney Telegraph
shrieked wildly about “a State-strangulation
scheme ” before it knew for certain what the
scheme was.
«■*■*■*
Just at that point it is interesting to pull off
the track for a minute and consider the Local
Government Act passed in 1906, on the strength
of which the Wade-Telegraph crowd asks N. S.
Wales to regard it as possessing something special
in the way of statesmanship. That Act, which
professed to extend local government so tre-
mendously that even the land tax was handed
over to the local governing bodies, binds these
local bodies to borrow no more than 10 per cent,
of the unimproved capital value of their ratable
lands. If a body wants its loan guaranteed by
the State, it must apply to the Treasurer, who may
absolutely refuse the guarantee, which means, in
practice, that he may block the loan. If a local
governing body wants a temporary loan, it has to
apply to the State Government for permission to
borrow, and it is barred from borrowing until
consent has been obtained. If a local governing
body defaults, the State may simply put in
an administrator, or receiver, or bailiff. The
position of the State in relation to these
bodies is just about the same as that of the
Commonwealth in relation to the States; and
when Wade and the Telegraph are the Federal
authority, that is the sort of agreement which
they reckon is necessary. Just how speechless
Wade would have been if Deakin and Lyne had
proposed anything of the sort, Heaven only
knows.
* * * *
There is one feature in this excessively affable
scheme which is so good that naturally the State
Frighters allude to it as impossible. DeaklN and
Lyne propose, in effect, that the States shall be
honest with regard to future loans to the extent
of a \ per cent, sinking fund. This very gradual
repayment horrifies certain State politicians who
have, so far, only borrowed and “blewed,” and
thrown the bill into the cradle for the baby to
wrestle with by-and-bye. The Commonwealth
must, however, insist upon a sinking fund. If it
doesn’t do that, is mighty little advantage
in taking over the existing debts; for the present
position will simply be repeated a few years
hence. And a few years hence the chances are
that the very existence of white men’s govern-
ment in Australia will be threatened, and when
that happens the Commonwealth will have to
borrow—practically no country can carry on
modern war without borrowing. For that
reason the Commonwealth is vitally interested
in seeing that the limit of Australia’s bor-
rowing power is not l-eached in time of peace.
In their struggle to meet the snapping,
snarling State-Frighters more than half-way,
it is very doubtful whether Deakin and Lyne
have safeguarded Australia sufficiently in that
matter; but the \ per cent, sinking fund is some-
thing, and that, anyhow, they must insist upon.
THE GUN THAT WAS LOADED.
One day last week Australian Treasurer William
Lyne propounded to the State Premiers his idea
of how the Customs and Excise revenue should be
disposed of after the Braddon clause ceases to be
mandatory, and the Sydney Telegraph stated
that the proposal had struck the State Premiers
with “consternation.” Lyne’s suggestions look
amazingly liberal, especially when the circum-
stances are considered ; still they won’t leave the
State Governments quite so well off as they are
now, and therefore they have made the Tele-
graph very mad indeed. Just about here it
seems to be up to that beautiful paper to look
back a few years, and remember how these pro-
posals came about, and how this “ consternation ”
arrived, and how the whole situation originated.
It is now 14 years since Premier George Reid,
of N.S.W., a politician who shifted with every
wind, and whose doings were as unaccount-
able as the banquets of an insane Roman
emperor, changed suddenly from a violent anti-
Federalist to an enthusiastic Eederationist, and
called together the conference that started the
latest Federal movement. As a result, certain
things were agreed upon. There was to be a
Federal Convention elected by the people; the
Convention was to draw up a Constitution; the
Constitution was to be submitted to a popular
rote ; and in N.S.W. it was to be accepted if the
voters gave a majority in its favor and if the
affirmative vote counted not less than 50,000
suffrages. The Convention was held and the Con-
stitution was drafted. But while all the other
States kept on the straight track, the N.S.W. Par-
liament, without any very active opposition from
Reid, who had broken out in one of his unaccount-
able new places, like the deranged but magnificent
Roman potentate who suddenly wanted boiled
lion for his banquet and wouldn’t be happy unless
he got it, did a curious act of treachery. It
passed a law whereby the Federal Constitution
would not be accepted in N.S.W. unless there was
a majority in its favor and at least 80,000 affirm-
ative votes. The Act would not have been passed
if Premier Reid had staked the existence of his
Government on the keeping of faith with the
other States, but Reid did nothing of the kind.
After asking the other States to join N.S.W. in a
Federal union, and voluntarily entering into an
agreement with them, he broke it as if it were a
joke. And the Sydney daily press supported
Reid in those days, and boomed him as a sort of
Heaven-born statesman, when he was merely a
jester or a clown, fooling round the engine-room
and turning handles at random to see if the boiler
would burst.
The Federal Convention, consisting mostly of
serious men, drafted a Constitution. One clause
of that Constitution —the famous Braddon clause,
which was then called, by the Sydney daily
press, the Braddon Blot—provided that, until the
Constitution was amended, the States should have
a permanent claim upon three-fourths of the
Customs and Excise revenue. The money was
either to be paid to the State Governments or
applied to the service of State debts taken over by
the Commonwealth. That clause put the State
Governments in a position of magnificent affluence.
But there was George Reid in the way— George
Reid the irresponsible, the performing bear and
dancing dervish of politics, the individual who
unaccountably built an edifice to-day and un-
accountably burned it to-morrow. Reid had not
cut the figure he might have done at the Federal
Convention. He looked rather like a side issue
and almost like a back number. And, in his own
words, he “liked to be top dog.” Therefore,
it was clearly up to Reid to demonstrate
that there could be no Federal Constitution
unless it was such as he pleased, and he was due
to pose as the god in the car generally. Reid
stormed the country with speeches in denunciation
of the Braddon clause, which provided that, until
the Constitution was altered, three-fourths of the
Customs and Excise revenue should be secured to
the State Governments. He called it a Blot.
Certain servile Sydney dailies, which had a wild
idea that Reid could do no wrong, and that
N.S.W. was vindicating its position and import-
ance by being nasty and insulting to the other
States, backed Reid up, and also hollered about
the Blot. When the figures went up the original
Constitution was accepted in every State where a
referendum was held, but as the affirmative vote
in N.S.W. numbered only about 72,000, and as
the breach of faith which the N.S.W. Parliament
had perpetrated on the other States required an
affirmative vote of at least 80,000, the Bill was
technically lost in N.S.W., and all the work done
was wasted.
» * * «
At this stage of the proceedings, it was plainly
time for George Reid, the person who upset the
affairs of a whole continent for no apparent reason
save to show that he was top dog, to do some-
thing. In spite of all his efforts, the people had
given a majority for the acceptance of the Con-
stitution. That majority had been nullified and
cancelled by the N.S.W. Parliament, but
it was a large and angry majority, and
unless George Reid got back suddenly from
anti-Federalism to Federalism it was bound
to make an end of him at next State
election. The only thing to be done was to get
some alteration—any old alteration made
in the Constitution which would excuse
a change of front, and enable Reid to say
that this was a different Bill, a new and im-
proved Bill, the sort of Bill that G. Reid could
conscientiously support. So there was, at Reid’s
invitation, a hurried and scrambling conference of
State Premiers, and sundry alterations were made
in the Constitution drawn up by the people’s
representatives. One of them was to the effect
that the Braddon clause was to last for only ten
years, and as nothing was devised to fill its place
after that period the Australian Parliament could,
if it chose, retain every penny of the Customs and
Excise revenue, while leaving the State Govern-
ments all their debts, and thus compel the States
to levy an income tax of about ss. in the £, and
a land-tax to correspond, the alternative being
blank insolvency. George Reid came back, with
a large smile on his countenance, from the Con-
ference at which he had driven the other Premiers
to accept this proposition. He would probably
have come back with the same smile if
he had got a clause inserted to provide
that the Governor-General should open the first
Parliament with a comic song. He assured the
people of N.S.W. that the Bill was now all right,
and that it might be supported with enthusiasm.
He had found his excuse for getting round the
corner, and changing from Anti-Bill to Pro-Bill.
He also had the satisfaction of posingas top-dog—as
the overwhelming individual who had taken the
job in hand and put everything right, after the
elected representatives of six States had only suc-
ceeded in putting everything wrong. And so
much were the N.S.W. people obsessed with the
belief in Reid which the Sydney daily papers had
preached to them as a gospel, that quite a large
section took the absurd person at his own valua-
tion.
* * * *
And now the Sydney Telegraph, which poli
tically created, or largely helped to create, the
crazy political jester Reid, says that the State
Premiers are struck with consternation at
William Lyne’s proposals for the division of the
Customs and Excise revenue after the Braddon
clause (formerly Blot) ceases to be mandatory.
If they are so struck, they should devote them .
selves to denunciations of Reid, the muddler, the
man who couldn’t leave things alone, the indivi-
dual who must show his little importance by
painting the building bright green with red stripes
when there was no need to do it, the consequen-
tial person who insisted on striking an attitude as
the god in the car when there wasn’t any car, and
who would pose as the Party Who Did It
All. If it hadn’t been for Reid, the State
Premiers could have, to a great extent, made their
own terms with William Lyne. They would
have had a lien on three-fourths of the tariff
revenue, and could have fixed their own price,
within any reasonable limits, for giving it up.
Thanks to Reid, the Commonwealth Government
need not give the State Governments a penny,
and William Lyne’s offer is wildly munificent
under the circumstances. But though, thanks to
the liberality of the Deakin Government, the
results of Reid’s very poor joke are not serious,
they might have been very astonishing indeed.
One moral is that it sometimes pays to be politi-
cally honest, quaint as the proposition may seem
to the Reid variety of politician. Anotheris that
it requires some brains to be politically dishonest
with advantage. N.S.W. should paste both facts
in its hat. It has now got another weird specimen
of a State Premier who, in the matter of the Aus-
tralian capital, wants to break faith with the
Commonwealth even as Reid broke faith with tne
other States. If he succeeds, this gun may also
prove to be loaded. There have been quite a lot
of gun accidents of late.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Our Honest Federal Government.
The scheme propounded by Treasurer Lyne for
the transfer to the Commonwealth of the State
debts is a good scheme all round, and it embodies
one feature so especially and magnificently good
that, if the whole Australian nation arose and
cheered till its voice ga.ve out and it had to retire
for refreshment, it would still have done the case
scant justice. The Australian Government lias
come forward as the first —the one and only—
Government this continent has seen which has a
definite purpose for getting Australia out of debt.
So far the Commonwealth has borrowed nothiW,
and the rarity of that good deed has been like the
spectacle of a just man in Sodom or a telephone
in Gomorrah. It has done very extensive
works—such works as the States have always
charged to loans —and has paid for them
wholly out of revenue ; and the daily papers have
looked at the post and telegraph returns, an
compared the cost of management, maintenance,
and new works under Federal control with the
cost of management and part of the maintenance
only 7 under State control, and they have sboute
“ Federal extravagance ! ” for as much as three
hours at a stretch. If the Australian Parliamen
is ever compelled to borrow (which Heaven tor-
fend !) it is certain that its loans will have a large
sinking fund attached for their extinction. As
regards the £245,000,000 or so of State debts whicn
it is proposed to take over (the States have been
piling up liabilities in a fearsome manner of late,
the debt was only £203,000,000 in 1901), the
Commonwealth is solemnly pledged by ®
Deakin-Lyne scheme to create a sinking


fund, and start the immediate repayment
of the gigantic liability. And, according
to the scheme, all new State borrowings are to
have a sinking fund attached, and the Council of
Finance is to overhaul that sinking fund and make
sure that it is really there. In short, under the
DeaKIN-Lyne method there will be no debt—State
or Federal —in all Australia which won't be in
steady process of repayment all the time. Con-
sidering the ghastly profligacy of State Govern-
ments past and present, this idea amounts to a
revolution. It is the biggest reform that Aus-
tralia has yet attempted. And Australia owes
the idea solely to the Commonwealth. The begin-
ning of Federation brought together in one Par-
liament most of the few honest capable men who
had previously been scattered among the
State Legislatures. They were an infinitesimal
honest minority in the six State pandemo-
niums, and so did little good; but when
(UoSt of them were brought together in
one Parliament they were sufficient to make an
honest majority, and to start cleaning things up.
Of course, the State Treasurers object to be
cleaned up, and their language is the language of
the sow that heard it was going to be washed—or
even worse language than that. But Australia
will appreciate the Dkakin-Lyne financial pro-
gramme, as soon as the dizzy feeling wears off and
the world ceases turning round. It is a very
dizzy sensation at present; when Australia
learned that it had an honest Government which
definitely purposed paying off the country's
£245,000,000 of debt, it got the same shock that a
pauper does when he hears that his uncle has left
him £3,000,000. But that will wear off, and,
meanwhile, the great bulk of the community cares
nothing at all for the shrieks of the hogs who
thiiric they are going to be washed.
—— ♦ —
Australian Defence: A Sane British View
In the next number of The Call, the official organ
of the National Defence League, will be published
an article by the military correspondent of the
London Times (who is the highest journalistic-
military authority in Great Britain) on the defence
of Australia. The Bulletin is indebted to the
courtesy of the editor of The Call for the right
to make the following extracts in advance from
what is a remarkably able review of the case for
Australian Defence :
In considering external dangers it is the part of states-
manship to regard all Powers as potential enemies, no
matter what their present attitude. History shows that
international alliances and friendships only endure tor so
long as the interests of allies and triends coincide. An
Army takes twenty years to create, and a Navy half a
century. It is too late to create armed force when a
crisis comes. . . .
Australia has hitherto relied, and she relies still, upon
the protection of the British Navy. But the pressure of
foreign naval competition has compelled us to concentrate
our armored fleets at home. We no longer maintain
quasi-permanently an armored fleet in the Pacific, and it
is uncertain whether we shall be able to do so again. Our
main battle-fleets —exclusive of the Mediterranean fleet,
which, if it be removed, must be replaced—are 11,257
miles from Sidney by the Suez route, 12,500 miles by the
Uape route, and 12,250 miles by Panama when the canal
is opened. Allowing an average and continuous sea-speed
of 12 knots lor a battle-fleet traversing such distances —
an average which would scarcely be maintained in prac-
tice —our battle-fleets, provided all preparations for coal-
ing and supply were made beforehand, would take 39
days to steam to Sydney from Plymouth by the quickest
route, and 47 days by the two other routes. But Sydney
is 22 days’ steaming at the same speed from San Francisco ;
15 days from Yokohama ; and 10 days from Shanghai.
Suppose an attack from one of these ports upon Sydney,
and allow that our fleets and those of the enemy start
simultaneously, Sydney would have the enemy at its
doors and on its hands for 17, 24 and 29 days respectively
before the British navy came in. The time might be
much longer. It could scarcely be less. Australian trade
on the high seas and round the coasts would, equally
with Australian interests ashore, be in peril for that time
provided, of course, that the enemy’s force is superior to
that of our Eastern fleet at the time. . . .
The enforced concentration of Britisli battle-fleets in
home waters has radically altered the situation of Aus-
tralia in war, and has seriously altered it for the worse.
When, therefore, Australians are asked, as I judge from a
speech by Mr. Reid that they are asked, to be confident
that their greatest danger is a raiding attack by a few
unartnored cruisers and a landing by 1000 men, 1 think
that the enemy is credited with a degree of forbearance,
and a capacity for sauntering, on which you cannot
count. ...
There is only one means by which Australia can
remain secure, and that is by making herself so strong
that the temptation to attack her may be removed. The
best defence of nations is attack, and an insular State
cannot attack without naval force. Consequently it is
the duty, as it appears to be the wish, of Australia to
create this force, if the naval power of the mother coun-
try does not, or may not in the future, completely cover
Australia as with a shield. The idea that m an Empire
of 40b millions of people, occupying 11 million square
miles of territory, the cradle of sea-power rests exclu-
sively in certain islands containing 44 million people and
measuring 122,000 square miles, is not a proposition that
can easily be demonstrated. I should rather say that 50
years hence the predominance of the British flag in
distant seas is not conceivable unless the self-governing
colonies begin at once to lay the foundations of their
future navies.
It will be by means of the gradual creation of an Aus-
tralian Navy, closely linked to that of the Motherland in
sympathy, tradition, and training, that, in my opinion,
Australia will best serve herself and the Empire ; and it
will only be when this Navy exists that Australia can pass
to the attack in war without help from home. But to
insure liberty of action to such attack, and to render the
home territory secure, whether now or eventually, the
Australian people must be armed. It will be 50 years
before an Australian Navy will seriously count in world-
politics, and during that time Australia must lay herself
out to create such defensive power for herself that she
will be uninviting to attack. It is not until the defen-
sive armor of a people at home is forged and riveted on
that they can pass to the attack and safely' transfer the
operations of war, and consequently its terrors and its
evils, to an enemy’s waters and territory. . . .
How can this defensive armor be best provided ?
There are three courses open to Australia. First, to
lean on support from England ; secondly, to improve the
existing militia and raise its numbers ; and, thirdly', to
create an armed Australia by means of universal training.
The first course alters nothing. It leaves Australia de-
pendent upon that blind god chance, and allows a mem-
ber of the British Parliament to ask why a wealthy'
community like Australia allows its defence to be paid
for by the starving peasantry of Connemara. , . . I
remain profoundly convinced that Mr. Deakin’s
solution is the right one. I believe that an
island continent like Australia requires very large
numbers for its defence ; that these numbers must
be found, and that only the Deakin principle can provide
them ; that the increase of your existing Militia to 40,000
men will do little more, when you closely examine the
question, than provide those obligatory and sedentary
garrisons of which Australia has peculiar need owing to
the exposure of her vitals upon the coast ; that in these
days an army must he national or nothing ; that the
defence of a State is the business not of a Government or
of a class, but of the people and of all the people ; that all
or most of the things demanded by the advocates of the
second course can be grafted upon the Deakin plan in
time and season ; and, finally, that an enemy who will not
hesitate to attack you if you adopt one of the first two
courses, will not dare to do so if you adopt the third and
Pursue it in a conscientious and tenacious manner.
All honor to Mr. Deakin for his great initiative ! And
all honor to Australia if she leads the British World, and
takes a step which will not only—as nothing else can —
make a White Australia practical politics, but will also
•hr the pulse and prompt the imagination of Anglo-
baxons far and near.

State Insects and Federal Finance.
The shameful and almost incredible lack of
patriotism of certain State Premiers in their deal-
lnffs with the proposed transfer of the public debts
should make Australia blush to think that it ever
put such cattle into office. This is the situation
n a fairly condensed form :
(1) Last year the Commonwealth paid the State Govern-
ments £7,345,574.
(2) The State Governments were only entitled, under
the Braddon clause, to £7,037,573, but the Common-
wealth gave them £BOB,OOl over. It got no thanks for
this—only curses.
(3) After 1910 the Commonwealth isn’t bound to pay
the State Governments anything at all.
(4) But it offers, for the first five years, to pay the State
Governments £6,000,000 a year ; to relieve them of their
present sinking fund expenditure ; to relieve N.S.W. and
Victoria of about £700,000 a year which they now pay in
old-age pensions by making old-age pensions a Federal
charge ; and to save Queensland, Tasmania, S.A. and
Westralia the otherwise inevitable outlay for old-age pen-
sions in the future. Also the Commonwealth offers that,
after the first five years, it will increase the direct subsidy
to the State Governments by about £91,000 each year
till it reaches £8,750,000 and stands at that. (These
sums are -not to be paid to the State Governments in
cash, but will be handed as interest to the States'
creditors, which comes to the same thing.)
(5) So the final result is that the State Governments
will get £8,750,000 a year, and will be saved a million or
more in old-age pensions, making about £9,750,000
instead of the £7,845,574 they received last year. Also
they will be saved an indefinite amount in sinking fund
payments.
(6) The actual temporary reduction in payments to the
States, allowing for old-age pension and sinking
fund outlays that the States will save, will be
less than £BOO,OOO as compared with last year.
The Commonwealth, as everybody' knows, wants
this money badly. It has two transcontinental railways
to build, an adequate defence system to provide, the
Northern Territory' to open up and settle, and other big
things to do for the good of Australia.
(7) And most of the States, by their own showing, don’t
want the money, and can perfectly' well spare it. They
are mostly boasting of big surpluses. N.S.W. has knocked
off £600,000 a year of taxation—most of it was knocked
off the rich ; and it claims that it will have a surplus of
£1,000,000 even after that. It will no longer have any
need of surpluses to pay off its debt with, for the Aus-
tralian Parliament is going to take over its debt. All it
wants surplus revenue for now is to build public works
with, and if it likes to restore the recentl.v-abolished
stanqi duties, it can, on last year’s figures, have about
£600,000 or £BOO,OOO a year clear for that purpose.
That is how matters stand, and, on the face of
things, the cantankerous State Insects should have
gone down on their knees to old Bill Lyne in
thankfuless for his liberality. But they are only'
Insects, and they must needs act Insect fashion.
Twaddell of N.S.W. expounded the State In-
sect position, and his pronouncement is to the
effect that the State Insects want two things : (1)
Unlimited borrowing powers, and (2) a share in
all future increase in Customs and Excise receipts.
Formerly they clamored for “fixed payments,”
so that they would know where they were ; now
they are offered fixed payments and they want
unfixed payments. That is to say, they want to
be partly fixed and partly unfixed ; they ask that
they shall never get less than the amount now
offered and that they may get any amount more.
The States get, under present arrangements, not
merely a share in any increase in railways,
lands, land-tax, and income-tax revenue, but they
get the whole increase under those headings, and
they want to grab a share in any increase of Tariff
revenue also, and to stand no share of any de-
crease. They want to leave the Commonwealth
with practically no prospective expansion of
revenue at all—with no money to provide
for the naval and military defences which
are absolutely necessary to the existence
of Australia, especially now that this country has
received so many broad hints that it must no
longer rely on Britain for its defences—for the
transcontinental railways, which are essential both
for defence and for settlement—for anything at
all, in fact. Deliberately they propose, as far as
they can, to cripple Australia, and to render it
helpless and defenceless, by grabbing money
which they don’t require, and leaving the Govern-
ment which has to provide for the country’s
security as nearly as possible a beggar.
Some of the State Insect proposals were
incredible in their audacity. One State person
suggested that the Commonwealth should guaran-
tee the States a subsidy of £7,000,000 a year
(about 355. per head) on the present population,
and 30s. per head per annum on all increase of
population. Under this scheme, when the popu-
lation reached 10,200,000, the Australian Govern-
ment, before it could find a copper to pay for
army, navy, civil service and all other charges,
would have to find £16,000,000 a year for the
States. The chances are that by that time, owing
to the replacement of imports by manufactures,
the whole tariff revenue won’t exceed the amount
which this State person demanded in perpetuity.
He practically wanted the lot and something more
besides.
“I Accuse.”
Dr. Richard Arthur, M.L.A (of Sydney),
formulates his charges :
I accuse the people of Australia of the blindest and
most insensate folly that a nation was ever guilty of.
Though over them is suspended the possibility—nay, the
probability—of an unspeakable fate, they avert their eyes,
as they laugh and play, lest some shadow of the future
cloud the delight of the moment.
I accuse the manhood of Australia of an incredible
denial of their first and most claihant duty—the duty to
make sacrifice of their pleasures, and give themselves to
the defence of their country. In season and out, they will
lavjsh their time, their money and what brains they have
on some trivial pastime, while the call to arms leaves
them cold and undisturbed.
I accuse the Parliament of Australia of an absolute in-
capacity to grasp and grapple with the stupendous prob-
lems that erv for solution to those who are the rulers of
the Commonwealth. For three long years the writing
has been on the wall for the dullest to read, and the
weapons of Australia —the army, the navy and the in-
creased garrison—yet await to be forged.
I accuse the State Governments of Australia of a callous
and purblind indifference to their chiefest function, the
making-ready and filling-up of the empty lands of Aus-
tralia. Called to great tasks of constructive statesman-
ship, they are content with the struggle for power and
the politics of the parish pump,
I accuse the Labor organisations of Australia, in their
opposition to compulsory defence and immigration, of an
astounding short-sightedness and ignorance of history
which will bear their fruit of bitterness and mad regret in
the lives of some now liviug. It seems impossible for
them to realise that it will be themselves and their child-
ren who will bear the brunt of the murderous competi-
tion that will arise when Australia has to open its gates
to Asia.
I accuse the possible parents of Australia of placing
deliberately their individual pleasuie before the future
welfare of the race, and of that shirking of their duty
which for Australia means the doom reserved through all
the ages for those people that have ceased to multiply.
For, at the present rate of increase, the population of
Australia will not have doubled till 1955.
I accuse the press of Australia of a pandering to the
idolatry of sport, and the lust for sensation, and of a
faint-hearted and infrequent warning of the people as to
the dangers of the days to Come. And yet they are the
teachers and the preachers of the present generation, and
forth from them should go without ceasing the cry to
awake, to garrison, to arm !
I accuse the great landowners of Australia of a lack of
patriotism and a cynical oblivion of the future that will
make posterity execrate their memories. Those who
keep sheep on broad acres where men might he, and call
consistently for “couples with no encumbrances,” are
each and all the veriest traitors to their country.
I accuse those who would undermine the doctrine of a
White Australia of the most pernicious and damnable
heresy. This is a sin against the people for which there
can be no forgiveness.
Finally —
I accuse the people of Australia of the levity of
Babylon when Cyrus lay beneath its walls; of' the
paralysis of Carthage when the Roman Senate had pro-
nounced its doom ; of the indifference of Rome though
the Barbarians were across the Alps ; of the heedlessness
of Poland when the wolves on its frontiers gathered from
every side ; of the imbecility of Paris when, rotten to the
core, its people shrieked “ & Berlin.” And with heavy
heart I see no other fate for them than that of the nation
which lives only for the day and takes no thought of the
morrow.
The Lamentations over the Few and the
Placid Ignoring of the Many.
Australia, and especially Victoria, stood aghast
lately over a sudden calamity that caused the loss
of 44 lives and partially wrecked a hundred or so
others. Further, Victoria had no sooner got over
the shock than it set about finding the reason of
the trouble, and taking all sorts of precautions
that the disaster should not be repeated. Yet
the same State that thus rightly mourns the loss
of 44 lives, annually sacrifices, in Greater Mel-
bourne alone, 700 lives without taking any steps
to stop the slaughter. The railway disaster,
costing 44 lives, was casual, non-recurrent, and
accidental; the other, which is regular, sure,
and the result of wilful neglect, wipes out
700 Australians, not once in many years, but
every year. On the Thursday after the Sunshine
smash, Ur. A. Jeffreys Wood told his Austral
Salon audience that, out of 2537 infants under one
year old, who died in Melbourne and suburbsin 1906,
no less than 700 were victims of “diseases which
resulted from changes in milk caused by heat and
staleness”; that is to say, the big city lost 700
lives that year because of a defective milk supply.
And not in Melbourne only ! The death-rate per
1000 of infants during the last five years
stands thus: Westralia, 122 ; Victoria, 94 ; Ma, 93 ;
Tas., 90; Q., 89 ; S.A., 82; and Maoriland, 73.
Note the high rate recorded in the Western State,
where fresh milk is scarce and the tinned abomi-
nation obtains. Further, to drive the lesson home.
Dr. Wood quoted the statistician’s record of the
infan tile death-rate in Melbourne (113 ’5 per 1000) as
against that for the rest of the State (79’2 per 1000).
Roughly the figures mean that Australia, every
vear, and not merely once in a decade or
more, loses some 4000 lives which it might
easily save. There are no columns of de-
scription of the deaths of these 4000 ; no
photographs of the scenes of their demise; no
searching inquiries into the why and wherefore;
no sympathetic messages from Governors and
elevated cattle generally. They just slip off the
stocks, so to speak, and are put- underground ;
and the milkman calls at one house no more, but
hunts up another where there is a new baby, and
purveys his overheated and stale liquid there,
till the new victim also joins the 700. Dr. Wood
not only diagnosed the disease, he also suggested a
remedy. He pointed to the infants’ mijk depots
that exist in parts of America, of England, and of
France as means of saving infant life'. These
depdts are municipal undertakings, where pure ,
fresh milk, not stale, preserved, adulterated fluid
masquerading under the name of milk s is sold. If
Australia is to make any honest attempt to save
these 4000 lives annually flung away, it must
establish such depots. The chances are, indeed,
that it may have to make their use compulsory,
wiping out the private milk-dealer altogether ; for
as long as that person exists, there will be ill-
regulated persons who will patronise him ; and
when the baby dies, these persons will put it down,
as they do now, to the act of God, or anything
else in the world except th areal cause—the milk
supply.
A Plan that Won’t Work.
“ F.C.” writes, with a great deal of truth :
If the Commonwealth Conference of Political Labor
Leagues, which sits in Brisbane in June next, hopes to do
any good for the party generally, it will have to take a
much w'ider and more workable view of some of the
proposals that will come before it than has been its habit
hitherto. Among concrete practical proposals, which,
however, are not to be settled merely by the passing of
resolutions, are such as the amendment of the Constitu-
tion to provide for uniform industrial legislation, a pro-
gressive tax on land values, and the adequate inspection of
shipping in Australian waters. Other items that the
Conference will be asked to accept are the Deakin Defence
scheme, the Commonwealth ownership of the railways,
elective Ministries, the abolition of the Braddon clause,
more stringent administration of the Immigration laws,
and a Commonwealth Bank. It is a very pretty pro-
gramme, and, if the Labor party had a good working
majority in the Australian Parliament, a large part of it
might be carried out in a mere handful of years.
But it looks—it looks—very much as if the Labor
Leagues don’t really want to see it carried out, for
another proposal before the said Conference is that “ no
alliance or coalition be permissible for the Federal Labor
party with any other party, and, pending the arrival of
elective Ministries, no member of the party shall accept
a portfolio in other than a purely Labor Ministry.” As
parties are at present, in the Federal Parliament, equally
with the State Legislatures, the acceptance of that pro-
posal means paralysis. The men who advance that reso-
lution know well that in no House in Australia has the
party on absolute majority ; they are not willing to make
a working and workable agreement with the party nearest
to them, which would go a long' way with them on
the road they have mapped out; they apparently
want all the rights and all the privileges they
can get, yet are unwilling to accept their re-
sponsibilities. It won’t work! The party can do
nothing positive u til it gets a majority—and what hope
has it of getting that majority while it declares its inten-
tion of holding itself aloof, and thus sacrifices the chance
to carry out part of its policy and the advertisement and
kudos it would thereby gain ? The Leagues have before
them the evidence of what Ballancb, Seddon, Kingston,
Price, Deakin and others have accomplished with the aid
of the various Labor parties, unreliable though that aid
has often been ; they know'that the same impracticability
that this proposal accentuates has already served to
baulk Watson and drive him from politics, and is hamper-
ing Fisher to-day ; yet, when there is a certain half-loaf
in front of them, and a very good prospect of the whole
quartern presently,, they refuse the half. Well, the
lesson will doubtless be learned in time, but some people
make very dull scholars.
Concerning an Act and a Partially
Somnolent Politician.
Towards the end of 1906 the Bent Government
formulated, and finally passed in haste, the
Amended Gaming Act. It was a distinctly urgent
measure, necessitated by the growing influence of
Tote-Boss Wren, whose rigi me was assuming the
respectable proportions of a satrapy. Gambling
then, just as it is now, was, for economic and
social reasons, an evil. Two-up and hazard-
schools flourished exceedingly ; indeed, the state
of things generally in Melbourne was, for a
period, iniquitous. As far as it went, Bent’s
Act was drastic enough—so long as it was
respected. And it must be said that, in the first
few months, it was fairly well enforced. The
facilities for betting outside a racecourse were, if
not altogether suppressed, at least severely
limited. The parasitical hazard joss retired into
obscurity with his inelegant crew, while citizen
Wren evacuated his notorious tote. That wasinthe
early part of 1907. Latterly, however, there has
been growing up a feeling of indifference, as if the
Gaming Act were about as negligible as a
worm. Street-betting is spreading; a vigorous
hazard school is stated to be in full swing
in the very heart of the city; and intima-
tions of “open betting,’’ despite the fact that
their publication is an offence, are dreadfully com-
mon. Such are pasted upon the interiors or ex-
teriors of pubs, or else cheekily advertised in the
daily papers, as they were like this on 24th April
last :
TO-NIGHT,
Good Sport, Well Conducted.
Flemington Running Grounds.
Sheffield Half-mile and Youths.
£25 Prizes £25
100 Entries 100
25 Events. Five in a Heat.
Open Betting. North Melbourne Cable Tram.
Next Meeting, Friday, Ist May.
The Bulletin has already drawn attention to that
open and flagrant violation of the law. The
papers that print such notices profess to regard
the “ lor” and public morality as things of divine
origin. At times they may be heard from an
awful eminence reviling the want of purity in
private life, or else dissecting questions like
Preacher Nash and the Unspecified Thing with
the sniffs of a superior deacon. Yet, if it comes
to a question of breaking the law for the sake of
a shilling or two, their scruples vanish with amaz-
ing ease. Their impunity is the result of the
supine administration of Victorian Chief Secre-
tary Peacock, who nowadays is futility and
feebleness personified. If he had displayed any
energy and courage, the Amended Gaming Act
would have been respected. Now ic is an indif-
ferent sort of thing, entitled to the scorn of the
“ gun,” and the scoff of the unspeakable “fizgig.”
Meanwhile, for the benefit of those hotelkeepers
and other persons who display bills on which
“open betting” is printed without any fuss, this
is appended (Sec. 23 of Bent’s Gaming Act): —
Every person who placards posts up or exhibits or
permits or suffers to be placarded posted up or ex-
hibited or who assists in placarding posting up or ex-
hibiting in or on or about any land building or premises
any information or notice or list directly or indirectly
relating to betting or totalisators or any unlawful game
shall be guilty of an offence.
Ignorance of the Act will not be accepted as a
defence in a prosecution, and this is what it
costs :
First Offence (Minimum) .. .. £5 or 7 days
Second ~ £25 or 1 month
Third and Subsequent Offences (Mini-
mum) .. .. .. .. .. 3 months
Yet, after all, that information seems rather
superfluous so long as the administration is left to
Alexander of the loud laugh and the flexible
spine.
A Palpably Bent Government.
The Bent Government in Victoria is beginning
to assume the appearance of a candle that has been
left in the oven. The Premier seems to have
very little real authority left. He wanders around
the country making explosive statements and—
to use an inevitable Australianism—“acting
the goat ” generally. When he says anything
important it is, as like as not, denied the next
day or explained away by one of his colleagues.
Last week Bent made an attack upon Mead, the
new Engineer for Water Conservation. In effect
he put it that Mead didn’t know his place, that
he was usurping a position and the privi-
leges of a position to which he had no
right, and that he must come to heel.
The next day, Swinburne, ostensibly a subor-
dinate member of the Bent Cabinet, made a
public statement that Mead was all right, that
Bent was quite mistaken in his strictures, and, in
effect, that Bent had better mind his own business.
A few days later Bent announced to the press
that Croker, the inevitable Choker, would re-

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

From . Sydney Telegraph’s report of State Premiers’
Conference.
Why this never-ending chatter?
It’s a very simple matter,
When it’s looked at in the right light; and the
bitter, venom’d cry
Politicians have been raising
About “ friction ” is amazing ;
We can settle the vexed questions in a moment if
we try.
Let there be conciliation
On the part of State and Nation—*
All too long have hate and envy and unkindly
thoughts been rife— *
And concession, yes, concession,
In the place of dire oppression
And a clear exchange of views instead of blatant,
wordy strife.
There’s a difference, doubtless many
Where there ought not to be any,
We’ll approach each problem fairly (though we
trouble see ahead);
First we’ll hear Australia’s version,
Then, with no force or coercion,
We will point out in a friendly style the path she
ought to tread.
It may be she will not heed us,
She, who always wants to lead us,
In her blindness may not realise the truth of what
we say ;
But, ignoring her transgressions.
We will introduce “ concessions,”
And the matter will be settled in a true provincial
way.
There are points that need discussing.
With some talk and lots of fussing
We will deal with what concerns us and with some
things that do not ;
We are here in force and mean to
Fix up all that should be seen to,
We’ve decided on our verdict and we will not
yield one jot.
Let “ State Rights ” our watchword ever
Be, we’ll see to it that never
Shall we recognise Australia in the things that are
her own.
Then, if there is no progression.
We will fall back on “ concession,”
But please understand it must come from the
Federal crowd alone.
N.S.W.
