The Sunshine Smash.
The report of Professor Kernot, on the condition of
the brakes on the Bendigo train which did such a
ghastly deed of slaughter on Tait’s Victorian
railways, suggests that it is up to Tait to consider
his position. Stripped of as much verbiage as pos-
sible, the Professor’s explanation is as follows :
As the quantity of air in each small reservoir is limited,
the power of brake may be lost or reduced by either
leakage at the niston, or too long movement of the piston.
With leaky piston the brake may be firmly applied for an
instant, but it speedily leaks off and becomes inoperative.
With a too long stroke the pressure continues, but is
poor and feeble all the time. The first of these evils is
met bv carefully and frequently examining the pistons,
keeping the leather packings of the latter well lubricated,
and renewing them when worn out. The second evil is a
constantly growing one. Every application wears away a
certain amount of metal from the brake blocks, thus per-
mitting the pistons to travel further in their cylinders
and reduce the effective pressure by expansion. To meet
this, adjusting screws and pins are provided in the con-
nections, and these need constant attention. If these
adjustments are wrongly treated or neglected great evils
ensue. If the piston travel is too small the pressure on
the brake is too high, and the wheels may be skidded,
the result being loss of brake power and grinding of flat
places on the wheels. If too large—and this is the more
likely to occur, as the blocks wear away—the brake
becomes weak, andfinally quite useless. The Westinghouse
Company publish instructions on this point, giving maxi-
mum and minimum travel for each type 6f cylinder. Ap-
pendix 5 attached will show how these have been neglected
in the train responsible for the collision, the piston travel,
as measured prior to our tests of 28th April, being in every
case except the second engine and tender abnormal. A
further appendix, No. 4. giving the result of careful
examination of brake blocks supposed to be effectual on
five different occasions, shows that out of eight vehicles
onlv four acted properly every time. Two acted four
times out of five, one acted feebly and partially four times
out of five, and one never acted at all. These results I
regard as most unsatisfactory.... I should say that
the brakes on the Bendigo train were certainly feeble.
That report seems to leave three alternatives : (1)
That there was needless neglect on the part of
somebody who should have kept the brakes in
repair; (2) that there was excusable neglect
because there weren’t enough men to do the
repairs, and considering the hideous economy in
the number of employees for which the Victorian
railways are notorious, this possibility is worth
looking into ; (3) that the brakes were so old,
worn, leaky and inefficient by reason of the
starving of repairs that no one was responsible
save the people who invented and maintained this
starving process. In the face of Professor
Kernot’s report, why is not Commissioner
Tait included among the offcials who are sus-
pended pending inquiry?
The Braybrook smash is pretty certain to revive
“the battle of the brakes” in a more or less
aggravated form ; for whatever they may decide
about it, the fact will remain that when tests were
made, the brakes on certain carriages and trucks,
as well as on No. 2 Bendigo engine, failed utterly.
The brakes in general use in Australia and
America have got such a grip that no other sys-
tem can push its nose in ; and the chancesxre that
that isn’t good for the system that has gdt the run
of things. Besides, the thing isn’t settled for
ever. Six years ago the Railway Engineer
and other technical papers published particu-
lars of most exhaustive tests, made on
the Austrian railways, and the results that
time were in favor of the vacuum brake,
which is the great rival of the compressed-air
system. What, however, is of more immediate
interest is that the examiners in that case said
that the compressed-air brakes occasionally failed
in an ordinary application without any apparent
reason. Now, a day or so after the Braybrook
smash, a sort of full-dress rehearsal was held, and
because the brakes pulled the train up, it was sub-
mitted that the smash couldn’t have been caused
by a failure of the brakes —just as though it had
been suggested that the brakes would fail every
time. In the English Board of Trade returns,
brake failures have a place; and, moreover,
the barrackers for vacuum brakes are careful to
point out that for years past their system is de-
bited with fewer failures per train mile than the
rival compressed-air system. It is ' altogether a
complicated and highly technical matter ; but even
the layman can see that the last word hasn’t been
said, while a study of the vast literature of the
subject shows that the public’s—and a good many
railway men’s —confidence in the infallibility of
the apparatus is very touching, no doubt, but very
foolish.

“A Sugar Worker” makes these statements
about wage rates in N.Q., which suggest that the
white man can’t live there—at the rates :
In the 00-operative Central Mill at Nelson, about 30
hands were employed to overhaul everything preparatory
to crushing, at wages from 225. 6d. per week downwards,
and what is commonly called “ tucker.” The quality of
the latter shows that the authorities of the con-
cern have not quite grasped the idea of a white
labor industry. Corned shins, a casual bit of steak
fried in water, now and again a few spuds as a
particular luxury ; tea, sugar, and bread without butter
or even molasses, constitute a fare not designed to keep
a white man in good health. The men decided to bring
the matter before the manager. They did so, and the
four spokesmen w r ere sacked, whereupon the rest, as
loyal mates, came out, but the evening saw all their
places filled again by other men.
During the last season the Central mill w r as famed
for its genero-ity. The men were “ found ” in a way,
and received 255. per week, out of which they almost
invariably spent 6s. Bd. to improve their fare.
It is a very mistaken idea in the South, that only the
scum of Australia’s toilers is coming here to work in the
sugar industry. I never saw more orderly men engaged
in any industry. There is no room here for the city
wreck, as he could simply not endure the hard graft.
The sugar workers formed last season a Sugar Workers’
Association, but out of nearly 500 men employed in this
district only about 150 joined, and these were all cane-
cutters. The mill hands, much abused as they u'ere by
their employers, could not muster the pluck to defend
themselves.
The workers here have, in the majority of cases, to
deal with employers still hostile to a white labor policy,
though it has been proved in the busy time cheaper and
more reliable than alien labor. Men are particularly to
be warned not to.tumble into any kind of agreement held
out to them by mills or cane-growers, but to inquire first
into the prevailing conditions, for the southern man is
practically a “ new chum ” in the North.
THE GREAT DEAD.
There died last Monday at the age of 57,
Australia’s Noblest Son, though no one ever
fastened the gaudy title upon him. Charles
Cameron Kingston, of South Australia, was
a good Australian all the time, an ! a good
Democrat all the time. Moreover, he
was a Whole Hog Australian and a Whole
Hog Democrat. His brilliant intelli-
gence and his great administrative capa-
city lifted him high above the category
of good Australians and good Demo-
crats who can merely talk but not act.
Kingston was the first stable influence that
came into S.A. politics. Before he became
Premier, the State had had 39 Ministries,
of which eight didn’t live a month and
21 didn’t outlast a year. Kingston’s Min-
istry lasted 61 years, mainly by reason of the
fact that it was the first strong, honest and
capable Democratic Government S.A. had
possessed. Its leader battled for woman
suffrage, for the graduated land-tax,
for a State bank, for industrial arbitra-
tion, for Protection, for payment of mem-
bers, for land settlement, and the break-
ing up of big estates, for Upper House
reform, for honest finance and the re-
duction of borrowing ; and he carried a won-
derful number of his ideas into the region of
what is called “ practical politics.” He was
one of the mostenthusiasticofFederationists,
and hebroughtinto theFederalmovementthe
all-Australia idea —not the idea that Federa-
tion was a scheme whereby each State should
try to get something at the expense of its
neighbors. While Turner and Forrest
were half-hearted, and Reid looked on the
Federal movement as one of the jokes of his
humorous political career, Kingston went
into his country’s business heart and soul.
When he appeared in England, along with
the polite Barton, and the affable Deakin,
and the pleasant but ordinary DICKSON,
and the fussy but painfully inadequate
Fysh, to see the Australian Federation
Bill through the British Parliament, he
was, according to common report, the
one member of the delegation whose
strenuous advocacy of Australian interests
made the tail of the Colonial Office swell to
unwonted dimensions. When the first F ederal
Cabinet was formed, Kingston was Minister
for Customs. The Bulletin urged his claims
as Prime Minister, and on his record no other
man in Australia had so good a claim. He
was the greatest man in Australia’s first
Parliament. But the pomp and dignity of
the Big States had to be considered, so the
first and third portfolios went to N.S.W.,
and the second and fourth to Victoria, and
Kingston, the very big man from the small
State, came fifth. In his new capacity he
toiled enormously, as he had always done;
he is reputed to have worked 15 hours a day
while preparing the first Federal tariff.
Then the Foreigntraders killed Kingston.
He and George Turner shared the work
of carrying the tariff through the House of
Representatives, but Kingston fought, while
Turner mostly compromised. It was in the
days when there were practically no Stand-
ing Orders, and when obstruction was un-
limited. Then McMillan, the importer,
was deputy-leader of the Opposition, and
Pumpkin Smith, the importers’ friend, who
could talk for seven hours and say not one
word worth hearing, was his chief organiser.
The Foreign-trade minority, clamoring for
the destruction of Australian industry, howl-
ing that the goods of every cheap nigger on
earth should be introduced to Australia free
of duty to cut down the wages of Australian
workmen and destroy Australian capital,
shouting through endless night-and-day
sittings for the abundant and un-
checked influx of the products of Ah
Sin, the Chinaman, and Ram Bux,
the Hindu, brought about Kingston’s
death, even as the interminable snarling of
many small animals may wear out a lion
through want of sleep and incessant worry.
He was a very sick man when the trouble was
over,andheneverrecovered. Heretiredfrom
the Ministry in 1903, because the Cabinet was
not progressive enough to sui thi m; th e trouble
was over his demand that the benefits of the
Federal Arbitration Bill should be extended
to railway employees. But, in any case, his
career was finished, and he spent the remain-
ing years of his life in a slow process
of dying. Of the miserably small men
who talked the great Kingston to death in
the anti-Australian cause, somearedead, some
have dropped into the contemptandobscurity
that they deserve, and the rest are mostly
dropping. And they can’t drop faster than
they deserve. It was a great tribute to the
falling giant that in 1903, when he Was notori-
ously decaying, and in 1906, when he was
known to be dead to all intents and purposes,
he was returned for Adelaide unopposed. It
was felt that he had a claim on his State and
on Australia which wouldn’t be paid in full
till he was really in his coffin.
From the Federal gallery :
C. C. Kingston had been a failing man these
several years. The first tariff fight killed him.
It is necessary to know all the circumstances to
understand how bitter was the stress of that fight
to him. The original Kingston tariff did not repre-
sent Kingston’s idea of Protection, nor did it rep-
resent the ideas of Deakin, of Turner, of other
Protectionists. It was a compromise on Freetrade
and Protection, a compromise dictated partly
by the financial needs of the Commonwealth,
partly by the desire to conciliate the Freetrade
sectaries in New South Wales. Another considera-
tion was the political one that a compromise tariff
would probably get promptly through the House,
and allow the pressing work of the nation to be
proceeded with. To the disgust of Kingston, the
tariff was met with frenzied hostility by the
Opposition. The anti-nationalists fought it at
every turn and with every device of unscrupulous-
ness. There were no effective standing orders, so
the opposition, which was not argument, or reason,
or fact, but merely yap, yap, yap, had a full oppor-
tunity for expression.
The struggle against that degraded guerilla
attack killed Kingston. He with his colleagues
had sacrificed much for peace sake, ant) as a reward
reaped an opposition of petty-minded malignancy
and sordid unpatriotism. Kingston worried the
tariff through, but the effort sapped his giant
strength. After facing that opposition and the
storm of obloquy which his campaign against the
faked invoice of the dishonest importer raised,
Kingston turned his face to the wall, and was a
dying man thereafter.
The first sign of collapse I noticed was at an
historic meeting in the Sydney Town Hall. The
campaign against the dishonest importer was just
then at its bitterest point. The Sydney papers
were full of the fiercest denunciations of the
honest Customs'Minister. So the masses of Sydney
(who at heart are just as Federal and national as
the people of any other Australian city) gathered
to the hall to do honor to the champion of
honesty. It was a splendid meeting, and cheered
Kingston again and again. His speech in response
was poor—a weak, rambling complaint of some-
thing the papers had said misrepresenting the
number of flags flown in his honor at Newcastle.
Shortly after, Kingston resigned from the Bar-
ton Ministry. I can recollect breakfasting with
him the next morning. His speech and manner
were those of a man whose brain was clouded.
From that onwards the degeneration of a great
mind was rapid, and led to some pitiful scenes in
a House where he had been the strongest figure.
Like most men who serve their country faith-
fully in politics, Kingston died poor.

Lacking “ Camille,” the repertory of the
modern emotional actress is held incomplete ; and
so at Sydney Criterion on Saturday night last the
management revived the dreary play of Dumas
fils, and gave Madge Mclntosh a chance to
show that she could love and die as strenuously as
the best of them. There never was a human being
like Camille; wherefore she may be played in a
multitude of ways, none of which is one whit less
convincing than the other. She may be sniffling,
a little hysterical, altogether pathetic (as in the
hands of a Nellie Stewart); or agonisingly mag-
nificent in the wounded megatherium or Nance
O’Neil manner; or, again, she may be energetic,
round-voiced and alluring till her painful end.
Madge Mclntosh conceives her in the last fashion.
The Bulletin never before saw so spry a gallop-
ing consumptive as Miss Mclntosh’s Camille;
nor heard so resonant a voice in one moribund.
True, Camille sways with half-closed eyes ever and
anon ; but one realises always that this is merely
in courteously conventional deference, as it were, to
the disease. There is little doubt in the onlooker’s
mind that, given provocation, this patient could
banish her fainting tendencies in short order,
and perform feats of strength to amaze a young
ladies’ gymnasium class. The anguish-effect of
Camille’s position isn’t lessened materially hereby.
The consumption business is. after all, but a side-
horror ; the essence of the lady’s tragedy (and the
one altogether human note in the play) is the
world-old tragedy, that occurs inevitably when a
follower of the globe’s oldest profession dreams of
becoming “respectable.” Madge Mclntosh, if
only in that she expresses with so much art the
unrestrained anguish of the spoiled woman, all of
whose life has been unrestrained, on learning that
the greatest joy life has to offer never can be hers,
compels warm praise. Nothing so good as her
farewell scene with Armand has been seen by
Criterion audiences for years past.
* * • •
Harcourt Beatty plays Armand earnestly
and well. Long practice in debonair parts has
given this actor a certain manner—almost of
jauntiness—which will out occasionally in the
wrong place. At any moment during the ball-
room unpleasantness one expects him to whip a
rapier from his trouser-leg, and, with a.rattling
military oath, to have at de Varville. But in his
scenes with Camille stageiness falls from him, and
he is quite excellent. Gaston Mervale gives a
fine rendering of de Varville; and notable, too,
is James Lindsay’s impersonation of the elder
Duval—true type of the prim, sombre bourgeois
parent so general in the France of reality, and so
rare in the France of the footlights.
*
At Sydney Palace, Maskelyne and Devant’s
show is plunging from the mysterious to the fear-
ful. In the latest novelty a doctor, while making
an incision in a patient’s neck, is startled by the
entry of a servant who lets a tray fall. A wild
flourish by the startled surgeon, and the man’s
head vanishes. The scared surgeon places the
top piece on a side table, where it makes nasty
remarks at him, and inquires anxiously how long
this silly sort of joke is going to last. The
doctor explains that the head is dead, or
ought to be, and begs of it to keep quiet
while he thinks how to dispose of the body.
But the head is insistent, and jabbers away
while the doctor and an assistant place the trunk
in an ice-chest. Having temporarily concealed
the body, the medico goes out to steady his nerves
with limejuice, and the body gets but of the ice-
chest, and wanders round the room trying to find
its head. The head assists it by giving directions,
and a union is at last effected, the body getting
hold of the head and reeling round with
it tucked comfortably under one arm. It
is amazingly clever, but creepily realistic.
A more pleasant illusion is that in which a man is
chained and manacled to the furniture and held
by the hands by members of the audience, yet,
like the dead on Resurrection morning, he changes
in the twinkling of an eye when a pistol goes pop.
One moment he is there, and the next he has gone.
Barclay Gammon has introduced a fresh stock of
tarradiddles and musical juggles which stamp him
as a past master in the manipulation of an audi-
ence. Altogether, the visitor to the Palace
is getting better value for his money than
when the eo. opened, which is more than can
usually be said for show companies.
** * *
There is no whisper of a change at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney), where “ Humpty Dumpty ”
breaks his shell and hatches a variety show, strung
together by a pantomime plot that is liable to be
hung up at any moment when it gets in the way
of a ballet, or a joke, or any other old thing. It’s
a good show, too, and deserves the run it is
having.
Down at Prince Afred Park, Sydney, the
great Circus booms along, and the booking office
begins to suggest the need for a permanent build-
ing. The acrobats and the rest now defy death
in totally different positions, which proceeding
while giving the spice of variety to the spectacle’
doesn't in any way reduce the risk. The public
gets its cold shivers in the old strength and
quantity. Of course one can always rely upon
any performer who can talk to instil some variety
into his performances—he is built that way ; but
at Wirths’, even the very animals seem to be’pos-
sessed of a repertoire, and the Wild Beast School
has apparently struck out in a new curriculum
Those wild beasts can apparently do all but talk
and are certainly quite able to understand every’-
thing their trainer says to them. Those who revel
in the classic should derive much joy gazing upon
the muscular Crotons, who now pose as living
statues on a revolving pedestal, and prove that
the ancient “Grakes” and “ Bowmans ” of whom
the only Dan’l O’Connor flutes so eloquently did
not possess a monopoly of manly finish.
* * * *
At Sydney Lyceum, Spencer’s Theatre-
scope pictures range from a journey across the
gloomy Atlantic in an up-to-date Cunarder to the
inter-State League match at Sydney Agricultural
Ground on Saturday last. ' Another fine film
shows the progress of the hide from the back of
the animal among the clover to the foot of the
dainty inhabitant of the boudoir, and the back of
the chair on which she reclines her tender form.
And it is good, and gives Australians some slight
idea of what a great industry means. A few more
films of the same sort would knock the last linger-
ing dregs of the old importing disease out of
the Sydney mind. A very fine film, entitled
“ Cossacks in Moscow,” shows what a lot of most
undesirable people can be gathered together in a
Really beautiful city. The Russian, however, on
tjae evidence of the films, is a good city builder
and recognises the wisdom of having great spaces
in a city of magnificent buildings. This causes
one to regret that a few eminent Russians were
not brought out with the First Fleet to give hints
to the Englishmen who are responsible for the
crooked lanes of Sydney.
* * * *■
Sydney Hippodrome, in a new version of
“ Under Two Flags,” is making play these nights
with a batch of British Guardsmen according to
“ Ouida’’—she (as the programme notes) “whose
sad death in Italy was recently chronicled, and
caused a thrill of emotion throughout the civilised
world.” The amazingness of these extraordinary
warriors is brought right home to the Common-
wealth resident. Lord Bertie Cecil (as to how,
why or wherefore Bert—the son of a mere Viscount
—comes to have “Lord” tacked on to him the
Hippodrome is silent) proves to be the typical
swell in the Guards of our housemaids’ dreams.
One conceives him on sight (even as his creator
did) in an incessantly changing kaleido-
scope of London seasons, Paris winters, ducal
houses in the hunting months, dinners at
the Pall Mall Clubs, dinners at the Star
and Garter, dinners irreproachable everywhere,
cottage for Ascot week, yachting with theR.V.Y.
Club, Derby handicaps ; dashing rosi&res taken
over to Baden ; warm corners in Belvoir, Saver-
nake and Longleat battues ; and all the rest of the
general programme, with no drawback to it
except the duties at the Palace, the heat of a
review, or the extravagance of a pampered licmrte,
etc. Bert’s brother, the Hon. Berkeley Cecil,
in his small cloth cap (so imposed as to show a
neat wall of head-growth upstanding starkly from
the forehead), his made-up tie, and his fine watch-
chain collection of C.T.C., Suburban Railway, and
other metal badges, is the Brigade subaltern to
the life. As to the Marquis of Rockingham
(known}as the Seraph), “ Ouida’s ” “ tall, fair man,
with the limbs of a Hercules, the chest of a
prize-fighter, and the face of a Raphael Angel”—
well, at Sydney Hippodrome, P. Goodwin is just
that particular sort of marquis.
There are divergencies from the parent-
story. Mr. Rake, the peerless English valet,
whose life was embittered owing to the hopeless-
ness of doing justice to his master’s “ tops, jacks,
moccasins, moor boots, Russia leather crickets,
turf hacks, and Hythe boots,” is interpreted by
Jack McGowan as a rather less than half-witted
Irishman with red hair. Baroni, the vindictive
Hebrew bill-discounter, “excellently dressed,
without a touch of flashiness and eminently re-
spectable looking,” is a fantastic creature, appear-
ing as Thomas Bent would had some enemy
slapped an outrageously large false nose on
him, and rolled him down 14 long and dusty hills.
The well-known incident where Rockingham
“ flings himself upon the Hebrew, tosses him up-
wards to the painted ceiling, and hurls him down
again upon the velvet carpet as lightly as a re-
triever will catch up and let fall a grouse,’’ is
omitted. It would need a steam winch to play
those sort of games with the Hippodrome Baroni.
But the grand old “Ouida” moral, which insists
that the person of rank shall be immune from all
unpleasant consequences, human and divine, is
there in full bloom. “Beauty ” Cecil is the Pet
of the Regiment in Algiers—for is he not
Noble? Arab Caliphs, sheiks and such-like fall
dead before him, (he is of the Blood); his
superior officer is * unable to assault him
(it is notoriously a physical impossibility
to biff a Lord); the Grand Marshal
of France, who never reprieved a man in his life
before, on learning that an inhabitant of Debrett
has been condemned to die, breaks up utterly and
sends urgent despatches countermanding the
sacrilege. And so on. If there is an objection
to be levelled by carpers at the Hippodrome show
it is that it concerns a British Household Brigade,
instead of, say, an Australian Police or lire
Brigade.

in Australia of the best modern drama, the society

Although the great Alfred Dampier him-
self is a very sick man these days, the Dampier
Dramatic Co. has just finished a successful tour
of the Western district of N.S.W., and is now
playing in the principal towns of the Northern
part of the State. the North has been
sated with the romance of “Robbery Under
Arms,” and other tales of derring-do, affection,
and passion, the co. will move on to Brisbane’
and thence carry civilisation and the drama
to the benighted heathen of North Queensland.

Most of the printer’s ink on the Tivoli
bills is just now devoted to people who don’t
make a noise ; and considering the bad noises
some people have been paid large amounts for
making at the livoli, this is a pleasant change
anyway. Ihe newest arrival of this sort
is 24 hours too new for inspection this
week. In the bills they call him “The
Human Frog,” but his front name is Ardo,
whereas the last human frog in these parts was
Ferry, and if Ardo is a better frog than Ferry
was, he deserves to have a large pond and a herd
of mosquitoes all for himself. In other respects,
the bill is what it was, except that the distressful
“English co.’ which played drama-ettes has gone,
and that makes the show worth 3d. extra at
least.

Bland Holt’s sensational bicycle ride, from
the stage of Adelaide Royal into the orchestra of
the same, had consequences more serious than
were apparent at the time, and he has had to
stand out of the revival of “One of the Best,”
with its English perversion of the Dreyfus case.
The Great Millionaire holds the boards at pre-
sent. The Tivoli has been adding to the
gaiety of its patrons by showing flickers of
the Sunshine catastrophe; and the Hippo-
drome has suddenly broken out with “The
Midnight Special, ’ which it considers appropriate
as depicting a replica ” of the Victorian
horror. The Scarlet Troubadours have left the
Town Hall. It is to be feared that Australia will
not take kindly to their London habit of charging
for a programme, even though it is loaded with
elaborate jokes at their own expense. “Robbery
under Arms,” per flicker, now occupies the Town
Hall. Even the Salvation Army has had the
nerve to produce flickers—faked, Heaven knows
how—of the shipwreck and massacre of some of its
emissaries. Other items of the webk are a fare-
well by the Appendreena Society to Neil Hepper,
who is going on I j regular stage ; and an Orpheus
concert that ini .duced Nellie Field, a 17-year-
old Adelaide u.il, said by her admirers to be on
the track of Ada Crossley.

Revived at Melbourne Her Majestv’s last
Saturday night, “Sherlock Holmes” demon-
strated that it is still fascinating, despite its
patent artificiality and unrealism. Very likely it
will long remain a popular madness, like love or
the seven deadly virtues. There is the central
figure, preternatural in his tall calm, uncannily
gifted and wise, and inhumanly courageous. Such
a man—a godlike sleuth and psychologist—has
well earned his pedestal of packing-cases
and his claim to strike attitudes on the gas-
box. Hour by hour, as he gathers into his web
of finely-spun postulates and deductions even that
incredible criminal Professor Moriarty, one feels
that right here is the master magician of the
bob ’ novel. His clothes fit him, and he scorns
death. His tastes include weird narcotics and
red dressing-gowns. Even his commonplaces
have an awesome significance. Yet he has his
human side—that is, he can be just as fatuously
irresponsible with the right sort of girl as a ’bus-
driver or an’Arry. This is pleasant to consider.
One likes to think of him as married to Miss
r aulkner, and rearing seven little Sherlocks, and
growing roses and cabbages, his domestic bliss
only suspended for a minute now and then, when
he steps out and detects an unfathomable crime,
or while he points his infallible finger over the
fence at the passing forger. But lie doesn’t get
really married in the drama; he merely bowls out
"rofessor Moriarty, the malignant being who
breathes fearful instructions down a tube.

Little Willie, throwing a seven, grows more fear-
some with repetition. His flaxen hair and tiny
white nightie remind one of the fatted anJels
peculiar to Christmas almanacs. And he always
pegs out with the distinction of a well-bred child.
Un Saturday night he gurgled in splendid form,
escribed the pasturesand dandelions of the Jasper
Shore with exactitude, stretched his arms to the
saints lounging on the back fence, and finally
jerked upwards in record time. Beyond Willie
it may be noted that the town of East Lynne
has risen to some renown of late. Its main
street now possesses a Town Hall, quite as small
as st. Beter sat Rome. Furthermore, the indif-
ference of sir Francis Levison as, manacled, he
strides jauntily to the hulks or something, sug-
gests that a race in the matter of calmness between
him and bherlock Holmes—the calmer of the two
to get the gate money—would be a good sporting
event. However, despite the colossal torture of
the theme, the play is at times surprisingly
well acted. Miss Frances Ross and Robert
Inman, as Lady Isabel and Archibald Carlyle
respectively, increase this paper’s estimation of
their capabilities; while Miss Maisie Maxwell and
Miss Georgie Leighford render fair service. Also,
.Leo de Chateau looks like an asset in Irish
characterisations of the robustious sort.
** * *
These days, thought upon “Miss Hook of
Holland, at Melbourne Royal, is mostly a
retrospect, in which Mr. Simon Slinks intrudes
Tvfu- vr i f n wa .V> Simon is inimitable.
All his life he has been afflicted with a magnificent
weariness that almost overcomes him. Yet, para-
doxically enough, his lethargy is the cause of his
activity. His fear that a job may overtake him
keeps him on the move. He toils hard at dodg-
ing work, and can’t be a fixed landmark for any
length of time. Even leaning up against the wall
of the Arndyk Canal is only a partial rest, for he
all the time hears the howling of the imaginary
job on his trail. To such a man life must be a
perpetual jim-jam, let alone a lament.
** * *
At Melbourne Olympia West’s Pictures still
flick humanity all over the atlas, while the froward
steam roller and other heavy implements go
wickedly on the jag. They take an hour or two
off, do these drunken luggages, and suddenly ex-
tinguish the peace of a village, besides an obese
John Hop. Proceeding, they prance through
oyster saloons and lending libraries, followed by a
gradually increasing population with very fat
legs. The legs are discordantly clothed, striped
like the creed of B. Smith, and make every ob-
struction a reason for falling down. And thus
the race continues over town halls, mountain-
chains, and distilleries, until the frisky imple-
ment has been captured in its flight.
** * *
“Peter Pan” leaves us on Friday, and
goes off to Queensland, where it is hoped he’ll
find an atmosphere more suited to his tenderness
and grace. In Melbourne he has wilted before a
nerth-easterly gale of public opinion. He has been
misunderstood and maligned by people who prefer
an Ethiopian epileptic’s gyrations to simple poesy.
He deserves to be cherished. “The. Merry
Makers ” take possession of the Princess’s on
Saturday, the 16th.
** * *
Melbourne Opera House bill remains in
the same exceedingly limp condition as yester-
week. Frank Harwood manages to sustain the
atmosphere of a suburban drawing-room, which
is like unto Irish stew grown cold and un-
sympathetic through long waiting. Alma
Grey, in riding togs and a brown wig, is
chortling “Take me Back to Bendigo” rather
tamely. And the Gibson Girls and Clarence
Tisdale are very obviously tired.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Hiphen”: Whatever else Meynell and Gunn have
done, or intend to do, in their theatrical venture, they
have already introduced to us the daintiest dancer that
has graced Australian boards for many years. Miss Essie
Perrin, of the “ Hook o’Holland” Co., is the nearest
approach to the great Genbe that I have seen in this
hemisphere. In many respects also she recalls memories
of our own gifted Maie Saqui. She is one of the very few
dancers one meets who know how to use the hands and
arms. As an exhibition of grace, it is quite as entertain-
ing to watch Miss Perrin’s expressive hands as it is to
behold her eloquent feet. Unconscious ease is the crown
of art, and that is just the fair lady’s strong suit. She
dances all over like unchained sunlight. She realises all
the floating, soaring grace that Tittell Peter Pan Brune
essays, when on the end of a stout wire she shows the
kids how to fly. If dancing is the poetry of motion, I
claim for the dainty “ Hook o’ Holland ” lady the poetess-
laureateship and any “ Red Page ” tin wreath, or iron-
mongery, or flotsam that may be vacant.
“D.’’: “The Lady of Lyons,” with which Julius
Knight wound up his last Sydney season, is the
grandmother of all British beliow-dratna. Take the
famous Act IV. The widow is recommending- her off-
spring to Pauline. “He is,” she says, “of poor though
honest parents.” Whereupon the ancient female is forced
to pause while the gallery rocks with emotion at the
dear, familiar phrase. Consider Claude’s magnificent
Bilandersonism later on. He wishes Pauline to under-
stand that there is no need for her to lock her bedroom
door. This is how he puts it: “ The sleep of virtue
never beneath my father’s honest roof e’en villains dared
to mar. (Loud and continued applause.) Pauline is no
slouch at the same game. When the noisome Beauseant
gets a strangle-hold on her, and strives to introduce into
the act some of the worst features of “ The Sign of the
Cross, she declaims: “Sir! leave this house. It is
num ole . but a husband’s roof, however lowly, is in the
eyes of God and man the Temple of a wife’s honor!! ”
Beauseant departs lest worse befall. However, “I
will not die till I am avenged,” he howls while
slinking off. How many of his moral calibre have
been saying the same thing during the last 70 years 1
Some of Beauseant’s exit-tags have lapsed, and aren’t
purloined for modern use except by asylum-dwellers.
P nnstance, when Melnotte returns and undoes him for
the manyth time, saying to Pauline’s father, “ I have a
prior claim. Before the face of man and Heaven, I urge
it. I outbid yon sordid huckster for your priceless
jewel, Beauseant (the “sordid huckster” referred to)
shrieks, “ Torments and Death ! ” before slinking off, No
villain observes “Torments and Death” nowadays.
“ Wrock ” : It takes a lot to discompose the practised
mime. Witnessed ail incident in point during a per-
formance of “ The Mummy and the Humming Bird,” in
Christchurch (M.L.), last Easter. The audience sat
spellbound while D’Orelli (Humming Bird) and Lord
Luinley (Mummy) raged at each other. Threat followed
taunt in quick succession, and Lumley hurled himself on
the disturber of his domestic peace. During the catch-as-
eatch-can exhibition that followed, smoke was seen to
rise from a paper shade over one of the ornamental lights.
Tnen a small tongue of flame shot up. An alarmed
whisper ran through the crowded theatre. The air was
electric. A woman rose from her seat with a half-
suppressed scream. Then the Mummy caught sight of
the conflagration. Dropping the Humming Bird he tore
down the shade and bore it blazing off the stage. D’Orelli
followed with the smouldering remains. The twain
returned within 10 seconds, breathingstertorously through
its several nose, and, ere the house had recovered its lost
breath the Italian fell back into position, the Mummy re-
sumed his stranglehold where he had dropped it, and
secured the fall in a trifle under even time.
“ J:A.C.” : F. Burnand, ex-editor of London Punch, has
written to the press to say that “Anton Strelezki,”
recently dead, a tolerably well-known composer of songs
and pianoforte music, was not his son, but the son of a
cousin, one Bransby Burnand. Some 30 years ago
“ Strelezki ” was a teacher of music in Sydney, but under
his family name of Burnand. He was then a young
fellow of about 20. One of his first pupils was Miss Maud
JntzStubbs, familiar to Sydney musical amateurs, then
little more than a child. The real teacher, however of
the lady was Giorza. Burnand was somewhat erratic in
ms methods, and one day suddenly disappeared from
Sydney, to blossom forth again in another laud as “ Stre-
lezki. It was then that the late J. A. Delany dubbed
hnn “ Stray latch-key.”
This is the advt. bait Messrs. Rupert Clarke, Meynell
and John Gunn have been throwing to the astonished
people of Brisbane:—“ Within the beautiful flower-laden
hedges on the roadside of life is often hidden the deadly
serpent of venomous purpose. Mankind walks stead-
fastly on the centre roadway; but some cling to the side
track, arid the serpent whispers to them. Thus good
deeds and bad deeds are created, and life’s drama is
materialised. But there is a reward for the good,
for when the sun sinks and the bespangled stars
glisten in the heavens, life is closing, and for those who
have been steadfast and true, the roadstead leading to the
Great White Gates is an open way. Lurkers will be found
m the hedges off the side track, where the flowers are
dying for sheer pity, and the serpent is grovelling in the
darkness. Now just think how the serpent would jump
if someone poured a shovelful of hot coals on him in the
midst of his dark grovel.
“Cockaigne”: An autobiography of John Hare, the
actor, could not by any miracle be made as attractive as
that of Sarah Bernhardt or Ellen Terry, yet Hare
launched off charmingly in the last Strand. His name is
Fairs. At school in London he spent as much time as he
could in theatres, much admiring Charles Kean, Phelps,
Robson, Mathews, Webster, Buckstone, and Boucicault!
Then he returned to his home in Yorkshire, and lost ambi-
tion for the drama in study for the Civil Service. However,
his talent was so remarkably evoked in amateur perform-
ances that he resolved on being an actor. His friends took
a very unusual course. They senthim to London to study
with Leigh Murray, an eminent retired Thespian.
This was the making of Hare, and he is un-
bounded in his gratitude to Murray. Here the chapter
of autobiography stops. Its most salient feature is the
suggestion Lhat actors are not so good as they were.
While insisting upon the value of conduct, Hare pleads
for that degree of innocent Bohemianism which consists
in the actor refusing to mingle with what is called Society.
Don’t let him be known off the stage, except in that
mysterious way affected by the histrions of Hare’s youthful
time.
Max Schliiter, the Danish violinist, is a bold fisherman,
and he was feverishly anxious to get permission to angle
for the eels which rise to the surface to snatch the bread
dropped into the lake at the Melbourne Botanical
Gardens. It took quite a lot of argument to persuade
him that it would be the worst kind of confidence trick
to entrap them.
“J.A.0.”: When last mail left London an Australian
arcanum was being evolved at the Alhambra, Leicester
Square. The mystery was a vision, announced as Aus-
tralian, in a pink gown and wearing a black mask, which
appeared on the stage and sang several songs of the florid
order of architecture. The papers stated that her voice,
though light, w’as pleasing and of great extension'
reaching E above the treble stave, and that she sang w’ith
grace. Australians in London were flocking to the
Alhambra to take part in this guessing competition. The
lady in question is Miss Violet Mount, a Maorilander, I
believe, who studied here with several teachers—amonu-
others the departed Hazon.
The cathedral city of Warrnambool (Vic.) was lately
treated to an unrehearsed thespian effort. The manager
of a touring dramatic company came across the local
bill-sticker, armed with two paste buckets, earnestly
occupied in covering the show-bills, W'hieh he had posted
only afew days previously, with vulgar, commonplace trade
advertisements. “ What do you mean 7” demanded the
justly incensed mummer-manager, and the other replied
with voluble cheek. Then the outraged man of the
stage seized one of the cans and inverted it on the head
of the man of paste. The bill-poster, breathing paste
and language, announced an approaching homicide, and
had to be soothed with the contents of the other can.
He w'as no glutton, and, thinking he had had enough
flour and w'ater to last him some time, took to his heels,
but not quickly enough to avoid being caught in the
small of the back with an empty bucket. The onlookers
agreed that performance was about the best seen in the
town for years.
Mrs. J. P. Burnett (Jenny Lee), whose stay in Australia
has been shortened by the death of her daughter Joan
(Joan came out under engagement to J. G. W. for “ Peter
Pan”), sailed for England in the Oruba. Her benefit
realised £250. At the matinee she simply crossed the
stage as Jo, the character in which she made her fame,
trailing the historic broom. In trying to make a speech
of thanks, Mrs. Burnett broke up.
Oscar Asche, sometime of these parts, is trying the ex-
periment of bringing London theatre prices down to the
Australian level—from five shillings down to one, with a
few stalls at 7s. 6d. As things are, stalls in any London
theatre of sorts run from 10s. 6d. up, and the public
mostly doesn’t fill them with great enthusiasm. Theatre
rents seem to be the biggest obstacle to reduction. In
London, Asche pays £375 per week for His Majesty’s, and
other houses of any standing bring anything from £250
upwards. Still, it’s been left to Asche to find out
whether full stalls at 7s. tid. are not more profitable than
partly empty ones at half-a-guinea.

When Frank Thornton was in London, 18 months or so
ago, looking for something to put the wealth of “ Charley’s
Au "t mto, somebody laid him on to the usual “real
good thing —a Broken Hill wild-cat with a magnificent
name lyd. 2ft. long. As dividends didn’t happen as ex-
pected, and news about the concern dwindled to nothin "■
or thereabouts, Thornton called at the co.’s office and
said he was leaving for Australia, and please would they
tell him where their mine was. Then Thornton waited
on the mat while a stupendous young gentleman went
behind a screen to find out where the mine was, and when
the stupendous young gentleman returned, he broke
Thornton up with this reply—“ Box 967, Broken Hill.”
Thornton looked into Box 967, but somebody had evidently
posted the mine away, for all that the receptacle held was
2in. of dust and an old bill.
A “command” performance is looked upon as a
great affair by London actors, but it is not all beer and
skittles to the management. The great Boss of the Empire
only provides first-class return tickets to Windsor or San-
dringham, as the case may be, and a good square feed.
All other exes—transport of scenery, properties and
dresses, cab fares for members of the company, etc
—come out of the manager’s pocket. London Chroniclk
stated that a recent appearance before the King cost
John Hare £BOO. A steady series of royal commands
would ruin any manager.
Amidst all the snapping and snarling at
the Commonwealth indulged in by the State
Premiers at their miserable conference in Mel-
bourne, their attitude on the lighthouse question
stands out as a contrast. Having asserted with
bitter reiteration that the Commonwealth is unfit
to shine boots, the Premiers with one voice urged
it to take over control of lighthouses, buoys and
beacons. Ihe reason for this strange conversion
lies in the fact that these works yield no return,
and the States’ representatives wish to be free
from the necessity of finding the funds for the
maintenance of the present and the erection of
future lighthouses. Also, they are under the im-
pression that, if the Commonwealth takes charge
of these works, it will at once draw a stupendous
cheque in payment of their cost to the States.
Pent declared that Dyne was at one time keen on
exercising the right given to the Federal authority
under the Constitution in this matter, but that
when he discovered that it would cost £16,000,000
ho dropped the matter. If the States imagine
that they are going to draw on the Common-
wealth for that amount for their present light-
houses they ought to be making a big mistake.
Do the State Premiers think that the Common-
wealth is some foreign body which intends to buy
up the old lighthouses in a job lot and ship them
to Darkest Africa ? There appears to be no end
to the list of people in Australia who believe that
the Commonwealth is something outside of, and
apart from the people of this country.
The Commissioners of Savings Banks are calling
tenders for their thirteenth issue of Government-guaran-
teed debentures for £lOO,OOO at 3* per cent., to mature on
31st October, 1919 ; the minimum price is £9B, and
allowing for redemption at par, the net return to in-
vestors will be £3 14s. sd. per cent. A deposit of only 5
per cent, is required with the tenders, and the balance
may be paid at any time up to 30th November, 1908.
Brokerage of 10s. per cent, will be allowed to members of
recognised Stock Exchanges, and tender forms may be
obtained from them or at any Savings Bank. Tenders
must be lodged with the Commissioners, before noon on
Tuesday, 2nd June.*
Though Kidston is keen after the siller
where the State is concerned, he isn’t without
compassion. In Queensland, as in most places,
the distressed mother can get State aid for the
support of her children. But if, with motherly
instinct, she resolves to cling to her children
rather than let strangers have the care of them,
she used to get less aid than the stranger got.
In some instances the stranger got 66 per cent,
more. Now, however, both are put on the same
footing, it having been recognised that a child is
more likely to receive proper treatment from its
mother than from a woman who, as a rule, is in
the business for profit and nothing else.
APART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a crowd on the terraced boulevard,
There’s gaiety in the air,
There’s beauty filled with a disregard
Of consequence or care ;
There’s Paris gay, and there’s Paris bold,
Intent on its joyous quest—
But one fair form in the sunlight gold
O’ershadows the whole bright rest.
The picture’s naught but a frame, forgot
For the light of her laughing eyes ;
Enchantment’s wrought on the furthest spot
Where her thought for the moment lies ;
Dulled themselves are the summer’s rays
Where her smiling glance is thrown ;
And my heart leaps gladty before her gaze
As it frankly meets my own.
She smiles to me as a lover true—
To me, of that great bright band—
Her face lights up with a beauty new,
As she waves a dainty hand.
Her parting lips and her shining teeth
Speak loud of a love divine,
That round my soul she has come to wreath—
And they tell me she is mine.
And I —l am fired with a passion grand,
But never a word I say ;
I give no sign that I understand,
And I let her pass away.
The love that I feel she’ll never know,
For there’s half a world between—
For I’m at the back of a Sydney show,
And she’s on the biograph screen.
x.s.w.

Something like 500 claims have been
lodged against the Victorian Railway Depart-
ment in connection with the Sunshine horror.
Doubtful if there were more than 500 people on
the luckless Ballarat train. Some of these claims
are almost humorous, scores of them being for
damaged attire, lost effects, broken property, un-
fulfilled engagements, and so forth. One victim,
formerly a firm believer in imported railway
managers, is said to be claiming damages for a
compound fracture of his "misplaced confidence,
which is smashed beyond repair. Another claim
is by a lady who was travelling with a dozen
gallipots of home-made jam. which she had stowed
on the rack above her. There was the wreck
of the next carraige on top of the one she
was in, the jagged points stuck through the walls,
a dead body lay beside her, and blood was oozing
all around, but the dame grimly opened her
hamper, and exclaimed dramatically : “ I here,
every single jar broken ! ” She herself escaped
without a scratch, and lives to make the depart-
ment pay for all that jam. Another claimant
bases his demand on the fact that a portion of his
belongings was spattered with the life-blood of
the department’s passengers, and that it is the
contract of the department to land him with his
clothes unsoiled. The department is considering
whether it didn’t kill the wrong passengers.
*,* * *
The breath of “ Spring ” :
One event which the Law should place its large, horny,
corny foot upon at shows is the Novice Equestrienne
episode over hurdles. It is a dangerous, ungraceful exhi-
bition, and seems to be put on only as a draw, not as any
great test of skill. Easter Saturday at the Sydney Show
saw one foolhardy woman endangering her own fair neck,
the horse’s grey neck, and a large quantity of the public’s
craned neck. Her mount was a spirited grey, whose fight-
ing blood got up after he had leaped the first hurdle. His
rider hadn’t the faintest idea of managing him along a
circular track ; she simply hung on to the reins with one
hand and held her whip and her breath with the other,
and let the grey do his own sweet thrill. The result was
that after the second hurdle was negotiated the steed
started to sow wild oats all about the arena. In his
career he just managed to miss knocking the judge off
his dignity and the lady off her seat. Then he charged
straight at the low surrounding fence, behind which sat
theprecious women andchildren of thenation. He thought
it was another hurdle he was up against, but luckily a
garish ad. for somebody’s Vile Greens hit him in the eye in
time to swerve him off to the exit, where his rider fell
fainting into the arms of Ma Earth. Most of the sight-
seers thought the event was great. When the lady
was well enough th reappear in public, they gave her
thunderous encouragement to do it again, and when she
could only give a faint, exhausted bob of thanks, the dis-
appointed silence could be heard. Yes, let the men risk
their necks because they know what they are capable of,
bur, somehow a woman never knows what she can’t do,
and very often she does it whether she can or not.
* * » •
Ballarat seems to be a first-rate place to
choose for getting rid of your enemy. In the past
few years it has seen Nicholls, Heeney, Johanna
McCarthy, Sultan Allum, Lizzie O'Reilly and
Madge Graham done to death, and in no case has
the criminal been traced. Yet the Victorian
police calmly stack the papers away in the usual
pigeon-hole and cursorily say—“ Nothing can be
done; it is ended for ever.”
♦ * * •
Having recovered from the excitement of
the tragedy of Madge Graham, and placed W.
lies away in a mental pigeon-hole, Ballarat has
been giving its attention to some episodes con-
nected with a band of alleged gipsies, who are
understood to be trekking towards Adelaide with
intent to return to their homes (if a gipsy ever has
a home) in Bohemia or Thessalonica or there-
abouts. These wanderers are apparently the
alleged “Greek gipsies,” who happened into the
Commonwealth some five years ago. Presumably
they have wandered around, laying up a com-
petence, ever since; and they still “tell
the pretty lady’s fortune,” and otherwise
acquire coin without toiling for it. One
dark-eyed damsel, forecasting the future for
a Ballarat youth, told him to tie up his
money in his handkerchief, expectorate on it, and
keep it tied up for nine days, when he would learn
something. He did—he learnt that the cash was
less by ss. Then he called the Law in. Of
course there was no case, and Ballarat laughed
loudly. Another youth, a Bunararee farmer’s son,
became so smitten with the charms of a dark-
skinned wanderer that he has abandoned his pro-
spects of sharing the old man’s wealth, and pro-
mises to follow the damsel to the ends of the
earth. The old man has passed the youth a warning
about the proverbial shilling ; but a wiser course
might have been to allow the youngster to camp
with the gipsies for a month and hustle round after
the shy coin, and the scarce meal, and generally dis-
cover the drawbacks of the Bohemian life. That
would have probably cured him. Still, one never
knows. A pair of deep, dark eyes have drawn a
man away from home, comfort, and fortune many
a time ere this. Mostly he was sorry afterwards ;
but when he didn’t go he was mostly sorry too ;
so it’s hard to say when to go and when to stay.
One poet wrote the song of Maud Muller showing
how the rich man didn’t marry Maud of the rustic
sun-bonnet and the fresh young beauty, and how
his life was miserable ever afterwards. And then
the other bard wrote about how he did marry her,
and she developed into plain, red, coarse, large-
waisted Mrs. Jenkins; and then also the rich
man was sorry.
“Adelaide”: So the Parliament House
of Australia in Melbourne is to be connected with
the drains ! (B. 16/4/’08.) Well, well /-as a noted
swearer used to say when words failed to express
his feelings. Really, I doubt if you could make
the average person in Adelaide believe this piece
of information. We used to call it “ Marvellou
Smellbourne,” of course, but we did think that
particular reproach had been removed. As for
our own city, one has to be a very maturely
middle-aged person to remember a time when we
had not deep drainage throughout, and by now
even distant suburbs have it for every two-roomed
stone cottage —we don’t use wood for building
purposes here. And yet Melbourne talks wag-
gishly of us as behind the times. Well, well!

Around Melbourne, the hog in the car is
getting liogger than ever. In fact, he seems to
have got hoggest. On tlie Point Nepean Hoad
alone, for the last few weeks, the police record of
casualties runs : a lady’s nose and wrist broken, a

and another damaged. And the hogs have not
been identified or caught or even shot at.

Among Vic. Commissioner Tait’s regulations
applying to guards are some enacting that trains
must not remain at suburban platforms more
than 30 seconds, that the guard must assist por-
ters in closing dioors, and that “ the guard must
not enter the train while in motion ” ! It was
just such regulations as these that led to the re-
cent “ lazy strikes ” on the Austrian and Italian
railroads. The “lazy strikes'’’ afforded much
merriment to Australian Fat papers ; but they
did their work—they directed attention to rules
which were not really laid down for the observ-
ance of the employees, but were designed solely
for the protection of the railway companies in case
of accident. If Tait’s regulations were strictly
acted up to, the system would he disorganised
in a day. How, for instance, is a
guard to get passengers out and in, and shut the
doors of a long train, and yet get back to his
perch before the train starts, all inside 30sec. ? It
is impossible. Even the 30sec. is impossible—one
fat woman with a child and three parcels takes
longer than that to get out. But if the train is
late, and the lateness leads to an accident, Tait
isn’t to blame—the stoppages shouldn’t have been
so long, and there is the regulation to prove it.
And if the fat woman is injured by the efforts of
the officials to keep to the 30sec. rule, Tait is
wholly free from responsibility ; according to the
regulations the train must not start while fat women
are half out and half in—even though the delay
runs to five minutes. And if the guard is injured
while leaping for the moving train —well, it is his
own fault. The regulations say that he mustn’t
leap even though he has to make up the 2min.
lost by reason of the fat woman.
Pushful Potts, director of the Hawkesbury
Agricultural College (N.S.W.), lectured the
Dairy Factory Managers’ Association the other
day, and declared that the milking machine was a
proved success, and was quite capable of dis-
placing child-labor in the milking yards of Aus-
tralia. Potts’ positive assurance on this point
suggests that the parsons of the Cow districts
might give the Ark and other ancient pieces of
mechanism a rest, and take the milking machine
for a text now and again. It mightn’t be quite
orthodox, but if God has half the pity they allege
He has, and half the love for little children, there
will be a special entry made in the ledger in which
those parsons’ records are kept.
Vehicles of contempt, ranging in size from
pantechnicon vans to mere “ bottle-o ” carts, are
driven daily through C. (1. Wade’s futile anti-
gaming legislation. Thus a Sydney grocer lias
taken to offering a sweep ticket instead of the
customary teapot or fingerbowl coupon for pur-
chases over a certain amount, whereby families
never before interested in the gambling curse are
now enabled to get a regular supply of the article.
Apparently the only effective way for Parliament
to squelch gambling of this kind is to pass (by way
of an addendum) a provision enabling the Execu-
tive to apply the intention of the Act to all new
schemes invented to defeat it.

THE FRANTIC PRAYER OF CERTAIN PAPERS AND
POLITICIANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Leave us our debts, O Lord !
Give ear to our sad groans ;
You know we can’t afford
To lose our precious loans.
Yea, hear our bitter cry ;
Our beards with tears are wet—
If we are not to die
Continue us in debt!
O, hear us as >ve nioan,
Our prayer do not refuse—
Grant us our daily loan
Our weekly I O U’s !
In w'eak financial health
We lingered, day by day ;
Now, this vile Commonwealth
Is urging us to pay.
To pay —O dreadful doom !
For this \ve make our moan ;
O God of Wrath and Gloom,
Give us our daily loan 1
Arid send us forth a sign—
The Sign of the Triple Balls—
To stay the ravening Lyne
That waits without the walls.
Restrain his fury, Lord,
For solvency and cash ;
Let things go by the board
Right to Eternal Smash !
For what to us the name
Or what to us the weal
Of this, our Land? The shame
Of debt ive never feel!
Lord, let us rest content
And cherish unafraid
The tinpot pomps of Bent,
The feeble pride of Wade.
So may we spend our days,
And borrowed money—set
Far from all solvent ways
Amidst Eternal Debt.
Amen.
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

“ A man shall not marry his grandmother,”
the Book of Common Prayer artfully begins, and
then it goes on to forbid him marrying quite
a lot of people whom he really might be disposed
to think of in a matrimonial sense. Those canoni-
cal prohibitions against the marriage of kindred
have been incorporated in the British law,
and from it have come to Australia. We
have modified them somewhat here, as, for
instance, in allowing marriage with a deceased
wife’s sister, which is still forbidden by the
British law. The question came before N.S.W.
Divorce Court last week whether the marriage of
a woman with a deceased husband’s brother was
legal. The Court said emphatically “no,” and
didn’t even ask for the question to be argued.
Yet it would seem to be reasonable in this matter
to take the obvious intention of the Legislature,
which undoubtedly was to make the relation of
brother-in-law or sister-in-law no bar to marriage.
Parliament probably didn’t mean to differentiate
between the deceased wife’s sister and the de-
ceased husband’s brother, but simply forgot the
latter, who as a matrimonial factor is a very rare
bird.
* * * *
Sydney’s primal physical geography is a
largely-forgotten circumstance nowadays, but it
still makes itself remembered in a few odd spots.
The old Tank Stream, which early settlers found
so convenient for rum-diluting and other domestic
purposes, has disappeared from the surface of
Australia under a mass of shops and warehouses,
but builders know that it is still to be found wetly
at its old address down under. The men who dug
the foundations for the new Challis House, in
Martin Place, met it in such a damp state that
they worked waist-deep in water part of the time ;
and a big concrete retaining wall had to be built
and a pumping apparatus installed to keep the old
stream from going up in the lift and washing away
the staid family solicitors and other professional
folk who toil jn the upper storeys of the big
erection.
THE UNHAPPY MEAN.
“ Her face is her fortune."
“ Oh, well, it won't bar her from the old age pension.”
Servia’s notable drunk is its Crown Prince,
who has been whooping things up ever since his
father got to the throne ; apparently he was so
staggered by the improvement of his prospects
that he went off any little dot he ever possessed,
and became a roaring maniac in all the public
houses of Belgrade. His latest performance was
a shaky attempt to shoot the ash off a cigarette in
a soldier’s mouth. He failed badly, and there was
a dead soldier, and the royal lout didn’t even
apologise to the relatives of the corpse. Poor old
King Peter is said to have a firm conviction that
he will be the last Karageorgevitch to sit on
the reeling throne of Servia. No dynasty can
last in that unstable land when the next heir to
the throne is such a bad shot and so devoid of the
graces of a Chesterfield.
“Jay bell,” from Brisbane :
Booth 8 anti-suicide bureau ought to do fair business if
*" £y er stret;c hes its tentacles into this district.
Three more or less determined self-destructors hap-
pened almost simultaneously in a country district not far
from this city. They suddenly resolved on taking the
great soul-journey within the same week.
No. 1 booked his ineffectual passage per medium of a
disreputable razor dragged across his windpipe. But the
weapon was blunt and cheap. So the world-weary one
threw himself listlessly into an adjacent “erik.” The
rivulet must have been a first cousin of Canberra’s trickle
of mud and tadpoles; it lacked the necessary amount
of moisture to float a tired soul even two inches along
the track to Tophet. So the interrupted traveller came
in out of what he thought was wet, and again cut his
throat with the crowbar that called itself a razor. The
last I saw of him he was still travelling, but not to
Tophet; he was in a spring cart going to the hospital.
Later, a police magistrate, in fine linen and a tailor-made
suit, committed him for trial before a gentleman with a
doormat on his head and a long petticoat hanging from
his shoulders. And the gentleman with the outlandish
female raiment said “Six months.”
No. 2 was a survey-camp cook—a clean, experienced
preparer of corned beef and swollen dough. He held his
head over an empty kerosene tin ; and did the sudden
episode. He was always neat and considerate, and there
was little or no splash. Came howling round the camp, a
few weeks later, his widow, accusing, in turn, each mem-
ber of foul play. But, when the circumstance of the tin
was explained to her, she said, “ Poor Gawidge wuz alwuz
so neat and tidy, he must ’ave did it ’isself.”
No. 3 cloud-chaser was found roped by the throat to an
ordinary post-and-rail fence one chilly morning. There
can be only one theory as to how this harmless bush indi-
vidual made his soul leap off the planet. He wore down
quite two square yards of verdant cow pasture in the
final skirmish with existence. Before the ultimate jolt
he had driven his heels into the lower rail of the fence,
and so he jibbed to the banks of Jordan. Had only a
Booth bureauist happened along, there might be another
live man on the land to-day, instead of merely hisblucher
footprints on a slab of timber in a countryside fence.

“D.” on a stricken revelry resort :
“ The Continental,” known intimately for well over a
decade to every gilded youth in the British Empire who
visited London and saw anything, while there, of the life
Jud is understood to abhor, has gone the way of all un-
masked British haunts of its kind. It was a curious
resort, unlike any other in the world in many respects.
Situated in Regent-street, within 100yds. of the Athenaeum
Club, the exclusive assembling-place of Britain’s saintliest
Bishops, and near to the Carlton, one of the
finest hotels in the world, the Continental by day
held its head up as respectably as any of its
neighbors. By day you could entertain your maiden
relatives there to lunch or tea, nor fear untoward
happenings. Also you might take them to dinner
—and get a fair meal withal—in peace and an atmosphere
of staid holiness. However, you would be well advised
to get them out by 10.45 p.m. sharp. For at that hour
Flossie—who was once Florence and the incredibly inno-
cent daughter of a provincial vicar—was wont to arrive
in riotous Lutetian droves. And very soon the hostelry,
erstwhile so staid, overflowed with her in all shapes,
sizes, ages, weights, colors and nationalities. She filled
a suite of supper-rooms on the ground floor ; packed
every step and each landing of the wide staircase ;
and teemed considerably at scores of tables in half-
a-dozen big supper-rooms on the first floor. Amongst
her roamed every brand of British and foreign
youth, joyous in theconviction thatin thus seeing Flossie
—who was once Florence —he was seeing Life, and com-
porting himself like a Perfect Devil generally. Flossie
purchased little drink and less provender. Each of her
disbursed a shilling for her table-seat, and the proprie-
tary scooped its dividends from the swarms of male
admirers who bought her “ wine ” and kidneys on toast
at exorbitant rates. The scenes on the Regent-street
sidewalk mid-nightly, when she and her acquaintances
sallied forth, were distractingly cheerful. Mostly the revel-
lers refused, or were unable, to sally when closing time ar-
rived, and so were heaved out forcibly by minions attached
to the hotel. London stood the Continental till last month,
when the licensee was prosecuted (25/3/’08), on behalf of
the Commissioner of Police, and fined for having “ know-
ingly permitted the premises to be the habitual resort,”
etc., etc. It was characteristic of the good old British
directors of the place that they wrote to the prosecuting
counsel immediately they heard of the proposed action,
suggesting that the summonses should be adjourned
sine die on an undertaking being entered into that the
premises should be closed at eleven o’clock, and pointing
out that they had no suspicion that the premises were
used for this class of business at all. The move availed
not, and they are debarred from re-opening the hotel on
the same lines. The sole result, so far as the non-royster-
ing public is concerned, is that the streets of London will
be flooded thereby with a still denser crowd of mislaid
females than already patrols them between 11 p.m. and
the small hours.
“ Corso ” writes :
The Full Court (N.S.W.) has decided that the attempt
on the part of the authorities to regulate the crowds on
the Manly ferry boats, by restricting them to the distri-
bution on their decks as described in their passenger
certificates, is abortive. The test Case Was the Brighton,
which has a passenger certificate for 1188 people, 833 being
allowed after measuring the main deck, and 274 after
ditto the upper, and the balance for the cabin. In the
test case it was shown that on the upper deck some 650
people were on the space calculated to accommodate
274. The Court held that the Marine Acts, did
not differentiate between the decks, and as long as
no more than 1188 passengers were carried, they
could all legally congregate on the upper deck.
As the law stands, C. J. Darley, who decided the point, is
probably right, if he doesn’t mind me saying so. His
dictum should make next season’s holiday traffic from
Sydney to Manly a first charge on the resources of the
Harbor authorities. This traffic is now of enormous
dimensions, and is difficult to handle. With a little less
luck this very Brighton would last summer have provided
a catastrophe which would have put Sunshine in the
shade. One obvious duty of the marine authorities
is to see that if, say, the Brighton claims the
right to crowd 1188 people on its upper deck, that
deck should be strong enough to carry them, and not
merely the 274 already mentioned. In other words the
passenger capacity of the boats is to be calculated on the
basis that the whole number allowed is at liberty to
crdwd on top, and the vessel must be still good enough
not to topple over. Wanted a picture of a boat that will
be quite safe going round Middle Head in a blow, with
1188 passengers on the extreme top and a few bags of coal
in the extreme bottom.

If only the Sunday-school teacher dared,
what an illustration he could get out of a
recent medical case to drive home the frayed
copy-book maxim—“Be spre your sin will find
you out.” The woman in the case was young and
charming, and the man she loved too well was a
married person, who masqueraded as unattached,
and turned up frequently in that disguise at the
house of the charming young woman’s parents.
When the consequences began to show up, the
married masquerader took the lady to a well-
known and reputable city doctor, who promptly
diagnosed the trouble. Then the married mas-
querader made such very complete arrange-
ments for the “trouble” that nobody else
in the wide world was to know a thing
about it—even the nurses wouldn’t learn
who the interested parties were. Then, just when
everything was going so smoothly, the lady fell
down some railway steps. A serious operation
became necessary, and the highly-skilled doctor
who knew the secret undertook the work. Then he,
too, must meet with ill-luck —he who was declared
by other doctors to be one of the most skilful men
at this sort of work in the city. When that hap-
pened the masquerader had to bring the lady’s
family to her deathbed ; and, to complete the
misery of everybody, the circumstances cried
aloud for an inquiry. Next to turn all eyes to the
inquiry, the doctors differed, some alleging gross
negligence, so the Coroner committed the operator
to take his trial. He was acquitted ; but by that
time nobody was wearing any disguise—the
public was looking on at the plain, drab facts.
* * * *
The alleged evils of Protection, as exem-
plified by the female factory operative, are one of
the stock jokes of the Foreign-trader, and he has
a yearning for our womankind to go on the land
and be happy. Here is the tale of One who did it:
Melbourne, Tuesday.—At the Horsham Police Court,
yesterday, Johann CarlJende was charged with leaving
his wife without means of support. Complainant stated
her husband forced her to shear 150 sheep, and lift
heavy wethers. During the last two years he had given
her £4 or £5. She and the children could not get enough
to eat, and had lived on potatoes and eggs. The grocery
bill amounted to only ss. per week. Defendant owned
3700 acres freehold, and had live stock worth £20,000.
The case was eventually dismissed.
Poor she-beast of burden, married to the econo-
mical “ backbone of the country ” ! She might
have been better off toiling in a boot factory.
There she would at least have got more than £5
in two years, and there would have been a surplus
wherewith to buy a hat and a big feather, and she
could have been a happy Collingwood tart and have
ridden on the front of the tram-car on Saturday
night.
Maoriland has fired the first blast at the
stupendous Arthur’s Pass tunnel, which is to
pierce the Alps at Otira, and cut the magnificent
Otira Gorge scenery out of the traveller’s
itinerary, and make a line across the South Island
from west to east. The stupendous hole is to be
nearly six miles long, and is to be’built in five
years at a cost of £600,000. This will take a high
place among the horizontal orifices of the earth.
England’s biggest tunnel—that Under the Severn
—is less than miles long, and took 13 years to
build.
The amorous adventure of Policeman
Holden in Fitzroy Gardens (Vic.) recalls the sad
case of Smith, of Adelaide, who was sitting with
his girl by the Torrens Lake, when he was
annoyed by Blokes. He explained quite clearly
what he would do to them if they did not leave.
Then two things happened simultaneously. (1)
They left. (2) Policeman X, becoming aware of
angry voices, strolled up to see what was the
matter. And Smith, hearing feet behind him,
and concluding that one Bloke had come back,
just remarked : “Well, if you will have it”
and, whirling round, he did to the astonished
copper as many of the things promised as he had
time for before learning the horrid truth. The
Police Magistrate in due course sympathised
with Smith, but fined him £1 to teach him to be
more wary in distinguishing between policemen
and Blokes.

“ Mate ” tells some truths :
“ Bobstay ” (B. 23/4/’08) enthuses some re the Union
Oo.’s training ship for officers. This idea is a good one as
far as it goes. Britain is a broken reed to lean on for
officers to man our ships, as for some other things.
It needs all, and more than all, the fast declining
sailing ships now produce. It’s not lack of means
for training that is the cause of the shortage. It’s
the disadvantages and lack of decent prospects that keep
boys who look ahead from going to sea. There are stiff
exams, to look forward to, and exemptions for all Aus-
tralian ports to get later on. The pay is not in pro-
portion to the position and responsibility. The work is
hard, and means long hours, in spite of Justice O’Connor’s
alleged reduction. Take a mate’s job in any of our ships.
He has to keep the ship clean and in order, inside and
out; indent for and supervise the use of all deck stores,
such as paint, oil, rope, wire, and many oddments ; see that
all cargo gear is in order, and renew it when it shows
signs of wear (if anything carries away he is held respon-
sible) ; keep note of the sailors’ overtime (doesn’t get
any himself); engage and discharge the hands—
plan their work, and see it properly carried out.
He’s always in request by stevedores, colliers, and
101 other people when in port. He must be on the bridge
coming in and going out of harbor, and all in addition to
his regular watches at sea. In some ships you never get
a Sunday or holiday at home—nothing but the 10 days a
year at the owner’s convenience. When you compare
this with the case of the average shore-going person who
knocks off at noon on Saturday every week, has all Sun-
days, and a dozen or more other holidays every year (as
well as the usual two or three weeks, in many cases), you
can’t wonder boys are shy of sailoring. There is a
remedy, but its chance of adoption is small as yet.
A CLERIC SEES THINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Speaking at the Presbyterian General Assembly last
night, Rev. John Paterson, who has just returned from
the Barrier, painted a vivid picture of Broken Hill—the
corrugated roofs, scorching sun, and consequent thirst of
the population.
It was the third city of New South Wales, he said a
place with a population of 50,000, largely men represent-
ing the young manhood of the world. The brainiest men
from all Europe were there ; but there, also, were forces of
immorality too awful for him to speak of in that audience.
In Broken Hill the forces opposed to Christianity were
organised as, perhaps, they were in no other city in the
Commonwealth. They had an aggressive and almost
triumphant Socialism, that made a scorn of the things
they held sacred.—S. M. Herald, 8/5/’OB.
Woe ! Woe ! And moral Horrors manifold !
Cling to your pews, my brethren, and prepare
For dialectic squalls. Hear me unfold
A tale to chill the spine and raise the hair.
For I have viewed the Place of Awful Thirst—
’Neath its scorched roofs men nothing eat, save beer :
All hands on an interminable burst
Go day and night, and howl and shriek and cheer.
The brainiest thinkers flock here from afar
To heap their intellects upon a pyre
Of alcohol. Success is not the Star
These aim at. All they seek’s Eternal Fire.
The Sabbath dawns ; the kirk bell tinkles loud.
