The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Mr. Ben H. Morgan, commercial editor of
London Standard, is wandering through Australia as
special commissioner for the British Manufacturers’ As-
sociation. This is another consequence of the disastrous
tariff. Before that arrived the British manufacturer
would have sent out a circular intimating that he would
have a new line of tweeds ready in three months, or a
new pattern of bedsteads, and would Australia please
send its orders by return of mail. Now the British
manufacturers look hard at the tariff wall, and then send
Mr. Morgan out to see what the prospects are in the way
of establishing industries inside the obstruction, and thus
getting the work done in Australia that hitherto has
been done in foreign parts. That is the first object of the •
commissioner’s visit.
The second object is to stir up Australia to
appreciate the antics of the English shipping ring. On
that subject members of the Labor party have talked to
Australia frequently during the past couple of years ; also
The Bulletin has mentioned the matter several times.
Australia, however, was frightfully busy discussing
whether Trumper really shouldn’t be dropped out of the
Australian Eleven, so the other matter received very
little attention. Now, however, that the representa-
tive of British manufacturers talks, it is quite
another matter. The situation simply is that
the big English shipping cos., passenger and freight
lines alike, have formed themselves into a close asso-
ciation, not only to regulate rates, but to prevent the
“ intrusion ”of any outsider. The regulation of rates is,
to a certain extent, right enough ; it isn’t good for busi-
ness to have freights cut to 255. a ton this week, and
then, to make up for it, sprung up to £3 15s. next week.
That sort of thing turns commerce into a gamble ; the
merchant who happens to get his stuff shipped during the
255.-a-ton week is dealt the joker and right bower, while
the £3 155.-a-ton man gets all the sevens in the pack.
There is no serious objection to regulation of rates within
certain limits ; there is very serious objection to the
choking-off of the outsider, and the elimination of all
competition.
This part of the business is “ worked ” through
deferred rebates. The shipping co. charges, say, 60s. a
ton. On top of that it charges 10 per cent, good conduct
money. The good conduct consists of the shipper abso-
lutely boycotting every shipping line that is not in the
ring. The good conduct money, or deferred rebate, is
held nominally for six months, but practically for as
much as twelve ; and if the shipper has behaved himself,
and given no encouragement to anybody outside the ring,
then he gets a refund of what by that time probably
amounts to some thousands of pounds. Thus the shipper
keeps in existence a ring whose business in life is, of
course, to maintain high freights. The United States had
precisely the same trouble ; but they got over it by a
simple provision in what is known as the Elkins Act,
prohibiting the carriage of any goods to the country
under any system of deferred rebates. One result is that
the American trade is w 7 ell catered for. Any old tub
isn’t good enough when the shipper has the right to go
wherever he likes to have his goods carried. The fear of
outside competition keeps the people in the trade up to
the collar all the time. B. H. Morgan has had conferences
with Prime Minister Deakin, Labor Leader Fisher, J. C.
Watson, and others ; and it seems likely that the Elkins
Act will be repeated in Australia.
To see the effect of competition—and also
the effect of the absence of it—compare the freights
for British and German or American goods on the vessels
within the English ring. There are three American lines
reaching out for the Australian trade ; consequently, in
order to compete with those lines, British ships will carry
American goods from Liverpool for as much as 30 per
cent, less than they will carry British goods. That is
where the shoe pinches the British Manufacturers’
Association. That body probably doesn’t trouble much
about Australia ; what it does trouble about is that, in
consequence of the ring, which is fed and kept alive by
the rebates, its members are placed at a disadvantage
compared with the foreigner. Australia is, of course,
most interested in having its own industries. It doesn’t
want to give work to either British or foreign factories.
But if it must draw from elsewhere, it is best that it
should draw from Britain ; wherefore it is now fixing up
a preferential tariff. And obviously this ring, or con-
spiracy, or whatever you like to call it on the part of a
few shipping magnates goes a long way towards neutral-
ising that preference; so from that point of view Aus-
tralia is interested in stamping on the thing.
* * * *
The Premiers’ Conference in Melbourne tackled
the question of the relations between the Governments
and the Stock Exchanges. Lately, when South Australia
tried to float a loan in the Commonwealth, the Stock
Exchanges virtually dictated terms, first, as to what
rates of brokerage they would charge, and second, as to
who would be permitted to quote or sell the stock.
Failing acceptance of their terms, the brokers agreed to
boycott the loan. The Price Government had to capitu-
late. When S.A. Treasurer Peake asked the Premiers’
Conference for assistance, Wade, Waddell, and Ashton,
the N.S.W. delegates, all threw cold water on him. They
remarked that, supposing the States passed laws, insist-
ing on Stock Exchanges dealing with Government
securities at a fixed minimum rate of pay, the Exchanges
would nominally bow to the law, but each individual
broker would say he wouldn’t quote the stock ; and if
this individual action became general the Governments
would be in a more parlous state than ever. Wade,
Waddell, and Ashton are jointly authors of the Indus-
trial Disputes Act, under which any trades union may be
forced to put up a money bond that its members will obey
any award fixed by the Industrial Court; but it evidently
didn’t occur to them that they could extend the principle,
and if any member of any Stock Exchange refused to deal
in any particular stock in which a boycott was sus-
pected, the Exchange to which he belonged could be cast
in damages.

This is a bit complicated ; and even then it doesn’t ex-
plain the whole position. First, as to the amounts shown
as written off. In 1904 the actual loss was £3688 ; but the
City Fund had to make good the annual contribution to
the sinking fund, and it did so. Nevertheless the debt
was not shown as a liability. Since then, however, the
advance has been paid off, the last instalment of £l4OO
being provided during the past year ; and for the sake of
convenience these amounts are represented as written
off. Then as to the deficit. It began with loan flotation
and preliminary expenses and “ damages.” With regard
to these paper “assets” a very gradual policy is being
adopted : they are being wiped out by annual payments
spread over 25 years 1 In the foregoing table, of course,
they are not regarded as assets at all. Then £31,000
appears among the assets as “ Goodwill, Empire Co.”
This, being paper, is also not regarded as an asset. The
Empire Co. was one of several which competed with the
municipal business. There were two courses open : one to
go ahead and possibly eventually run these older private
enterprise concerns out of business ;' the other, to buy
them out at a fair price and be done with it. The latter
policy, which had several things in its favor, had been
successfully adopted by the older municipal undertaking
in Melbourne, and was also adopted in Sydney. It in-
volved, however, paying something substantial for the
goodwill of the business. In fact, there was nothing but
the goodwill that was worth anything to the munici-
pality. One other business (the Strand Co.’s) has since
been bought: a third (the Oxford Co.’s) is now the sub-
ject of negotiation.
The following will perhaps make the present
position plainer :

This presents some more details of the working
of Sydney’s electric light and power business :
Working Net

The figures in the last column don’t correspond exactly
with those of the front column in the first table. The dif-
ference represents the interest received on the sinking
fund ; and that interest doesn’t, of course, go into the
working account, this being an honest sinking fund, and
not like the one Oarruthers established for the State in
N. S. Wales. The taking over of the Empire Co.’s busi-
ness has disturbed some of the figures. The whole basis
of the transaction was unusual. The City Council took
over the Empire Co.’s customers, and charge them
the usual rates. Of that revenue the Council
retains a penny per unit (which is about the cost of
generation) and hands over the balance to the co. in
payment for the undertaking. Thus the Council had to
float no new loan to acquire the business—the business
provides the money to pay for itself, and while it is pro-
viding that money the Council is nothing out of pocket,
except for 5 per cent, interest on the unpaid balance of
purchase money, now £22,670. There isn't any apparent
reason, though, why the Council should go on paying
5 per cent, on that amount ; it would seem to be good
business to wipe the debt out promptly, in which case, of
course, the Council wouldn’t return any of the revenue
to the co. Apparently the position with regard to the
co. last year was as follows : The municipality got £1938 for
supplying the co.’scustomers, at a cost to itself, it seems, of
£1359. A further £6126 was collected, and was available
for paying off the capital debt. As against that, £1796
was paid as interest on the unpaid balance of the debt.
The City has borrowed £350,000 on debentures,
and, besides the £22,670 owing to the Empire Co., it owed
the bank £23,005 and sundry creditors £10,370. The
annual contribution to the sinking fund is 1 per cent. ; so
the City Council’s honesty is twice as rapid as Deakin
and Lyne have horrified some State politicians by propos-
ing that the State’s honesty should be. The depreciation
allowance (2 per cent, on £250,463) isn’t so satisfactory ;
2 per cent, isn’t half enough so far as a very great many
of these appliances are concerned. The Melbourne City
Council, which started with cent., found that it
wasn’t enough, and almost immediately made it 2 per
cent. But as it got more experience it saw the need for
further liberality ; so in 1906 the extra provision ran to
5% per cent., and last year it ran to 6 per cent., or a total
of 8 per cent, for the year. The Sydney undertaking will
almost certainly have to go the same way. In fact, until
it goes that way, any statement as to its net profits must
be viewed with a good deal of doubt.

This co., one of the very few which remain unregis-
tered under the limited-liability provisions of the Com-
panies Act, has nominally a capital of £lOO,OOO ; and
it is steadily building up the paid capital to that height
by transfers of undivided profit. It began in 1872 with an
actually paid capital of £6250 ; so if the present dividends
were based upon that, they would amount to something
dazzling—a trifle of 165 per cent, or so per annum. The
present dividend is actually, however, not referred to as
even 14 per cent. The plan adopted is to pay a fixed
8 per cent., and thereafter to divide 20 percent, of the
premiums among policy-holders and shareholders in
equal parts ; consequently the policy-holders’ 10 per cent,
bonus is shown in this case, whereas in connection with
some other offices, which pay the same bonus, the amount
is simply deducted from premiums received. Recently 7 the
transfer of reserves to capital has been rapid; consequently
the increase of capital has completely outpaced the in-
crease of business (and, therefore, bonuses); hence the
reduction of the total dividend from 24 per cent, to about
14 per cent, inside 10 years. (This dividend, of course,
includes the shareholders’ half of the bonus.) It was said
just now that the increase of capital had outpaced the
increase of business. This is largely the result of the co.’s
policy in confining business to N. S. Wales, and strictly
selecting its risks. The refusal to take risks outside
N. S. Wales was largely the result of the non-limiting of
liability : practically all the shares of the partnership being
held in Sydney, it was held that business should not be
pursued, as it were, out of their sight. But the time is
passed for such restrictions, and the directors now propose
that the co. shall be entitled to do business anywhere in
the Commonwealth and Maoriland, and not only fire
business, but every 7 other insurance or guarantee business
“known or hereafter to become known,” except life-
assurance. But no announcement has yet been made
with regard to an alteration of the basis of the co. en-
titling it to write “ Limited ” after its title—an alteration
which the two big Maoriland cos. lately carried through
without in the least affecting their credit or their popu-
larity with insurers, so far as can be judged by results.

The effect of the restrictions is seen in the comparatively
small increase in premium receipts: the effects of the
strict selection of risks are seen both in the low loss rate
and the high expense rate. Certainly the Australian
Mutual can never forget its constitution ; it is bound to
a policy of great caution. All the same, if the premiums
had been increased to £60,000 at a cost of 33 per cent.,
and with losses of as much as 45 percent., the underwriting
profit would have amounted to £13,200, as against £12,934
actually realised for the past year. The investments are
not specified fully ; they are described as “city freehold,
mortgages, fixed deposits, and money at call,” and they
show a return all round of just under 4 per cent. ; but
whether that can be considered good or only fair depends
upon the proportion of call money, fixed deposits, etc.
* * * ♦
The Royal Bank of Queensland lately decided
to alter its balancing months from June and December
to March and September ; and, while the change doesn’t
affect the public, the abolition of the extra grind at mid-
summer is something that the staff will be grateful for.
Under the new arrangement, the books were made up as
at March 31, and accounts have now been issued. For the
quarter a net profit of £6304 is shown, as against £12,626
for the previous six months; so the recent fine improve-
ment is just about maintained. Out of this, a dividend
at the rate of 4£ per cent, per annum is paid, absorbing
£5708, and £596 is carried forward, bringing the re-
serves up to £71,206. The amount left over after pay-
ing the dividend is small, as usual; but from
the steady increase in the earnings of the bank’s assets,
it looks as though the latter have, been steadily written
down out of undisclosed profit. From the fact that for
many years past the shares have been and still are at a
discount, investors have obviously assumed that there
was a good deal of writing down to do, and that, in fact,
there were no real reserves at all. Rather than add largely
to reserves which pretty evidently don’t exist in fact, it is
understood that the policy of this carefully handled bank
has been to quietly write down the book value of assets in
the hope that presently book values and market values
will correspond ; and when that happens there
will, of course, be no deficit so far as capital is
concerned. And that some people reckon that the bank
hasn’t much, if any, further to go in that way is proved
by the further fact that it has latterly been getting in
some new capital at par. The total paid capital is now
£485,003; the total liabilities are £1,157,611. Even
making a liberal allowance for over-valuation of assets,
capital and reserves are thus large in proportion to
deposits and other liabilities, so the latter are very well
protected.

Besides the reserves shown in this table, the co. has a
“ Repairs, suspense, and contingency account,” which was
in credit £1557 at the end of the half-year, a reduction of
£llOO during the term. This money and more was put
into the steamer Bega ; and just after the half-year closed
that unsteady vessel took a list first to port and then to
starboard, and finally capsized and sank. The co.’s luck
has been right out lately. First, the South Coast felt a
drought worse than it had ever experienced before ; then,
tired of the exactions of South Coast landlords, there was
a steady exodus of population to the North Coast and
other districts ; thirdly, the co. was importing a new
steamer, the Moruya, and had got it out as far as the Vic-
torian coast, when it ran aground, and was badly
damaged; (4) the co.’s trade during this past half-
year was disorganised by a strike; (5) the Bega
sank as before mentioned; and, finally, it was
announced the other day that an inter-State co.
was going to call in at the South Coast, mainly
in order to promote a direct trade between there
and Melbourne. The co. had done very little in the way
of strengthening its fleet of late years ; but with the Eden
laid up and the Bega laid down, it has had to give an
order for a new vessel (in Great Britain, by the way—the
Moruya’s troubles on the way out didn’t teach it to go to
an Australian yard), and it has also bought the Kempsey
from the North Coast trade. At March 31 the position
compared with that at corresponding dates in this way :

Fortunately the cash position is very strong, so the pay-

loss of the Bega may not make a very big hole in the re-
serves. The profits, it will be noticed, are shown net;
something unknown is written off for depreciation. The
book value of ship property has been written down from
£42,471 to £39,366 in two years, notwithstanding that the
Moruya has been added to the fleet in that time, though
possibly some payments may have been made previously.
It appears, therefore, as though the profits are by no
means over-stated.

As a whole, Maoriland trustee cos. are not very satis-
factory, mainly because they mix the trustee industry
with other business. One (or perhaps more) of them even
goes in for sharebroking. The extent of this co.’s sinning is
not clear ; in setting out a “ statement of assets in the
hands of the co. as trustee, executor and agent” it
observes that the amount is “ exclusive of value of share
scrip ” ; and, though that sets one wondering, it doesn’t
necessarily mean that the co. is a sharebroker as well as
a trustee. In Maoriland, however, it is advisable for a
real trustee co. to make its position clear, because in no
other Australasian State do trustee cos. mix things as
they do over there. The trouble is that, when
other business is taken in hand, the capital
and reserves can no longer be , egarded as a
fund wholly available to guarantee the right performance
of trust duties. The position is worst where deposits are
taken and other liabilities assumed in order to carry on
an advance business. This co., fortunately, seems to keep
clear of that. Certainly there are “ balances due by the
co., £13,485,” but that is rather more than covered by
cash and “ balances due to the co.” Apparently these
balances are in connection with the agency business.
The trust assets in hand total £1,490,368; in 1906,
when the corresponding statement was first published,
the total was £1,290,047. The gross revenue from com-
mission was £3804, or ss. Id. per cent, of the value nf
trust estates in hand ; so the charges appear to h
moderate. And yet the profits ran to £9 Os. 10d r>
cent, on combined capital and reserves—anotherinstane r
of the remarkably profitable character of trustee busf
ness, which requires so little paid capital to conduct
that one quite understands why for so long a period it
was regarded as a choice plum by the Gent One who nur
sues six-and-eightpence and as much more as he thmtl
he can catch. ns ®

This trustee co. improves the form of its accounts

years past it has shown among its liabilities a large' item
—“ Amounts owing by the association.” Six months avo
the total was £11,606, or £1606 more than the co.’s
capital. Now, when trustee cos. owe large amounts on
overdraft or on deposits, and lend out the money, obvi-
ously their capital (or more) maybe required to’niake
good a slump in the value of the securities into which the
borrowed money has been put; and that isn’t the posi-
tion any trustee co. ought to be liable to fall into. It
looked as though this co. might be doing that sort of
business ; but the more complete accounts show that
the position isn’t as bad as it might be by a
long way. The “amounts owing” are now
shown as “clients’ credit balances” (£20,820);
and on the other side the whole of ’ that
amount and a trifle more is represented by cash
£18,545 ; and clients’ debit balances, £3796. The interest
on investments (now stated separately) amounted for the
half-year to £367, showing a return of £4 12s. per cent.—
very reasonable, seeing that nearly one-third of the in-
vestment is on fixed deposit in the name of the State
Treasurer. The business (like most trustee businesses) is
very profitable, looking at it from the point of view of
capital invested ; capital and reserves show a profit equal
to £lO 3s. Bd. per cent, per annum.

No regular bonus has been paid to suppliers during the

(butter sales have amounted to £22,879) the profit is cut
very fine ; even disregarding the distribution of the

but £4 of the profits ; and that isn’t satisfactory in a
business which must inevitably make some bad debts.
The grade of this co.’s product doesn’t appear to have
been as high as it ought to have been, and the Board,
reckoning that the quality of the cream supply has been
to blame, proceeds to make some severe remarks to the
local Cow man on the subject.
* * * *
S. M. Herald has had an article on “The
Darkest Cloud above the Horizon of our Prosperity.”
Said darkest cloud is alleged to be “ undoubtedly the
depression in the mining industry,” due to the low price
of silver and lead ; and, as usual, the Government is
urged to “bog in” and play up some more borrowed
money—of course, on the same old “ reproductive public
works.” Most of this is plain rubbish. Unless you judge
by the standard of the phenomenal boom of 1906-7, there
is no mining depression —especially in N. S. Wales—and
the metal markets are not extraordinarily low. Lead now
is £l3 per ton. In the 20 years preceding the recent
boom (that is to say, between 1885 and 1904)
lead averaged less than £l3 in no fewer than 12 years.
Copper is worth about £5B. In 14 years out of the2oprevious
to the recent boom its average was lower than that. In
10 years out of the 20 the yearly average was, in fact,
below £5O. Tin is worth £142 or more. Before the recent
boom it never once in the previous 20 years averaged so
much as that for 12 months. In 1894 it averaged £6B
(omitting shillings and pence); in 1895, £63 ; 1896, £59;
1897, £6l. The alleged extraordinary low prices of metals
are therefore a myth. They don’t exist except in the
minds of people who can’t remember further back than
the remarkable boom of the past three years.
Whether price levels have moved up on account
of the extraordinary increase in the output of gold—the
metal by which we measure other things—is another
matter. In the last 20 years gold production has trebled.
Going back no further than 1901 the output was worth
£52,000,000 in round figures ; in 1906 it had gone up to
£83,000,000. To what extent this affects prices is dis-
puted, and can’t be definitely settled; but it is pretty
certain that it must affect them more or less. Theretor
what would be a normal price for silver, lead, co PP er ’
etc., 10 or 20 years ago would be below normal now. ine
market boomster stops at that; the man who tries to
honest can’t afford to, for he has to admit that it
cheapening of gold sends up the prices of lead and copp
it must also send up the prices of timber, niacmner},
wages, etc. In short, the same movement that sends p
the price of the product sends costs up also.

A copper smelting furnace, designed to embody
the recommendations of the best Australian and American
experts, has just been constructed for the Great Fitzroy
Gold and Copper Mines Ltd., Queensland. The furnace is
of large capacity, being capable of turning out 100 tons of
matte per day. It is rectangular in shape, and is higher
in column in proportion to its hearth area than is
customary. By this means both the roasting and
oxidising operations will be conducted in the same furnace,
the oxidising being done in the upper portion and the
smelting in the lower. The furnace has two tiers of mild
steel water jackets. The lower tier rests on a c.i. plate,
supported on c.i. standards, or, should hearth plate be
removed, the tier will be swung on girders ; the upper
tier of jackets is always swung from girders supported on
columns. The columns are held together by a strong c.i.
frame. The air blast passes from a pipe encompassing
the furnace through Hustle pipes to the tuyeres. The
Austral Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne,
have designed and built this furnace.* 11

LUBECKER MACHINE COMPANY. Excavators and Dredging: Machinery.
Importers of Tramway, Mining, Industrial and Electrical Machinery and Materials of all kinds. [3
on its record it ought never to have gone beyond that.
Even now there is insidious booming ; the public is cun-
ningly told, for instance, that for the past half-year the
working account showed a profit of £9619. What is the
use of humbugging people like that? The things that
have to be paid out of that—insurance, lease rent, office
expenses, etc.—are just as imperative as the other costs.
At March 31 the position was that the co. owed £7285,
and, apart from stores in hand, it had £5946, represented
by bank balance and cash (£492) and concentrates. It is
a wretched position from which to face a slump after a
metal boom. The co. cannot make a call because all its
shares are fully paid ; but it will be very lucky if it
escapes reconstruction.
Six months ago it was calculated on the basis of
the previous half-year’s work that, without charging any-
thing for depreciation or wear and tear, the co. could
make ends meet with lead at £l3 IBs. 7d. per ton, pro-
vided silver kept its price. For the past half-year 6441
tons of concentrates were produced, assaying 61'5 per
cent. lead. That means 3961 tons. The gross profit, as
shown above, was £7376, equal to £1 17s. 4d. per ton of
lead. The average obtained for lead during the half-year
was £l5 10s. 9d. ; consequently it cost all the silver and
£l3 13s. sd. per ton of lead to produce the metal. Silver
has fallen a trifle further, so that, unless the cost of pro-
duction has been further reduced, the expenses must be
now over £l3 13s. sd. per ton of lead. As to costs, things
may have improved a little ; but they have not improved
enough to show any reasonable amount of profit with lead
standing now at £l3. Indeed, seeing that in the foregoing
the profits are calculated gross , it is pretty eviden t that the
co. must now be really working at a considerable loss
when depreciation is allowed for.
* ■* * *
The modern ore-dressing mill runs away.with a
pot of money. The zinc-flotation plant, which the Sul-
phide Corporation put up for the Minerals Separation
Co. at Broken Hill, was to have cost £lB,OOO ; but when
the bill was made up the total was about £26,000. Still,
the Minerals Separation Co., though it stared hard at
first, feels better now ; for the plant, designed to treat
2010 tons a week, actually puts through 3000 tons.
* * * *
Broken Hill Block 10 got into very bad trouble
during its last half-year. It made a profit of £24,694 ;
but from this it had to write off £12,338 on account of
previous over-valuation of product. (Final adjustments
are made three months after delivery of concentrates.)
Six months ago, when the metal markets looked a bit
rackety, £9034 was put aside as a special reserve to meet
possible reclamations. All that was swamped, and the
£12,338 besides. Then £10,145 plant depreciation has
also to come off to get the net profit. This co. has writ-
ten off very large amounts, but that has been and still is
necessary on account of so much plant having been at
various times thrown out of use through surface movements.
The co. should have received £lO,OOO from the Zinc Cor-
poration during the term, but actually received nothing ;
it did, however, take credit for current production of
"tailings” at the rate of Is. per ton, and the consequent
£2764 is included in the profits, though this stuff has not
been realised. Still, though the prospects of zinc-treat-
ment were absurdly exaggerated a while ago, Block 10 is
no doubt justified in taking credit for its output of zinc-
bearing tailings. Certainly, if Block 10’s tailings are
worth nothing, it is a cold look-out for the rest.
• * » *
Broken Hill miners are growling. The
trouble is that the mines have cut contract prices
till some miners can’t earn the standard 12s. a
shift. But the thing is not so bad as it has been
described. On account of the changeable nature of
the ground, it is impossible for the most experienced
contract setter to fix the prices for ore-breaking, driving
and sinking so that each man will earn an even 12s. per
shift. A price that would mean good money for one
party would mean starvation wages for another in the
same ground. Here is an instance, audited and found
correct: A party took a contract, and failed to make
wages, so the management increased the contract price.
Still the men couldn’t make a living, and pulled out.
Another party went in at the same contract price, and
made 30s. a shift.

J. Bull again did very well last year out of his
lucky purchase of the old Gallymont, Cobar district
(N.S.W.). As the Mt. Boppy it is easily first among
N.S.W. gold mines. Out of 117,150 shares, Bull paid cash
(£1 a-piece) for 47,150, and the vendors got the balance.
That was nine years ago, and since then over £250,000 has
been paid in dividends. Last year, £57,475 was paid ; that
is to say, £36,300 (6s. per share) was paid during the year,
and £21,175 (3s. 6d. per share) was payable after the
annual meeting held recently. From the time when the
co. started, the mine has put up this record :

* * * *
Something over 12 months ago, the Mount
Boppy (N.S.W.) promoted the South Boppy to work what
used to be the Boppy’s south block ; the parent took
55,000 shares out of 150,000 for the ground. Manager
Pascoe, of Boppy, has clung tight to a high opinion of
the property, and he had the satisfaction of telling the
shareholders in London lately that he looked like coming
out on top. One shaft, which is being sunk to get the
Boppy lode, was, at time of reporting, in highly favorable
country ; and in a crosscut from the other they had
struck the Canbelego lode, 49ft. wide, with values up to
9dwt. This was at 200 ft. deep.
* * * *
The Cobar Gold Mines (late Fort Bourke)
erected three Elmore units to treat the siliceous, copper-
carrying ore from the unoxidised levels. So far these
units have been a failure. When will cos. learn that it
pays to put up a trial unit first ? The co. is now experi-
menting with concentrating tables. The mine looks well
in depth. Ore averages 80 per cent, silica and 1 per cent,
copper; and the gold in the deep-lying ore is said to be
about lOdwt.
* » * •
Occidental, Cobar, which on a capital of
£4OOO has paid £44,000 divs., besides erecting plant, is
doing g°od work with an awful mill on oxidised ore.
bnoxidised ore carries copper, and will require new treat-
ment. The Moore process, in the hands of metallurgist
appel, works splendidly. Sands cost 3s. 6d. and slimes
(including sixpence royalty) 3s. 9d. per ton. The recovery
is about 7s. 6d. in battery and 9s. in the sands and slimes.
4he residues contain only about llgr.

t se k now Baker’s Creek (N.S.W.) never
Pct regular results or constant conditions. The vein
is small, the containing' slate rock subject to explosive
bursts, and the shoots of gold-bearing stone inconsistent.
'? u |r ectors are therefore naturally shy of declaring
dividends, although at the close of the half-year (March
al) they had £.8260 available. Perhaps the good stone
put througn since then may moderate their somewhat
tight-fisted policy. The directors explain that No. 18
level is disappointing, so evidently the last crushing of
z4u tons for 410 oz. did not come from there ; and if No. 17
has to provide all the crushing stuff, it cannot be ex-
pected to last very long. Development cost £2317 for the
six months ; it has been fairly rapid, and the machine
drills did good work in the long crosscut to reach the
reef. There is this much to be said : No. 18 level, which
has so far proved such a duffer, is not right through to
the boundary, near which No. 17 was richest. The winze
below No. 17 has proved payable ore in places, but great
care has to be taken to prevent injury from bursting rock.
The show is only repeating its past experiences, and a
fresh discovery may perhaps put the market in better
heart. An improvement seems likely near the cross-
course and the southern boundary.
* * • *
The closing down of the Eleanora section of the
New Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.) came as a shock to ardent
admirers of the hydro-electric power scheme and the
numerous new ideas introduced at the battery. It was
a mystery to old miners why large trial lots could not
have been crushed before going to such great expense.
Past experience certainly did not indicate anything very
wonderful, the average being about 15dwt. The develop-
ment of No. 2 reef, parallel to Smith’s rich little streak of
stone, brought in a few “ buy at best” orders, though all
that can be said is that for a length of 100 ft. prospects
were good. If the gold makes again at the end of the
level things may brighten up. No. 13 on Smith’s vein
should also help to keep the pot boiling. Shares are being
snapped up, but this seems to indicate merely an over-
sold market.
* * * *
New Wylie Creek tin dredge, away up on the
Queensland border, isn’t repeating' its success of 1905-6,
when it paid a pretty regular 3d. per share every other
month. For the past half-year it was only £196 to the
good, and then nothing was provided for out of profits to
cover the rapid depreciation of this sort of plant ; where-
fore the real profit fell a good deal short of £196. The
co. formerly had a substantial contingency account in
liquid assets ; but the bulk of this has now been put into
a new sluicing plant. It is not certain that this will
pay' ; but much of the country ahead was looking very
unsuitable for dredging, and the sluicing plant seemed
the co.’s only hope.
* * * *
As anticipated a few weeks ago, J. Bull’s
Lloyd’s copper mine, Burraga (N.S.W.), has closed down.
The co. had horrible luck. It had got rid of the deben-
ture debt, and was going ahead nicely when slides cut
the lode off, and while manager Corbould was poking
around to pick it up again the co. missed the best of the
copper boom. All the time the ravenous furnaces were
going, the timber fuel was getting more and more remote,
and the smash in the copper market came just too soon
to allow of the co. building' a tramway out to timber
country, for which work it had been saving up money.
* * * *
The easing-off of base-metal mining in N. S.
Wales is followed, as usual, by a shrinkage in the gold
output as well. To the end of April the 1908 yield has
been 71,3010 z. fine, as against 85,8850 z. for the first four
months of 1907.
* * * *
Victorian mining has the blues badly, the
yields of every centre in the State showing a reduction.
For April the total output was 35,8380 z., and for worse
than that it is necessary to go back years. Bendigo has
failed in its deep levels, Ballarat is moulting badly, and
deep-lead mining outside of Rutherglen is a sink for calls.
Not a new mine shows on the horizon, and the Daylesford
frost is so hard that one wants an axe to break it. There
never was a time when shareholders were so gravelled
without any prospect of early recovery.
* * * *
Duke United. Maryborough (Vic.), was a catch-
penny to set the Junction and other deep-lead shares of
the Stoneham group jigging. But the penny did not
multiply, because the gutter went poor. Now, nothing
is being done save to take out some odd patches of wash.
Stanley Hunter, Government Geologist, wants the co. to
go 1000 ft. down the lead to reach the White Cross and
Sailor’s lines of reef. But J. Bull is a very tired man. He
sees that the debit at profit and loss is over £17,000 ; he
knows that disappointment has followed disappointment,
and that £7OOO is wanted to carry out the scheme, while
there is only a chance of getting divs. in return. So he
withdraws —the only wise thing he has done in connec-
tion with such a patchv lead.

Here it is again ! Mt. Bowen (Vic.), near the
Monaro country, has “ a lode 50ft. wide worth 10 per
cent, copper.” The hundred-eyed Argos says prepara-
tions are being made “ to sink a shaft to test the lode
underfoot.” Also, “leases have been pegged out north
and south.” It all recalls Mt. Deddick and the sad
memories of the saddest of boom days.
* * m *
Great Southern. Rutherglen (Vic.), is a mine
with a future, if—and it isn’t a big if—gold contents of
the wash don’t shrink much. The gutter pans out now
13Jdwt. dirt. This costs a shade over lOdwt. to get out,
leaving 2£dwt. profit. The co. has only 36,000 shares, so
that such yields mean steady 6d. divs. If the gutter
could be squeezed in from 1000 ft. to 700 ft., profits would
be better ; but Nature gave the prehistoric man big
rivers to paddle over before she sealed them up for the
moderns to dig out. The lead north is trending west,
and if it cuts far across-country the “if ” cited above
may have to be reckoned with. Going south the bottom
is uneven and hard in No. 13 block, but it is better ahead.

“D.C.E.”: According to Dodd, chairman of
the Oathcart, 8., M. and Co. made a terrible mess of
things in trying to manage the mine (B. 7/5/’08). This
has apparently only now dawned on Dodd, notwith-
standing that he was on the spot and chairman of
directors all the time. When 8., M. and Co. took an
interest in the Oathcart, and were given the management
for all time, the show was about to close down. This was
why the Ararat crowd was so anxious to get Bull’s help.
But J. Bull got tired after keeping the thing going for
eighteen months, and now Dodd has nothing but fault to
find with the way Bull helped him. For once Victorians
had let an apparently good thing go ; but, now seeing
their mistake, they mean to have it back again, and the
27,000 shares should help them. As there are to be no
more calls, the local people should be truly thankful for
Bull forfeiting his shares. If the past management has
been so bad, why did not Dodd demand an explanation
of it?

It is significant of the improvement in the look
of things at Charters Towers that a once-familiar monthly
report, issued by a Towers firm of brokers, has resumed
publication after a spell of 12 or 18 months.
* * •» *
Mills’ United, Charters Towers, is out of favor
because both the quality and quantity of the ore in the
mine has declined. The directors say “ the output will
be maintained and dividends paid”; they can talk thus
confidently because they have a big cash balance. Last
half-year 24,477 tons produced a profit of £52,954. The
stone averaged £4 4s. 6d., which is a good deal better
than late crushings. A lot of money has been spent on
development and equipping the shaft on the School
reserve, but so far nothing payable has been found, so
naturally enough the reserves are smaller. There is a
large unexplored area both above and below No. 13,
which is the deepest level and has a long way to
go to the boundary. Talking of boundaries, both
the New Brilliant Freehold and East Mexican may
freshen up Mills' United chances, if developments are as
good as the market seems to consider them, which is
very doubtful. There has been a run on shares in
boundary mines, and the parent mine has been left in
the cold. It is hoped the hanging-wall reef in Mills’
United will turn out better than prospects in drives and
rises seemed to indicate, but this is an unstable kind of
hope. The New Brilliant Freehold has also to drive into
the hanging wall, and a cut in that direction may have
given the shares their jump. Yet a sudden improvement
in quality of the stone in such a locality should not carry
speculators off their feet.
Mount Morgan (Q.) wants to add coal-mining
to its industries. It holds 5J sq. miles of country in the
Dawson and Mackenzie field, where two seams have been
proved, three to four miles from the Central railway. One
is as much as 4£ft. and the other 7ft. thick. Prospect-
ing is now being carried out to see if the seams cannot be
cut closer to the steel rails. The Mount consumes 40,000
tons of coal a ye&r, and 36,000 tons of firewood, so this
new work is of importance. The coalfields thereabouts
have long been played with. They will now get a fair
Inal. Hitherto the big quantities of water struck in the
shafts have been the crushing handicap.
I\orth Queensland tin shows are uncertain cattle.
There was Dalziel’s, with a wonderfully fine surface show,
now worth sixpence a share. The remarkable thing in
that case was that for a long while the chairman of
directors, who insisted that the bottom was out of the
mine, wasn’t believed and had clods thrown at him.
Now, within a week of the news that Gilmore’s was show-
ing a great improvement, comes the further news that it
was quite a flash in the pan, and things look distinctly
less favorable than they looked before. Southerners let
northerners have even their Vulcan to themselves for
years ; and|t was the only safe policy.
From a metallurgist who wandered that way :
ite Bulletin’s remarks (30/4/’08) concerning the failure of
Aldershot and the need for a smelter in Queensland.
What sort of a chance has a lead smelter in Queensland,
anyhow ? The history of customs smelting in N. S. Wales
and Queensland so far has not proved the urgent
need of one. The imported metallurgists who formerly
ran these shows do not seem to shake off the
effects of their earlier training at Freiburg, Leadville,
and in the States generally. That training leads them to
assume that lead must be the basis of such a proposition.
There has always been trouble at Dapto and Aldershot,
which had not their own mines to fall back on, with regard
to lead supplies ; and when arrangements for a perma-
nent supply from Broken Hill were made, the price which
had to be paid was always a millstone. The general class
of customs ore received at a Queensland smelter would be
refractory gold ore, not lead ore. Even Ohillagoe, with
its extensive plant, can keep only one furnace going. As
a vehicle for collecting the precious metals, copper will
answer as well as lead ; and it has this advantage—that,
while the lead required is at least 12 to 15 per cent, of the
charge, 3 per cent, of copper in the charge will be quite
as effective. There is a future before a well-conducted
copper smelter in Queensland; but, although customs
lead-smelting may be a success for a time, it is doubtful
whether there is anything permanent ahead. A Broken
Hill is wanted in Queensland first.
* * * *
The Oaks (N.Q.) rush has started in a new line
of business. Several finds of rich tin ore have been made
a few miles to the east of that publess township. The
quantity lying about on the ground was so great that
many thought it couldn’t be tin. An assay, however,
gave 66 per cent, of black tin, carrying 70 per cent, of
metallic tin.
* * * *
Mararoa management (W.A.) hopes to treat
2500 tons a month and lower the grade to 435., or there-
abouts, when the additions to the mill are completed.
On this basis a clear profit of £1 per ton is anticipated ;
and, given management in the interests of the mine and
without regard for the market, the co. should be in a
strong financial position by the end of the year. Taking
the last official estimate of reserves as correct, the divi-
dend value of the developed ore runs out at about 7s. per
share. What it is a fair thing to pay for the chance of
getting more ore and more profits is entirely a matter of
opinion.

“H.C.”: Statement (B. 2/4/’08) that Wes-
tralian Red Hill Co. is being financed by tributors’
royalty goes too far. Since the co. acquired the leases on
December J, 1906, the expenditure on mine has been
£22,140, and the total royalty to date is £l7B. The co.
holds 108 acres, and the main reef, which extends 1600 ft.
in length, has all along been worked by the co. The March
crushing was 1065 tons for 3310 z., besides residues; and
April tonnage will be larger. Barring prime cost of leases,
only £750 working capital was provided, and all cost of
equipment and development has been won from ore
taken from about the 75ft. level.

In the past eight years— since the arrival of this
devastating Federation, in short—the Bank of Adelaide
has, besides increasing its dividend,'accumulated £135,000
of reserves, or only £35,000 less than the total for the
previous 35 years. In addition to formal reserves, the
chairman announced at the recent meeting in Adelaide
that the bank had £27,000 of internal reserves available
as the foundation of an officers’ provident fund. By an
overwhelming majority the shareholders agreed to estab-
lish this fund. (Loud applause—even if this paper has to
make all the joyful noise itself.)
* * * *
The result of the voting for A.M.P. directors
(three wanted) was: Black, 70,846; Winehcombe,
65,846 ; Fairfax, 64,387; Campbell, 23,966. Of course the
first-named three were the “ office ” nomination. Only
once has anybody won without that nomination. That
was when Benjamin Short was returned, on the strength
of his long and valuable service as a canvasser, so even
that was a case in which the officers probably had more to
do with the victory than the policy-holders themselves.
* * » *
Though the Westralian State Government is
such a lusty mendicant, the local municipalities haven’t
so far done anything very serious in the way of borrowing.
The State’s Statistical Register shows that, up to the
end of 1906, the aggregate of the loans was £713,350, and
in most cases there is a fairly respectable sinking fund at
work. At the same date the total of the savings in the
hands of the Sinking Fund Trustees was £110,005. Still,
many of the wretched little loans ought never to have
been raised at all; they were got for making roads and
forming gutters—in two or three cases for buying fire-
brigade carts and things—and a community that digs a
gutter and asks its grandchildren to pay for it hasn’t got
much spirit to be proud of.
* 9 * *
The shrinkage in gilt-edged stocks in London
during 1907 was 9 per cent, of their value on 31st De-
cember, 1906, or £342,000,000 altogether. Two stocks
may be taken as an example of the paralysing effect of
this fall in values. Rio Tiritos and De Beers a year ago
were worth seventy millions. On 21st March their market
value was thirty-four millions ! Australian Government
debentures suffered the least on the list, largely because
unusually-prosperous Australia was a consistent buyer,
and thus keDt prices stiff.
* * * *
The Darling Downs (Q.) have a co-operative
dairy co., now in its fourth year. For the past half-year
the profit was £1502 ; but this includes an estimated sur-
plus of £866, to be received from butter exports. This
butter-export business is highly speculative: for one
shipment this co. got 150 s. percwt., and a fortnight later
the market was down to 116 s. It is highly desirable,
therefore, for the protection alike of. shareholders and of
creditors, that a substantial special export reserve fund
should be created. So far no formal reserve whatever has
heen established. All profits, from whatever source, are
lumped. Six months ago the total was £1771, and no
less than £1552 of this was distributed either as dividend
(£224) or as bonus to suppliers (£1328). Apart from
ordinary trade debts, the co., with a capital of only
£3828, has an overdraft of £2lBO, which, with the capital,
has been put into plant. With very little in the way of
unfavorable conditions, the finances could easily become
rotten ; and directors ought to go very slow in the
matter of dividends and bonuses.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
The story of the recent experiences of
Sergeant Whelan, stationed at Coen (N.Q.), shows
what a Way back bobby’s life is peppered with.
He left Coen on January 4, to collect rural statis-
tics ; he had with him five trackers and 12 horses,
equipped as if for some exploring expedition. A
flood swept down the Archer, which, for a start,
meant much swimming of creeks and wading
across plains. Five weeks out, he received
a wire bidding him patrol Weipa Mission
Station and annex a “wanted” black. When
the party got to Mein telegraph station
with its prisoner, murders were announced
amongst the Archer River natives, and the ser-
geant was side-tr. -ked on to that job. After
fording and swimming many floods, he wired, on
March 22, that he could not reach Archer, but
had gone to Pascoe to assist a white man who was
said to be in danger. While there he got word
by telegram to investigate the cause of Paddy
Farrel’s death at Batavia. All this, time the
statistics were waiting. Officialdom then lost
trace of Sergeant Whelan amongst the flood-
waters of the rushing Gulf rivers, but it was after-
wards learned that his camp had been washed out
of existence and the twelve horses lost. Latest
accounts of the party were very indefinite, for the
rain persisted in putting up a record, and it was
reckoned that it might be a couple of months
more before Whelan could get back to a change of
clothes and a dry bed.

“ Vigilans ” ; Tambo is one of the oldest
towns in Central Queensland, and one of the
most backward. A bore throws 2,000,000 gallons
of water per day, and a stream meanders down
the gutter of the main street. From this the
inhabitants obtain supplies. Water isn’t laid on
to the buildings, because the majority of the in-
habitants object to additional expense. The
gutter is a good trap for drunks ; also at night the
stranger steps clean into it. There are no lamps
in Tambo. The Chow is strongly represented,
three stores and the usual bakery being run by
Confucius Pigtail. Buildings are old and rickety,
the courthouse and post office standing out in bold
relief. The usual School of Arts is not to be
found, and a sense of melancholy broods over the
place like a very large crow. Tambo is one of
those villages where the inhabitants have waited
vainly for something to turn up, and have aban-
doned hope. The barber tries to make life happy,
and is of a humorous turn of mind. This is his
signboard :
Cranilogical Tripsis and Crinicultural Abscission, Ton-
sorial Artist, Physiognomical Hair Dresser, Facial
Operator, Cranium Manipulator, Capillary Abridger.
A. B. Foley, Artist.

The fly is the curse of humanity in the
great, thinly-peopled areas of Western Queens-
land. Probably if there were more people there
would be fewer flies. It is pitiable to see in those
parts the large percentage of school children
whose sight has been injured by the ophthalmia
that the little buzzer carries around. The Kidston
Government has tackled the fly question, and Dr.
Taylor’s report is now before Parliament. The
public schools are largely blamed as the media of
contagion, and
improvements
are recommend-
ed. When the
disease has been
acquired, fre-
quent bathing of
the eyes with
boiled rain
water, to which
salt and boracic
acid have been
added, is recom-
mended ; it will
alleviate the
affection until
medical , assist-
ance can be se-
cured. Dr. Tay-
lor suggests that
the complaint lie
declared conta-
gious; that where
practicable, pro-
vision should be
made for its
scientific treat-
ment, either locally, or at the nearest coast centre ;
that chronic cases be treated at a sanatorium
near Brisbane; that qualified surgeons be ap-
pointed for the Northern, Central, and Southern
districts to superintend the treatment of patients ;
and that the doors and windows of schools be
enclosed with wire gauze.

“ Kink ” : River traffic on the Murray has
dwindled to almost nothing now. The droughts
and the railways to Cobram, Swan Hill, and
Mildura account for this. Still there are a few
timber barges plying on the Big Trickle in flood
time, for Echuca’s main industry, since the boiling
down works closed, is sawmilling. Round the
bend from the border town is a spot called Rotten
Row. Here a score of the staunch old crafts of
lang syne are rotting—hauled to the N.S.W.
bank side—unhonored and unused. On the
Victorian side, embedded in the mud, a few feet
from summer level, may be seen the remains of
the first vessel ever used on The Trickle. It was
made of staunch stuff, and was only the size of a
whale boat. It was sent overland to the river per
team by an unmarked route. One thing strikes
awe into the gizzard of the voyager Swan Hill-
wards, and that is the large tracts of country
visible on both sides of the Murray that seem
never to have carried timber for centuries—
certainly never in the recollection of the earliest
white man. Yet, here and there, where the
floods have worn the banks away, blackfellows’
ovens are made visible by the ashes, showing where
the tribal revels of long ago were held. How did the
blacks get the fuel to make their fires? Was it
carried long distances across country, or canoed
up pr down stream ? Anyhow, a large note of in-
terrogation lingers on the tongue when one views
the country from the river, barren and bare for
miles, without the tell-tale trace of a gum-root, or
of a snag long submerged, If timber ever did
exist in these areas of solitude it must have been
ages ago—for there is nary a characteristic of
timber country apparent, yet the relics of the
corroboree days are discernible in the buried ashes
of the past!
A RANGER.
MRS. REGISTRY (to cook from up country); “You are sure, Bridget , that you are accustomed
to ranges .”
BRIDGET: “An wasn't I horn an' bred on wan ?”
“ J.P.K.” : On the Paparoa Ranges, near
Blackball (M.L.), the Paparoa Coal Co. is opening
up a fresh mine. One of the new camps is called
“Soldier’s,”because an old soldier worked a sluic-
ing claim there years ago, and left a gruesome
story behind him. Several of the diggers near
by, on their way to Blackball for a spree, called
on the soldier, and asked him to join them. He
refused. On their return, a few days later, they
found him dead in his claim. A priest was
brought, the funeral service read, the sluice box
turned over on the body, and then the hole
was filled in. Many months afterwards, some of
the party were again on the spree at Blackball,
and their funds ran out. While discussing means
of raising money, one of them remembered the old
soldier buried under the sluice-box, and suggested
that they should go back and see if there was any
gold on the body—because “hatters” usually
carry their gold about with them. They went,
and, unearthing the sluice-box, started to work
putting the stuff through, bones and all. The
sight was too much for one of them, an old Ger-
man, who exclaimed “Mein Gott ! I can’t stand
this!” and left in a great hurry. The others worked
on, but found no gold. It was a dispirited lot
that got back to Blackball. There they were
astounded to find the old German in a state of
supreme happiness, and “paralytic.” It turned
out that when he had departed he had sneaked
away with him what were left of the decayed
soldier’s trousers, and in the pockets had found
over an ounce of gold. Then his mates made
remarks.
♦ * * *
The story of a close call :
Jones’s Flat, near Ross (M.L.), had been first of all
honeycombed with shafts by the alluvial gold miners,
and later on the sluicing claims had deposited their tail-
ings there, covering the surface 10ft. or 15lt. deep. In
the late ’sixties, one Kelly had his tent pitched on these
tailings, and one evening, while he was frying his bacon,
there was a roar and a rush, and Kelly, frying-pan, fire
and all disappeared. When the camp turned out there
was no sign of Kelly—there was merely a hole in the
tailings, and below that another hole. Kelly had
pitched his tent immediately over an old shaft, and
he was now buried in it. Men came in crowds—all Ross
was out. In 20-minute spells they toiled, two at the
windlass and one in the shaft, while others prepared the
slabs to timber the sides. They had got down 50ft. when
one of them heard a knocking that seemed to come from
half-way down the shaft. They located the sound at last,
and found that 30ft. from the top there .was a drive
which they had not noticed, as the tailings had
blocked the entrance. The drive was cleared,
and there they found Kelly about 20ft. from the
entrance, unharmed. He had come down the shaft
, with the first rush of the tailings, and had heen hurled
into the drive, which the tailings had not completely
filled. Kelly, who was the possessor of a substantial
balance at the bank, spent the .lot that night, and there
are still some people in Ross who talk about the spree
when the people of Jones’s Flat drank all Kelly’s loose
change.

“ Janor ” : “Jimmie Pannikin,” re goat
(B. 9/4/’08), presents the usual glimpse of the
Bush from the towny’s point of view ; conse-
quently he garnishes his goat yarn with a
‘‘haunch,” a joint unknown in the Bush, and
which the average bushie wouldn’t recognise if it
fell on him. But any of him can distinguish goat
meat from mutton by the color, texture, and lack
of flavor. No need for the tell-tale hairs with
which “ J.P.” adorns his haunch. Why drag hair
in, anyhow ? Mutton is served without wool;
why not goat without hair? Goat flesh is de-
spised in the Bush, and rightly so in a country
where good mutton is cheap and plentiful. It is
only fairly palatable in the kid stage, and then it
is usually dished up in a pie, with plenty of
seasoning to hide the dry insipidity of the flesh.
The tough, tasteless galah also appears under a
crust for the same reason. As food the galah is a
horror, and rarely eaten. Leaving out a few
faddists, food maniacs, and misers, the bushman
only eats tucker of the goat, galah, and gohanna
kind from necessity, and it is sad feeding for him.
As for the allegations about “ colossal ignorance ”
and “ unreasoning prejudice,” bah!
On the disputed subject of aboriginal
glass spear heads, Alfred Searcy (who wrote “In
Australian Tropics ”) sends along this letter which
he has received from Dr. Lynch, formerly Govern-
ment Medical Officer at Australia’s back-door
The only glass spear-head I ever saw in the Territory, I
had the good fortune to secure. It was at a big lagoon,
“ The Serpentine,” about 20 miles west of the Daly River
Mission Station. This was in 1892, on one of my periodi-
cal visits as Government Medical Officer, and a big mob
of niggers from the westward (Fitzmaurice district) were
camped there. They knew very little English, but I
managed to make them understand our visit was a
friendly one, and, -after some presents of beads and to-
bacco, they gave me a dilly bag, which contained,
amongst other things, a beautifully moulded spear-head,
fashioned from the side of an aerated (glass) water bottle.
The concave-convex Shape is distinct, and leaves no doubt
as to what it is. There was also in the bag a barb about
six inches long from the tail of a stingray ; this was
ground off to a sharp point, and wrapped round with some
wax and string. Both the glass spear-head and the barb
had been dipped in decayed human flesh, and the natives
most demonstratively warned,me to be careful of scratch-
ing myself with them. They gave me to understand that
the barb was kept in great secrecy by the old gins, and
used as a lethal weapon during the victim’s sleep, or in
the excitement of corroboree orgies. The victims usually
died a few' days after. These were the only poisoned
weapons I ever heard of or saw.

Winton (C.Q.) is “ skiting ” about a queer
horse it owns. The yarn is that, some time ago,
while feeding from a nosebag, the hessian caught
fire and the scared animal bolted. In its career
it collided with a sharp stick, which penetrated
the forehead bone. The burns, as they healed,
closed up the nostrils, but the hole in the forehead
does duty instead, and the animal now breathes
that way regularly.
TRUSTFUL WILLIAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The team comes to a standstill, and Bullocky Bill is
glad to stop and have a yarn with you. However in-
credulous other people may be at the length of your
journey, he can exchange faithful confidences with you,
and will slowly remark, “ Long stage, Boss,” whereas the
ordinary passer-by will say, “ Ah, garn, what are ye
givin’us?”— W. M. Hamlet's “ Travel Pictures” (S. M.
Herald, 2/5/’08).
A wonderfully pleasant soul is Bill,
Who calls the cattle home (and other things) ;
No carping doubts his kindly broodings fill,
Suspicion William far behind him flings.
Hast a loved snake-yarn that has time and oft
Been jeered at, as mendacious, in the town ?
Take it to Bill; his heart (and brain) are soft—
With William it will certainly go down.
Do you require an audience of one
To listen to your travel-stories, and
To cheer at intervals ? The thing is done
When Bill arrives with bullock-whip in hand.
Mayhap your fav’rite lie revolves round beer,
Or stoush, or rum, or other things that kill
When earnestly partaken of ? Don’t fear.
Gather it up, and sling it off at Bill.
Writers have hitherto passed Bill the bun
As linguist with a voice like war’s alarm.
Yet when methinks the truth is forced on one,
Bill’s simple hound-like trust’s his chiefest charm.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

“ K. D. Starr ” : In the matter of chained
tykes, some people have a mania for tying-animals
up. At a put) near Wentworth (N.S. W.) there
used to be a captive dingo securely fastened by a
bullock chain. In time he grew tame enough to
the family that owned him, down to the youngest
baby; but outside that circle no one dared ap-
proach him, not even the butcher boy who passed
him everyday. All day long the captive used to
sit, watching with implacable yellow eyes, and all
night long he howled dismally to his kin afar in
the scrub. Ido not think he ever slept. Why he
was kept Heaven alone knows, for he was neither
use nor ornament, and his owner frequently used
to beat him with a rail for howling the howl that
God had given him. But one day, after the years
of his pitiful captivity had numbered eight, the
Lord remembered him. A neighboring child ven-
tured within the ring of his chain circle, and
retired with a torn cheek and reduced calf, and to
satisfy the neighbor the animal’s owner reluc-
tantly shot the beast. It was a merciful relief.
* * * *
‘‘Polstan”: We have all heard of the
man who can tell you the time to the minute,
although he may not have seen a timepiece for a
month ; of the man who can mimic every-
thing on earth, including a fried egg
in the distance ; of the bloke who
plays Wagner on his front teeth with
a pen-handle ; and so on. But my
expert is a catcher of falling ar-
ticles, and he arrests them in the
air as easily and naturally as a cat
drops on its feet. Was watching
him, the other day, sitting at a bench,
working. He moved his elbow and
swept a bottle of ink, two pens and a
ruler off the board, and he caught the lot
before they got halfway to the floor. Just
to try him, L knocked a pailful of assorted
articles off the bench when he wasn’t look-
ing. Like a flash he turned instinctively
and grabbed so fast that I couldn’t see his
hands going. He caught the lot. That
man has never smashed anything, through
letting it fall, in his life. All the same,
the knack has its drawbacks. Recently
his sister commenced to pour out a cup of
tea whilst he had his back towards
her. Like lightning he revolved
as the sound of the falling liquid
reached his ear, and grabbed at the
scalding tea as it left the pot. For-
tunately he caught his breatli in time, or his
sister might have been shocked.

THE DEGRADATION OF IT.
SAVED SAM (gloomily): “ Yes, it's drink an gambling that brought me to this."
Jimmie Pannikin ” : At last the motor-
car has reached the Hindquarters of Beyond for
practical purposes. Recently Mr. 0. E. Taylor, of
Bootra Station (N.S.W.), defying the sand and
saltbush, drove a 10 h.p. De Dion motor-car from
Broken Hill to his home, which is on the Farther-
out-side of White Cliffs, 240 miles from Silver
City. The actual travelling time was 15 hours
45 minutes. The car has been over the Open
Spaces track twice. The only mishap was a
puncture. The chauffeur in charge has come to
the conclusion that the regions which drag at the
Tail of the Sunset no longer offer serious obstacles
to the practical motorist. He asserts that he had
no trouble in getting through the sand. But three
years’ experience West-o’-Darling leads me to
fancy that motor-cars will not be successful in the
Dim Remote unless provided with very wide tyres
for the sandy stretches. It will be interesting to
learn how the motor-car affects the stoic gravity
of the camel-trains. Petrol smell cannot be worse
than the odor of Afghan camel-conductors. How
the horse-teamsters will rejoice if the motor-car
terrifies the ship of the desert just as the said shic
maddens the average steed !

THE LOVES OF THEN AND NOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
0, the western girlies’ love we knew in the days of
Auld Lang Syne ;
0, the western maidens’ love was true, and their eyes
’ were large and fine I
Yet we lost them all, aye, one by one, as the sad years
onward rolled—
But their cheeks were kissed by the rising sun, and their
hair was brown and gold 1
Ah, our love was then a sacred thing, untarnished by
change or chance,
And you?... You smiled for your Youthful King and
wept for your First Romance !
Yet well you kept your innocence—learned nothing of
Words or Art;
But I loved those things with a love intense, and so we
drifted apart 1
We met each night where the roadway twists, and we
wandered out by the streams
When my hopes were vague as the mantling mists, and
our world was a world of dreams !
And I lived for you while the cynics said that love was
wasted and wild ;
But you were a thoughtless maiden led, and I was a
foolish child !
I told my love (Oh, the world was fair!) and vowed it
would never change
(But what could I know' of The Days to Come and The
Cities Beyond the Range ?)
I said your eyes in their softening shades were brighter
than all the stars
(Ah, but what knew I of the Mateless Maids, and the
Houris Behind the Bars?).
I said my love was a lasting love, and you kissed me in
reply,
While the glistening frondage waved above, and the
planets swung in the sky !
And I believed my words that night, though the passing
years have proved
That the Loves of Then and the Loves of Now are a
thousand times removed !
I toyed with your clustered curls, as we leaned and
laughed on the boundary fence,
And the hushful hours that intervened were filled with a
lone suspense.
I said your cheeks were gardens fair, where red roses ever
bloomed
(And often I kissed the blossoms there—what though they
were unperfumed !).
Your love was a sacred treasure then, and mine was a
youthful pride ;
But I felt the longing to write of men where the walks of
the world are wide.
And you? . . . You shielded your innocence (and that is a
w'oinan’s art) — La
But I loved the world with a love intense; and so we
drifted apart!
0 the Western girlies’ love we knew in the days of Auld
Lang Syne !
0 the Western maidens’ love was true, and their eyes
were superfine !
Yet we lost them all, aye, one by one, as our hearts grew
warm —or cold—
But their cheeks were kissed by the rising sun, and their
hair was brown and gold !

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
John McL. : Is it worth wasting the space?
(1) Your original statement was that N.S.W. gave the
other States the run of its open markets, while they
closed their markets against N.S.W. Now you admit
the N.S.W. sugar duty, wine duty, bacon, cheese, ham,
tobacco, etc., duties. (2) You also admit that the other
States were large buyers of N.S.W. goods, which you
previously denied. (3) The correspondent in question
didn’t, as you assert, “ overlook the very important fact
that out of Victoria’s imports from N.S.W. of
£3,294,618 worth, the sum of £1,399,442 represented
wool for oversea export.” He drew most special atten-
tion to that, and also to the fact, which you overlook,
that of N.S.W.’s imports of £5,301,214 from Victoria no
less than £3,489,150 was gold. (4) Is it, in any case,
worth arguing further about the alleged “Freetrade”
of N.S.W., which used to collect over £2,000,000
a year in Customs duties, practically all of them
protective duties ? In 1892, and several other
years, the Customs revenue collected in N.S.W.
were more than those in Victoria Sydney

Thomson : There is just one large hole in your argument.
A Protectionist country doesn’t tax the things it can’t
produce itself, therefore Freetrade and Protectionist
countries both buy our wool. But the woollen industries
of some countries are growing because of Protection,
while those of other countries are decaying owing to
Freetrade. So it comes to pass that in 1901 the United
Kingdofn bought about 60 per cent, of our exported
wool, while in 1906 it only bought about 45 per cent. If
’T&jjrtain takes to Protection and rebuilds its wool indus-
tries it will want to buy more Australian wool ; if it
keeps ■on with Freetrade it will probably want to
buy a great deal less. And until we can use
our wool in our own manufactures we would
sooner sell to J. Bull than to anybody else....

The nearest approach to shaving water
thrown up by any of the artesian bores in
N. S. Wales is to be had on the track between
Wanaaring and Milparinka—and that’s almost too
far to bring a mug from every morning, Mil-
parinka being 811 miles from Sydney, and the
track not asphalted all the way. There are two
bores in that road, the Clifton and the Tinaroo,
where the water is 139 degrees warm when it
reaches the surface. The depth of the hole doesn’t
seem to have much connection with the heat of
the water. Clifton, for instance, is only 1638 ft.
deep, while a private bore on Warrana, practically
the same depth (1660 ft.), gives a flow which is a
mere 90 degrees warm. The Kenmare geyser, on
the track to Hungerford, is probably the best—
anyway the biggest—of the State-owned supplies;
it yields a trifle of 2,050,000 gallons a day. But
numerous private bores top that; one on Lila
Springs runs to 4,000,000 gallons, and Lissington
station’s No. 4 is about the same size in waterspouts.

When Bill the Burglar got run in
He’d close cropped hair and shaven chin.
“Why groanest thou?” the warder said.

The widow of Mark Hammond, ex-
M.L.A., did not long survive her husband. She
died on May 3, aged 02 years.
Sample of the learned eloquence of Motor-
car Kelly, M.H.R., of Wentworth—he of the

barked at by a thing like you ! ”
Charles Tucker, ex-Mayor of Adelaide—
who defeated King O’Malley at Encounter Bay
for a seat in the S.A. Assembly—-was released
from Yatala Stockade on May 5. He has grown
quite grey while in seclusion.
Premier Tom Price made the directors of
the Colonial Mutual Insurance Society tremble,
for he has a policy of £lOOO with them, and when
motoring in England, last month, the driver
nearly carried him over a precipice, owing to a
sudden fog. Tom generously remarks that he is
glad the society did not lose its money.
Obit in London, aged 90 years, Isabella
Baker, widow of John Baker, one time Premier
of South Australia, in which State he arrived in
1838. The deceased lady was the daughter of
George Allan, of Allanvale, Tasmania, and was
married in June, 1838. Her eldest son is Richard
Chaffev Baker, late President of the Senate. Old
John Baker made only one appearance in S.A.
Ministerial life ; he was Premier for 10 days, and
iheld no portfolio either before or afterwards.
JOE THOMAS , M.H.S.
A pioneer miner, Richard Higgs, died
recently in the St. Arnaud (Vic.) hospital. Higgs
was one of a party that, in 1855, struck gold in
New Bendigo, now known as St. Arnaud North.
A rush followed, the area of discovery was ex-
tended, and the town of St. Arnaud was the
Jesuit. It was named after the first French
Commander-in-Chief in the Crimea—the amaz-
ingly diseased person who got the command in
his last hours as a reward for services rendered
and murder done in Nap. lll.’s usurpation.
George Connolly, Government Architect
in Queensland from the early ’seventies till ’93,
when he retired, is dead. Most of the fine
structures that recall the profligacy of the Con-
tinuous Government were either designed or
completed by him, and his hearse should have
been dragged grave-wards by a white elephant, in
memory of the work he had to do.
V ” : I have no idea what kind of
politician Redwood, of Toowoomba (Q.), makes,
but the barley-growers of the Darling Downs
should have a good word for him. Back in 1877
barley-raising was a big thing on the Downs, and
Perkins’ brewery was taking all that could be
grown. When the brainy E. W. Pechey re-
signed, Patrick Perkins and Angus Mackav
had a tussle for the seat (Aubigny), and
Perkins won. Somebody said that the
barley-growers voted for Mackay ; any-
way “Paddy” suddenly discovered
that Queensland barley wouldn’t
malt, and those who had a crop on
hand had to make bacon of it.
There was one exception—he sent
100 bushels down to Castlemaine,
and, between one charge and an-
other, he was called upon to send
a postal note for 9d. to square
accounts. Barley-growing at once
fell into disgrace, and remained
so for nearly 20 years. In the
mid nineties the Redwoods
came over from Maoriland,
Vernon being under engage-
ment as maltster in Perkins’
brewery. He tested Queens-
land barley, and said it made
first-class malt, and then barley-
raising boomed again Since then
Vernon Redwood has run his own
malt-house, and Darling Downs barley-
growing has gone on prospering.
Queensland District Court Judge Rut-
ledge, who was a Wusslean parson before setting
out in pursuit of Costs, had a racecourse sharping
case to try the other day, and declared himself
ignorant of the meaning of “deaden” and
( punter, although he admitted knowing what
stiffen means. Rutledge, who probably was
never on a racecourse in his life, was once
Attorney-General in a Philp Ministry, and should
have picked up some worldly wisdom while hold-
ing that job.
Some idea of the length of time over
which the late C. B. Fisher’s manhood stretched
is given by the fact that he won the prize for
“ potted butter ” at the first Adelaide Show in
1844. His father, James Hurtle Fisher, came
out with Governor Hindmarsh, in 1836 ; they had
co-equal powers, one as Governor, and the other
as Resident Lands Commissioner representing
the South Australian Company. They quarrelled
so, however, that both were removed from their
positions, and the next Governor was made
supreme in S.A.
S.A. “ Union Parliament,” a debating body
which trains the young men of Adelaid’e in a
knowledge of public affairs, has just celebrated
its 25th year. Its first “Ministry/’ in 1884 in-
eluded Jenkins, now Agent-General; Sowden
now editor of the Register ; and Johnson, now
Mayor of Adelaide. The Parliament is made up
of the picked men of close on 50 literary societies.
Prominent in it at various times have been Price
Premier of S.A. ; Propsting, since Premier of
4 asmama; Vardon, of the Federal Senate; and
Willis, M.H.R.
One John Gallagher turned up in N.S.W.
Full Court last week to remark that he wasn’t
dead, and would the Court please give him back
100 acres of land which had been sold by the
administratrix of his estate, she having some
years ago told the Probate Court that he was
deader than he was. The Full Court looked at
the probate papers, then its component parts
looked at each other, and then they all looked at
Gallagher, and the decision was that Gallagher
was at least dead enough for probate purposes.
Jell Stow, about the most picturesque
old party that ever sat in the editor’s chair of an
Australian city daily, has died at last. Parsons’
sons are rarely just average boys ; they either get
into the rut that leads to the old man’s pulpit or
they rebel and run wild. “Jeff” ran wild, and
roved about the northern parts of Australia
mainly because there was more room there to kick’
Then he pulled in a bit, settled down on the
?• Aus. Advertiser, and let his hair run wild
instead. He reached the Advertiser’s chair-
but he was never happy in the confinement, and
so he got himself appointed a Stipendiary Magis-
trate, and held on to that job till he was called off
at 70. He was the best yarn-spinner in S. Aus
and wrote a readable book or two, mostly of travei
and knockabout life.
This year the Sydney Observatory cele-
prates its jubilee. On a tablet on the building is
inscribed the legend : &
Erected 1858.
Sir W. T. Denison, K.C.8.,
Governor-General.
The first Astronomer was Lieutenant Dawes of
the Marines, whom Governor Phillip located on
Point Maskelevne, now Dawes Point. Sir Thomas
Brisbane, in the early ’twenties, dabbled in
astronomy, and built himself an observatory at
Parramatta. We have also had several amateur
stargazers, but the first Government Astronomer
was Rev. William Scott, who retired in 1865, and
is understood to be still living, He was followed
by George R. Smalley, who filled the post from
1865 to July, 1870. The late H. C. Russell was
then appointed. The just-deceased H. A. Lenehan
was Acting-Government Astronomer from 1903 to
the end of 1907.
Robert Dunbar, who voluntarily retires
from the General Traffic-Managership of Banana
land railways, has been continuously in the same
service for 44 years. His age alone stood between
him and the Deputy Commissionership, filled a
few months ago by the appointment of ex-Auditor-
General King. Dunbar was appointed to the
charge of the Ipswich railway stores in 1864, and
in 1870 was transferred to the traffic branch over
which he now presides. In 1884 he was appointed
auditor and inspector, and in 1885 he became
of the northern railways. 'After
filling a similar position in the central and southern
divisions, he was appointed General Traffic-
Manager in succession to Thallon, who was raised
to the Commissionership. Mr. Dunbar is entitled
to a small pension as one of the contributors to

Captain W illiam Cottier, a very ancient
mariner, who retired from the sea 20 years ago,
and went to live on an arm of it at George’s River,
near Sydney, died on May 6, aged 87 years. For
many years he was connected with the old A.S.N.
Company, and he was commodore of the fleet
when the company merged into the A.U.S.N. Co.
In his time he commanded the Clarence and The
Lady Bowen.
