Quayle Hudson is fast graduating for the
position of a historical character. He, or what
remains of him, resides at Germanton (N.S.W.).
A short time ago a pea rifle went off accidentally’
and took away a couple of his fingers. Next, his
house was burned to the ground. Next, while
rushing to meet his mother (or his mother rushing
to meet him), a pair of scissors she was carrying
entered his abdomen, and he retired for overhaul
and repairs. Just after being mended, he laid
hold of a knife to trim a horse’s tail, and the blade
slipped and severed an artery in Hudson’s leg.
That is as far as he has got up to now ; but there
are still possibilities.
Daddy Young, ex-Minister of N.S.W., ex-
Speaker, ex-Protectionist, ex-Freetrader, and
latterly in the cold generally, drops off at 74. He
looked, felt, and was, mentally, older. In Parlia-
ment his voice never counted as a saving or
making influence, and his knowledge of politics
was rudimentary. As Speaker he was a calamity,
but he regarded himself as something specially
excellent in that line of business. When he re-
signed the wig and the income he felt quite
certain that a sympathetic vote would restore him
in a few months, but the durable Abbott had got
the job, and Abbott stuck, and no one seemed
to notice the absence of Young. His life repre-
sents nearly 28 years of Parliamentary service. In
his private capacity J. H. Young led an unevent-
ful life. He was . born in Hampshire (England).
He did a little time at sea in the P. and O. ser-
vice, but gave that up in ’53, and went storekeep-
ing at Port Macquarie. Then in 1860 he moved
to Sydney, and for most of the rest of his days he
did battle with the onion in Sussex-street. His
death leaves only one N.S.W. ex-Speaker above
ground—Justice Barton of the High Court.
C. B. Fisher, “father of the Australian
turf,” weighed-in at Glenelg (S.A.) last week,
aged 90. He arrived in S.A. in 1836, and started
out to raise the woolly sheep with his brother
Hurtle. In the latter ’so’s the two brothers
founded the Maribyrnong stud, near Mel-
bourne ; and when Hurtle dropped out, in
1859, as the result of being knocked about
m a shipwreck, C.B. took charge of the
steed foundry, which has made a name for
itself as big as a mastodon throughout this
Commonwealth, and has supplied winners for
most of the events on the card. Mr. Fisher
was a prominent member of the Victorian
Racing Club from its inception, and for a
long time its Chairman. The two-mile flutter,
the C. B. Fisher Plate, was instituted in acknow-
ledgment of his services to the club.
“F. Croson,” from Melbourne :
Selina Murray Macdonald Sutherland, as the Govern-
ment Gazette calls the lady whom all Melbourne knows
and loves as Miss Sutherland, has been having* trouble
With her catmittee. Years ago, she and Dr. Strong, then
pastor of Scots’ Church, started the Presbyterian
Children s Aid Society, and, the lady being born for that
work, it succeeded. Presently the Church became
jealous of the woman and took over the business, where-
upon Miss S. started a society of her own. She called it
the Victorian Neglected Children’s Aid Society, and an
ornamental committee of cats was appointed as a o*uarari-
tee of good faith with the public. Miss Sutherland
her self-appointed work well, and thousands of
children passed through her home from helplessness and
hopelessness to self-respect and safety. But latterly a
new catmittee arose which knew not'Miss S. This cat-
mittee—or at least a majority of it—desired her to resign.
She declined. The cats met, with great enlargement of
the tail, and squalled fiercely on the roof. Also they
interviewed Chief Secretary Peacock, who told them they
must “ relieve ” the lady, appoint a new manager for the
Home, and inform him, whereupon he would gazette the
withdrawal of Miss S.’s license as guardian of the children,
.and appoint a new manageress in her stead. All this was
done, and the catmittee prepared to step in and run the
business—which another had built up—on its own new and
approved lines. Unfortunately for the new cats, Miss
Sutherland wasn’t taking any fluffy, superior Lady Bounti-
fuls, and promptly denied them admittance to the Home
■where the city’s waifs have ever found shelter. The cats
threatened a raid in force. Miss S. requisitioned a con-
stable to keep the peace, and there promised to be a
notable siurmish, with some of Melbourne’s leading
ladies facing a magistrate on a charge of common assault"
and ordmarv bashed hat. But friends intervened
and the stern-faced but kind-hearted comforter of Mel-
bourne’s lost will, in future, hang up her sixpenny hat
and hold out her friendly hand at the Receiving Home in
Latrobe-street. How the cats and the new matron will
shape at the Parkville Home remains to be seen, but they
W ,‘ Ilxt® t 0 show almoBt the sympathy and the tenderness
of the Nazarene to equal Miss Sutherland’s record. And
the average catmittee is wont to motor out with the
gjn-e rather than tramp along with the Carpenter’s

The late C. B. Fisher was responsible for
many of the best stories told about Hindtnarsh
bouth Australia’s eccentric first Governor flip
Governor’s first levee in Adelaide was, according
to Fisher, the most striking ever held in Aus^
tralia. “My goodness !” said Hindmarsh “what
does dis mean ? ” He had been in Heligoland and
was something of a Dutchman. The mystery was
explained later ; there were only two dress-coats
in the settlement, and the loyal subjects, as they
retired, had to undress and give the garments to
the next persons with right of entree. Hind-
marsh had noticed the undressing and the fleeing
of his subjects in their shirt-sleeves. George
Milner Stephen, the man who breathed on the
sick and the dead and cured them with red flannel
was a great power in that vice-regal court. Hind-
marsh was his father-in-law, and Stephen ran the
show for a time. That was before he became a
curative breather. George Stephen, who died a
few years ago in Sydney, was born in the primi-
tive mansion, and Harold, the well-known littera-
teur, picked up as a nipper much of his know-
ledge of high life by observing the rows that went
on between Hindmarsh and his dad.
Dr. H. R. Horne, who drove out of his
Traralgon (Vic.) practice in a capsizing motor-car
recently, had two close calls before the fatal one.
First, he was shot through the lungs and heart in
the Boer war; and, later, he was unexpectedly
engaged by bandits what time he was pushing
business in Tungau (China) for the China Mutual
Life Assurance Society. The doctor and his fellow
fiend (Eadie) put up such a willing fight (although
severely mauled) that the Chinkies got the tired
feeling, and left. Horne had no business after
that to go motoring, or riding, or driving, or to
patronise Tait’s trains, or to go out on Friday, or
to sit down 13 at table. After two such close calls
as he had, he should have recognised that he had
used up his luck. In each of his first two scraps
with Death he was killed quite dead according to
all the rules of the game, and his recovery almost
amounted to unprofessional conduct. A great
recoverer was Dr. Horne. He even recovered
£lOOO damages from the Chow treasury.
THE DEVILS’ UNION SMOKE-NIGHT.
"Say, George, that’s a terrible weed you're
smoking. ”
“ Yes. It’s one of the last batch of larrikins.
Yours is a rotter, too.
“ Oh, well, it’s a blooming capitalist.”
It was a grizzled old warrior, a monu-
mental mason named Tope, who raked up the
scandal in connection with the St. Kilda (Vic.)
cemetery, and hung on grimly to his grievance till
the chief executive officer went away for a while
into seclusion. But the ancient isn’t finished yet,
and in flaming-red handbills he scatters the infor-
mation around Melbourne that “ shocking things
are still allowed to exist,” and he threatens a
public meeting at which he will expound what
they are.
Chief-inspector A. P. Stewart, who follows
Francis Adams as General Manager of the A.J.S.
Bank, and thus takes on about the toughest con-
tract in Australia, didn’t begin banking till he
was 24, and then started with the A.J.S. as a
junior at £5O a year. Up to that time he had
been “on the land” on the northern rivers
(IN.S.W.), where he was born. He was still a
country manager at Armidale when he w 7 as ap-
pointed chief inspector) He got the job, it was
said, because he had pointed out that there was a
horrible smash coming ; and when it did come
they reckoned that he was too good a prophet to
waste in a country town. Though reputedly a
humorist, A. P. Stewart —largely, perhaps, on
account of the job he has had —hasn’t made many
people smile these last 12 or 15 years.
A STATESMAN AMONGST THE GIRLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was on Friday afternoon, at the Max Schliiter
“ At Home,” a fantastic fascination in watching Mr. Bruce
Smith, K.C., who sat in the winter garden with the
“ Salons of the Past” still on his mind. Few of us can
eat a cress sandwich with grace. —Sydney Star “ Woman's
Chatter Column,” 6/sl’oB.
He is so fine,
So big, so grandiose ; an almost regal air
Pervades our afternoon “ At Homes” when he is there,
So that from ev’ry suburb worshippers repair,
Winding (not vine) but blameless tea-leaves in their hair,
To watch him shine.
Observe the clothes 1
Consider how resplendently his plug hat gleams *
There on the floor, his gamp beside. By Heaven, it seems
Like some huge sable Koh-i-noor—a gem o’ dreams :
Ah ! do you wonder that we girls pour forth in streams
What time Smith glows ?
But hush ! he speaks :
Place gently down the cup, put by th’ unfinished bun,
And list! Sesquipedalian discourse has begun,
And till this course of polysyllables has run,
Let no one move. Shame, shame on the anarchic one
Who causes creaks.
’Tis o’er at last.
And now occurs one of his very grandest feats :
Couchant upon a stool the great man deftly eats
A sandwich made of cress ! ! Cheer upon wild cheer
greets
The deed. Then silence—and a small voice fain tly bleats
“ My, but he’s vast 1 ”

GOOD BLOOD MEANS GOOD HEALTH..
Pale, Weak Sydney Woman Built up by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.
Just a Shadow op her old self ; Cold Hands
and Feet ; Ankles Puffed up ; No
Strength to do Anything ; Unable to
Sleep.
The lack of sufficient red health-giving blood
doesn’t end merely in a pale complexion. It’s
more serious. Bloodless people are the tired,
languid, run down persons who never have a bit
of enjoyment in life. Everything’s a bother.
Food doesn’t nourish : there’s indigestion, heart
palpitation, headache, backache ; sometimes faint-
ing fits and always nervousness. If anaemia or
bloodlessness be neglected too long, a decline often
starts. Sufferers become so downcast and disheart-
ened that they believe they have most of the
diseases possible to have.
Just a little more blood cures everything. To
cure everything, so simple too. Just more, rich
red blood ; then abounding health and vitality and
a pleasure in life. To get more blood the remedy
is Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
That’s what they are for. No other medicine
increases the blood supply so quickly—the cure
begins with the first dose. This is not a mere
claim. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have been doing
this over and over again in Australia for twelve
years.
* ‘ A few years ago my blood got very weak and
watery and I was very ill indeed,” said Mrs.
Nellie Mitchell, 20 Broughton Street, Glebe,
Sydney. “ I couldn’t bear to sit down to a meal;
if I ate a little I’d bring it all up again. I had a
terrible weight on my chest, as if I had a leaden
weight there ; some days it was worse than others,
and there was also a dull ache between my
shoulder blades. I grew very thin ; I was just
like a shadow of my old self. I hadn’t a particle
of color in my face or hands, my ankles puffed up
so that I couldn’t get my boots on. and my feet
and hands were as cold as stones. I hadn’t any
strength, it was as much as I could do to struggle
out of bed and dress myself. If my sister hadn’t
come and given me a hand I would never have got
through my housework. I attended the hospital
for two years, but I might just as well have stayed
away. Straight down the centre of my forehead
I had a most dreadful pain, and I had some very
bad bouts of neuralgia. My face seemed on fire.
What mostly killed me was the sleeplessness. I’d
toss about the whole night through. I seldom
went out My heart troubled me a lot. At times
I thought it would stop beating, and then again it
would beat like mad and I thought that I was
dying. At the slightest sound I’d tremble like a
leaf. One day I picked up a book telling about
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. As the doctor’s medi-
cine wasn’t doing me any good I thought I’d try
them. I took four boxes before I could see that I
was any better. After that I began to get a bit
of an appetite, and presently I could sit down to
the table and eat without any unpleasant after
effects. As I kept on with Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I got a little color in my face and gained in
weight. I also began to sleep better and felt less
weary in the day time. Now I Dan do a day’s
work with anyone, for my health is excellent.”
By their direct action on the blood Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills actually har« cured anaemia, sciatica,
indigestion, paralysis, biliousness, nervousness,
headaches, backaches, neuralgia, debility, rheu-
matism, and all ailments of women.
Sold by all chemists and storekeepers, and by
the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Australasia
Ltd., Sydney, 2s. 9d. a box, six boxes, 15s. 3d.,
post free.

POLITICAL
POINTS
One of the jokes of the N.S.W. by-
elections these times is the clamor that the people
should support the Wade Ministry (which is partly
Waddell and Perry) because it put an end to the
fearsome extravagance of the See Government
(which was also partly Waddell and Perry). This
makes things look mixed for Waddell and Perry.
The blowfly party” in the Federal
party —Reid and the lijjJe section of Reidites,
whose main happiness is to blow on the reputa-
tion of Australia—is just now filled with anger
because the Australian Government circulated a
reprint of an article from The Lone Hand
“ Prolific Australia,” saying a good word for our
motherland. It evidently hurts the Stinking
Fishites keenly to think of Australia being
praised.
The license-reduction party in Sturt, an
outlying district of Adelaide, owed much of its
recent victory at the poll to a skilfully-conceived
and stage-managed advertisement. Joe Coleman,
a popular fisherman, is in gaol awaiting his trial for
slaying policeman Ring. Legally, the shooting
was done in cold blood, but as a fact the blood
was heated by much liquor. And the day before
the poll there appeared a prominent advertise-
ment, by two persons who “have had recently
brought home to us in a very tragic way the evils
of the Drink traffic,” and want “the places of
danger reduced as far as possible.” That appeal
was signed simply “Ida D. Ring, S. Coleman ” !
EXIT—UNION JACK.
In the official statutory rules, issued by the military
authorities, is found the following :—Regulation 35: The
leading “Union Jack” is cancelled, and “Australian En-
sign” substituted.— News Item.
AUSTRALIA (to J. Bull): “ Returned with thanks."
When we get a decent patent law and some-
thing like real Protection, Australia will simply
boom along. The stump-jumping plough, the
reaper, the harvester, the forest devil, and a few
other things invaluable for the man on the land
havebeenevolvedby Australians, only to be pirated
by the Yankees. And now a Victorian has
blown along with a new machine milker which is
said to knock the Canadian idea kite high, and a
Queenslander has invented a motor plough which,
with 22h.p., is calculated to plough five acres per
d a y ; There is plenty of inventive talent in Aus-
tralia if it could only get the chance to do some-
thing with the things it creates.
A letter from Perth :
Sir,—ln writing of the exodus of farmers from
Victoria to New South Wales and other States,
you say it was reported that half-a-dozen were
off to try their luck in Westralia “of all
places. ”
It is inexplicable to me that such a usually
well-informed paper as Thf, Bulletin should
display such ignorance, or prejudice, in making
such a contemptuous reference to the agricul-
tural capabilities of this State. I have con-
siderable knowledge of South Australia; l
also know something of the other States, and
I am satisfied the opportunities presented by
Westralia are superior to any of them.
There are millions of acres still available
here for farmers at 10s. per acre, with 20
years’ credit free of interest, where the rainfall
is regular and reliable, ranging from 12 to 40
inches, and suitable for cereal and root crops.
Light agricultural railways are being pushed
out from the main lines, at a cost not exceed-
ing £lOOO per mile, wherever they are re-
quired.
There is a land boom on here, and the
Government office is crowded daily with selec-
tors.
I will not trouble you with figures to prove
what I say. These are easily obtainable if
you care to pursue the inquiry.
This State has, during the past two years,
been suffering a mild reaction from the gold
boom, but it is now building up a firm and
lasting prosperity upon a much firmer basis
than that of mining, though the latter will,
for very many years, be a great industry
without a doubt.
A few years ag > we were large importers of
foodstuffs, and now we are exporters, perhaps
to a small extent as compared with some of the other
States ; still the available surplus is rapidly increasing
each year.
Our wheat average will compare very favorably with
any of the other States. In horse feed we have helped
to supply the shortage in N.S.W. and Victoria to the
extent of 3000 tons, notwithstanding the heavy freight.
In cattle we number upwards of 700,000, and our sheep
are increasing at a rapid rate. During the last year or
so we lost to a small extent by emigration over im-
migration, but the tide is now setting the other way, and
our population is larger to-day than ever it was.
I fear you are not posted up in the agricultural and
pastoral statistics of W.A., or you would hardly have
allowed such a sneer to escape you.
I am, sir, etc.,
Thos. Ooombe.
138 Murray-street, Perth, W.A.
Queensland State Premier Kidston goes to
England fairly well convinced that, if he joins
Philp or if Philp joins him, he will not be able to
reckon on the continued support of either Treasurer
Airey or Railway Minister Kerr.
“Larry” O’Loughlin, in the absence of
Premier Price in England and two of his col-
leagues at the Premiers’ Conference in Melbourne,
ran the 8. Aus. Government on his lonely own
for two weeks without turning a hair. Probably
his only regret was that he was not allowed to
draw the four Ministerial salaries during the
period.
“ Croson ” : The latest proposal of the Fat
papers is that Customs Minister Chapman shall
withdraw his 2001 b. bag proclamation, and that
the States shall then restrict the use of heavier
bag by railway regulations. But if the State
insects are honest in their professions anent
the heavy bags, there is nothing whatever
to prevent them making such railway regulations
forthwith. And they don't. They say the regula-
tion of the size of wheat bags is their business,
but they don’t attend to their alleged business.
The first case dealt with in Victoria under
Bent’s Closer Settlement Act was that of the
Konongwootong estate, and the result is that
Thomas has to pay all the law costs and
£100,330 2s. sd. The Court laid stress on the
fivepence. In return he gets 10,000 acres of land.
He can settle, say, 20 families on this at a price
that would nearly pay for a railway accident.
No wonder Swinburne is looking round for
another way of dodging the inevitable Land Tax.
This way is too slow and too small and too dear.
“H.S.R.” : I had an idea that the Aus-
tralian capital beside the Snowy River might be
called Megalopolis, or the Great City, for it would
have a chance to be great on that site. But if the
Little Australians have their way, and build a dry
and hopeless hamlet at Canberra, I suggest as a
name Mieropolis, or the Little City. Let us end
the shindy by making the Northern Territory a
J ederal State, and let us have the capital there.
Then there will be room for expansion, and the
metropolis of a continent will be out of the reach
of halfpenny State jealousies.
According to the S. M. Herald, N.S.W.
Premier Wade has announced that there is no
objection to the Commonwealth advertising Aus-
tralia. The difficulty is to decide where adver-
tising ends and encroachment upon “our inter-
national policy begins. Hooray ! But, who the
deuce is Mr. Wade, and what nation does he
represent, and what use has he for an international
policy any more than a serpent has for a plug hat?
A cry from the shore :
The aldermen of Vaucluse (Sydney) have alighted with
all their feet on the sellers of ice cream, peanuts, apples
buns, gingerbeer, and lollypops, who come down from
Sydney by the Watson’s Bay boat and sell their wares to
the detriment and discouragement of the Watson’s Bay
shopkeepers. Protection to home industry is their motto
and they have booted out the importer by municipal by-
law. Yet when it comes to protecting the industries of
Australia from outside competition, Vaucluse goes solid
for Willie Kelly, Foreign-trader. It can understand Pro-
tection for Vaucluse, but Protection for Australia it
doesn’t comprehend worth a cent.

When the threatened reconstruction of
Victoria’s Cabinet happens, it isn’t impossible
that the new team will omit Bent himself. The
Brighton bellower has been making some extra-
sized blunders of late, and Swinburne and one or
two others have shown signs of restlessness.
Thomas’ latest break was the announcement that
he had appointed the genial Croker as “counsel
for the public,” to assist the Commission of In-
quiry into the Sunshine Railway smash. Bent
similarly appointed Croker to assist the Butter
Commission, and when the favored lawyer’s bill
came in—for £sooo—Thomas had to do a lot of
earnest explaining. This fearsome account was
subsequently reduced to £4OOO, which the public
paid. Now, it remembers the bill and begins to
ask what the country pays its own law officers for.
Premier Kidston, of Queensland, is paying
back the Courier in its own coin. Before the
elections the morning paper—confident of the
defeat of Kidston and the ascendancy of Philp—
almost ignored not only Kidston’s candidates, but
Kidston himself. Now the politician is giving
the paper an anxious time by seeing, accidentally,
that all important official information is supplied
to the Courier via the news columns of the rival
Daily Mail. T’other day the Courier printed a
sensational charge of repudiation against the
Government, and then waited breathlessly for
Kidston’s explanation. None was offered. The
paper editor wired to Kidston for information.
The answer explained everything—but it wasn’t
wholly satisfactory to the Courier. The cynical
Premier wired his information to the Mail, and
the Courier didn’t see it till next morning’s issue
of the “reptile-contemporary.”
Twofold Bay, the outlet of the Australian
capital at Dalgety, is fast becoming a live port.
The Melbourne Steamship Co. has announced that
its big inter-State vessels will call there fort-
nightly in future, with the chance of a more
frequent service later on, and the boats of the
Union S.S. Co. of Maoriland also look in.
The papers have lately been full of a
scheme which is to end in the establishment on the
N.S.W. coast line of some thousands of Scotch
fishing folk, who are to transform the local fish
supply, and to start canning and exporting fish
on a big scale. Obvious difficulties obtrude them-
selves, but the Sydney papers profess to regard the
thing as settled. Many things are openly quoted
as the cause of the coming invasion. The
N.S.W. climate, the convenient coastline, Frank
Farnell’s politeness, and other like reasons are
given. The real cause (if the industry ever is
established) hasn’t been even mentioned. If the
despised Deakin Government had not offered a
bounty on canned fish, the Scotch persons would
probably never have been heard of.
The second latest by-election in England
was at the manufacturing town of Wolverhampton
Two years ago the Liberal and ‘ ‘ Freetrade” majority
at Wolverhampton was 2865, and the same old
Freetrader held the seat for 28 years. This time
the Protectionist was only beaten by eight votes.
The London Daily News, which is strongly Free-
trade, says with tears that there is no explanation
of the downfall, “save that tariff reform is a
growing force.” There have been over 30 by-
elections since the present Parliament was created
and in every case save one the result has been
either a victory for tariff reform, or an awkward
scrambling home of the Freetrader by a
sadly diminished majority. The Wolverhampton
majority of eight in a poll of 9020 is a very poor
scramble home. Still Winston Churchill has got
in by a split vote at Dundee; he got 7079 votes
out of 16,208 polled. Two years ago the Free-
traders could collect 9276 votes at Dundee.
SANCTUARY!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas midnight at the Sydney G.P.0.,
The clock-face said the hour was One-past One,
And yet the chimes
Rang forth twelve times
And gave the dial the lie direct! And so
Those chimes (alas those chimes !) have ever done !
There is no other earthly timepiece, none,
W> lose dial is always marking One-past One !
A lurching stranger staggered up the steps
And lay there in the lengthy colonnade ;
Eftsoons to snore 1
He’d taken more
Than cocoa made by Cadbury or Epps
A passing bobby saw him in the shade.
And, after due investigation made,
Awoke the man, who started this tirade:
“ Rash minion of the grim, parochial Wade
Wherefore disturbest thou my slumber snory !
Upon my word
Thou art absurd !
A truly monstrous error thou hast made !
’Twill make indeed a very pretty story !
Seek thou, State myrmidon, elsewhere for glory?
This colonnade is Federal territory ! ”
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE
MORE TROUBLE DOWNSTAIRS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Premiers’ Conference, “the Premiers were
earnestly discussing the momentous question of ‘channels
of communication’ with the British Government, and the
urgent necessity of State Governors, instead of the
Governor-General, being authorised to exercise the
prerogative of recommending fit and proper subjects for
titular distinctions.”— Age, 5/5/’OS.
What do we lack in nationhood ? What dignity not ours
Is yet within our grasp if we but use our native powers ?
And They that sit around the State’s deliberative oak
Where all the bloated inkpots are—these staid and
solemn folk
Who scribble on pink blotting-pads—they deal in crimson
speech
Because— because the knighthood-gaud, is ravished out of
reach !
This is the great State-masters’ moan ;
For this, in turn, they grouch and groan !
Now, wherefore should they dwell upon the Crawl before
the Throne?
Why now, amid the issues of this nation-making time,
Do these, our men of State, put forth their eager hands
to climb
To higher planes before they pass, dead gods, beyond the
pale ?
Ah, this is where we common men of simple methods fail
To know the utter need there is that we should use our
“rights ”
To dignify the nation with another bunch of knights.
This is the time, undoubtedly,
When we our leaders’ worth should see—
Now, when they’d bow the knee to bring their States more
dignity!
A thousand noble men are here to help Australia on,
But (this i* in a whisper) where are all the knighthoods
gone l
The knowledge of our leaders’ deeds is fresh within our
hearts,
And they should get their ticketing as men of many
parts;
So may they look ahead with hope to that entrancing
time
When they will reap the grand rewards they earned while
in their prime.
So may each man of State grow fat
Beneath an ornamental hat,
And sport his splendid presence at bazaars, and things
like that 1
VIC.
FRANK HENTY.

Beyond all, the advantage of the Artists' Music
Rolls gives the Claviola a distinct artistic character,
and removes the objections that have hitherto been
raised against the ordinary piano-players.

THE COUPON SYSTEM.
CHAPTER I.
pHE credit system for years weighed
heavily on all Householders, chiefly on
people with small means and women in
particular whose duty it was to make the
weekly wage or the weekly salary go as far as possible. Honest
people who paid their bills at the end of the month had always two
additions to reckon on —first, the storekeeper’s charge for giving them credit,
that is, for being kept out of his money for a month ; and secondly, the
“ squaring ” charges which all business men have to make to save themselves
from the losses they make through dishonest or unsuccessful persons who do
not pay at all, and everyone who thinks for a moment will see that in acredft
business it is absolutely necessary for the .Storekeeper to take these two
elements into consideration in fixing his charges, and so it is that the honest
public who do their business on a credit basis have to pay more for their
goods, not because the goods are worth more, but because, unless the Store-
keeper increases his charges to provide for these two items, he would soon
find himself on the wrong side of the ledger. And then again there is a
third objection to the credit system, viz.—the possibility of mistakes in
charging for goods already paid for, and which mistakes might occur in the
best-managed business. Add to this the fact that the Storekeeper who did
a credit business would not have as much cash as the man who did a cash
one, and would not therefore be able himself to buy from the wholesaler on
such good terms as the Storekeeper who had the cash, and it will be seen at
once what the credit customer had to contend against, and how much more
he or she, principally she, had to pay, than would be the case if everyone did
a cash business.
It then became necessary to look round for a system which would give
the man who paid cash some inducement to do so, and so save him from
having to help to make up to the .Storekeeper for his bad marks. The
Coupon System does this, and we shall tell you more about it in our
next chapter.

FACILE PRINCEPS
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 12, 1908.
My Dear Arint,—
When contradictory reports, mostly untrue,
and some of them quite uninteresting and all
crossing each other like weather currents, begin to
be circulated about a person, the significance is
that the person is of importance. Miss Carrie
Moore is feeling hot about the collar over the
insistence of interviewers who ask her if the man
to whom she’s engaged cameout herewith her. She
is getting tired of the question. He didn’t come, he
isn’t coming, and she doesn’t know exactly when
she’s going to be married. Her Australian thea-
trical engagement, as it stands at present, is for
twenty-six, or so, weeks. Then she goes back to
her London work ; but from sisters Eva, Lily,
Bobbie and Olive a second Moore gemus may be
selected to be let loose on the Commonwealth.
Carrie has an ingenious Birmingham toy that is
sprung on her callers. It is a little box in which
is hidden a tiny gold bird. The pressing of a
spring causes the bird to fly out and chirp most
melodiously for a minute. Mr. Frank Gardner,
on his late visit here, used to show a like idea,
only his boxed bird was once a Papal treasure —a
fearsomely valuable thing of wrought and
jewelled gold. The box had also been a Papal
treasure. The production by Carrie of her metal
warbler is always made, she explains, when the
interviewer for the Daily Alligator gets too
pressingly personal, and asks how He parts his
hair, and which of the Kohinoors on the third
finger of her left hand is the engagement loop.
Talking of mummers. A betitled physician in
London has been commenting on the fine health
of actors and of the female of their species. Their
complexions are also a theme of admiration,
and they never look old. He says he thinks
he’ll recommend some of his patients to go in for
the stage cure. And yet the outsider thinks that
by all the laws of everything the actor and ’ess
ought to be below the normal in health, by reason
of the conditions under which they work. Their
dressing-rooms are poisonous little cubicles, un-
ventilated, and over-lighted (as must be for the
necessities of make-up); the stage on which they
fume nightly is atmospherically vitiated, or they
are exposed to draughts in extremely ventilated
costumes, and to sudden weather changes.
Further, their everyday mental processes,
the outsider thinks, are mainly composed
of envy, hatred, and ever present fear of
the coming rival. And everyone knows how in-
dulgence in these passions gives one a lowering
brow and a moutji of scorn, like King Tarquin’s,
when he stalked in the Forum with the twelve
tomahawks waiting on him. The actor who can
keep the corners of his mouth curled upward,
after reading in the great daily the first-night
notice that he, Mr. Wyndham Sawkins, “ap-
peared as Romeo, but that the subject is
too painful for further mention,” must
(the outsider thinks) have a heart that
plays like a brass band. Especially if
the Mercutio of the production be praised in
terms past all whooping. Another delusion of
the outsider is that the actress (leading women of
the species understood) nightly joins a gay supper-
party after eleven with a rabble of men, and pro-
longs her supping until the lark at Heaven’s gate
sings, and that she gets home to her couch what
time Phoebus begins to fall out of bed and bump
his head against the floor of another day.
Actor and actress have pretty good consti-
tutions to begin with, or they make no headway
in the profession. And if they “gave in” to
passing ailments—to influenza, “blues,” rheuma-
tism, neuralgia, and overdoses of crayfish, as
members of other professions would—their ex-
tremity would be the understudy’s opportunity.
Horrible thought ! Consciously or otherwise, they
are Christian Scientists. They sandbag them-
selves, if necessary, into the belief that they are
at the top of their physical condition. They get
the habit of good health, and travel and frequent
change of scene count in their favor.
Miss Ola Humphry is the most marked case in
point your “ Akenehi ” has met. She suggests the
possession of a skylark for a heart. Under
adverse conditions—as when playing an exhaust-
ing rdle in weather that would try a lorrv driver
and which prevented her eating and sleeping’
when chased by hostile comment, or haunted by
delayed gowns, or piles of correspondence that
came at the same moment as thoughtless callers,
or absorbed in the studying of a new part when
her.fragile, small-boned frame must have been on
the verge of collapse, she still seemed as gay as a
skylark. She would not give in to sickness, or
worry or weather, and presently those drawbacks
-gJve into her will-power, and she no longer had
to act the thistle-down buoyancy part. She was it.
As for the late suppering. The average actress
and singer of any standing loathes it. Instead of
going to her hotel with the rabble of men at her
heels, she hurries there with her maid, laps a lot of
hot milk or soup, and scamps her prayers in her
haste to get to bed and to sleep.
Miss Patricia Mort, who will be the first known
Australian to become a foreign baroness, is staying
at Naples with her baron’s people.
Mrs. Margaret Thomas Gordon has lost some-
thing in a watery grave. Her Pa had shipped to
her a grand piano. It was on the Star of Japan,
but the luminary was wrecked, and sank, taking
the music with it. However, Pa is sending
another to Mrs. Big Eyes.
The Man of the Moment in N.S.W. during the
past week-all assertions to the contrary in * ‘ The
Lady Dandies ” notwithstanding—has been Prime
Minister Deakin’s athletically capable youn<*
nephew, N. E. Brookes. He is other things
besides champion lawn-tennis player of the world.
For instance, he is as good a billiardist as the
average professional. At the Australian Club
where he abides when in Sydney, it is not unusual
tor him to register a couple of century
breaks m a game of “500 up.” Also lie
worthily represents Victoria at the strenuous game
of golf. And finally prime social consideration
—he has a generous private income. Can the
local maiden be blamed for loading herself with
the gladdest chiffons or furs (as the weather may
be) in her ammunition wardrobe, and discharging
the result at so alluring a matrimonial target. It
has been said of Mr. Brookes, as it waft of
Henry 1., that he never smiled again. But that
statement only applies to his attitude while play-
ing tennis. He has quite a pleasant smile at
other times. It is also alleged that, even in the
hottest weather and the swiftest tennis game, he
never perspires. When I heard this I rang up a
doctor of my acquaintance to ask if a man who
never got hot was quite human, and if he wasn’t
really a vampire from Dalmatia, but the reply
allayed my fears.
Mr. Willie Watt, who died of malaria on board
the Himalaya last week, was one of Sydney’s
most important young Society men. The owner
of good looks and a large income, it seemed at one
time that Fortune had a permanently favorable
eye on him. He was never quite well again,
however, after the East African illness he con-
tracted 18 months ago, and to the very last was
hesitating as to whether or no he was fit enough
to start for cold climes. The damp heat of Cey-
lon’s “spicy breezes” aggravated his old trouble.
His brothers, Mr. Ernest and Mr. Oswald Walt,
are both in England just now ; and a married
sister is also in those parts.
Miss Khadijah Cooper, the girl whose first name
suggests the tinkling of a camel’s bell, is preparing
for a trip to New York, leaving on June 8.
Mr. Sam Hordern, senior, is said to be wander-
ing in the Garden of Allah —that is, if Egypt be
part of the Garden of Allah. The point of his
absence is that, through it, he missed seeing his
new Bronzewing swim out of the air into the
water last week. Bronzewing 11. lies at a ship-
builder’s pier at Balmain, getting its final silver
point etchings, so to speak. The baptism
of the sea-bird was achieved by a small
Hordern girl—the owner’s grandchild. It is
110 ft. long—the boat, not the grandchild—
and, on the word of its architect, Mr. Walter
Reeks, is the largest oil motor boat in the world.
The ceilings are white and gold, and the uphol-
stery salt-bush green, a most artistic tone. Aus-
tralian flowers are wrought in the oaken carvings,
and there are arc lights, and telephones, and a
huge ice-chest, and a powerful searchlight. The
saloon is over 20ft. long, and nothing in the way
of anchors, and chocks, and davits, and coils, of
rope, and funnels, is missing. The stylish bird
cost some £BOOO.
EVA CHAMPION , a promising Contralto.
Mr. Buckingham, the manager of Kubelik’s
tour, looks a medium-sized edition of Shakspeare.
His star fiddler’s young life, from the time he left
his wooden cradle up to, May, 1908, has been raked
through and through and crosswise by a thousand
inquisitorial pens, so naturally the manager with
the great name and the small pointed beard hasn’t
much of what might be called exclusive informa-
tion. Young Kub’s first engagement, in Vienna,
after his father died, brought him four guineas,
and of this sum he sent three to his
mother. Since then, he has settled £lOOO a year
on her. He is a great cat-lover, and didn’t some-
one say that the boy who is kind to his mother
and the cat will make a good husband. Mrs.
Kubelik (she drops her title and calls herself
Madame) is quite satisfied with her Jan.
As for Manager Buckingham’s own observa-
tions in this country, two things struck him with
the force of a brick travelling rapidly—the high
standard of physical beauty attained by the Aus-
tralian women and the seriousness and thorough-
ness with which the Australian reads his papers.
The Englishman reads the summary of one (or
more) papers, then throws it aside. Here, he
reckons, we know our daily and weekly press from
soup to nuts.
Marie Narelle leaves in a few days on a joyous
song tour of the northern rivers, commencing at
Raymond Terrace. The tour route is off all rail-
way lines, so the singer has bought a coach and
four spanking steeds, and borrowed a brother-in-
law, who can drive anything that neighs, to
dominate the box-seat. The coach party will
appear at a fresh township every night; and if
only there was room for passengers, what a rush
of applications would occur !
Max Schliiter left for Melbourne last night, after
a successful trio of concerts. He will make it six
after Kubelik has poured his rain of melody over
Sydney.
Next Friday Miss Ella Crosse, who is making
elocutionary fame in this State, will give a
dramatic and humorous recital at St. James’ Hall.
She will be assisted by Misses Ernestine Louat
and Lilian Frost, also Mr. Cyril Monk and Mr.
Lawrence Campbell.
Mr. and Mrs. T. J. West, of the wonderful
West’s Picture Films, are off on Saturday for a
round-the-world jaunt, and, incidentally, to catch
more moving pictures.

1 Bay Hackney MARE.
Personally I hope the bay colt will be elected.
Municipal balls and functions are, as a rule, dreary
things in small towns, and the election of a horse
as mayor might break the dreadful monotony.
A romantic-looking captain in the Coldcream
Guards (or should it be Coldstream Guards ?), and
the romantic port of Ceylon, and an unusually
pretty girl with a voice to match —a romantic
bunch of persons and place ! He knew and ad-
mired her in Sydney, and when she and the
party with which she travelled got to Colombo,
he came down a long, long way from the fast-
nesses of his rubber plantation to go on board and
say adieu, and then later to wave his hat and
stick from the small boat manned by the pic-
turesque nigger as the liner wore to sea again. He
went on board, but he didn’t do any foolish waving
later on. He bought some shop suits, drew some
money from a bank, and went on in the vessel
with the Sydneysiders. And now the girl’s
friends are asking will she cultivate that voice or
will she accept the usual marine product.
On a day last week something in the nature of a
portent or omen was to be seen. It was a respect-
able, middle-aged, married woman, and she sat
calmly in the Winter Garden of the Hotel
Australia, smoking a cigarette. I conceive that,
had Mr. Judkins entered in quest of a “straight
water,” or “hot milk shake,” or “small bottle of
plain soda,” or other refreshment of the kind, and
seen her, he would have fled thirsty, frothing at
the mouth, and from the handiest stump have
delivered an oration proving to demonstration
that ruin came upon the pubs of the Plains, and
on the frequenters thereof, and on the Cities
which harbored them, owing to just this sort of
wickedness. However, fortunately, Mr. Judkins
remained without and at peace. And instead the
fond gaze of approval was directed at that
gallant pioneer female by many a secret smoker
of her sex who lacked the nerve, hide,
cold-steel cheek, or whatnot to do like-
wise. Scores of women partake of the harmless
cigarette in the home circle these days. More-
over, the husband of judgment mostly approves ;
it acts as a combined nerve-soother and scandal-
prophylactic. To one who has grown used to a
cigarette after a meal it is a plaguey, uncom-
fortable matter to forego it. Throughout Europe
nowadays ladies —as opposed to lydies—light up
readily in all the great hotels. And a growing
feminine section here doesn’t care how soon the
convention spreads to local caravansaries. The
old mid-Victorian idea of naughtiness and
nicotine being indissolubly wedded, is deatb-
rattling noisily on all sides, so the day of emanci-
pation in this regard should not be far distant.
Wandering Australian girls who have brought
out dream-frocks in their carpet-bags for the envy
of the stay-at-homes, tell what look like fabulous
stories about the size of the bill. But in most
cases the prices really haven’t grown at all on the
voyage. Paquin’s and other dream-gown factories
have to carry such a tremendous lot of money on
their books, and they have to carry it for such ages,
that the customer has to pay a pound for material
and about three pounds for interest. It takes
such a mint of money to run one of these
businesses nowadays that when the Paquins
started in London 10 years ago they insisted on
the new co. having a whole half-million capital;
and though tens of thousands of pounds are
scratched out every year as bad debts, the con-
cern still had £273,000 of unpaid bills out when
it made up its books a few weeks ago.
Still, don’t go and waste sympathy on
Paquin; for, in spite of the unpaid bills, he
managed to pay a 20 per cent, dividend for last
year —and a man who has an uncle that knows
about dividends and things says that, while
Paquin gets 20 per cent., he ought at least to be
able to pay his laundry lady something on account.
Talking about the laundry lady, the London
Laundry Record (yes, there is such a paper,
though the Chimney Sweep’s Chronicle hasn’t
been actually published yet) announces that “ the
first Chinese laundry established in London to
accommodate the Englishman ” has just hung out
its dragon “in Manor Park, Ilford”; so J. Bull
has a chance now to get his boiled shirt sent home
in a paper marked with characters that look like
horned caterpillars writhing in great agony.
The Montefiore Home Annual Ball occurs in
Sydney Town Hall on Tuesday, June 9. This
frisk is in aid of a worthy charity, besides which
it is one of the occasions on which the Town Hall
“presents a most brilliant appearance.” The
Home’s supporters frock themselves splendidly.
Hon. treasurers are Mrs. Nathan Jacobs and Mrs.
A. Diamond. Hon. secs., Mrs. L. R. Wilson and
Mrs. Victor Wilson.
I have it from one or two who have come in con-
tact with the shipment of English domestics that
those special importations are keenly disappointed
in their Australian employers. The trouble is
that the local families are distinctly “no class,”
and it is a grievous hurt to the pride of the British
menial to be in the service of common people who
do not say “ Haw ! ” and who have no connections
whatever with the first cousin of an earl. The
absence of “ dog ” in the Australian home is felt
with a keenness that can only be understood by the
Australian who has put up at a cheap Cockney
boarding-house in the Cold Country, and wit-
nessed the painful efforts of the 305.-a-week clerk-
lets to live up to aristocratic ideals as presented to
them in the Society papers. “ These people go to
the theatres, and sit to dinner in morning dress !”
shrilled one disillusioned domestic. “Why, my
brother wouldn’t do that! ” And then the
harried kitchen demoiselle broke down and wept
bitterly into the dust-pan,
A correspondent from M.L. writes about a day
of muggy heat—the weather that mushrooms and
such-like fungoid growths flourish in, but under
which the normal woman wilts and demands why
the Almighty has fixed his canon against self-
slaughter. The correspondent met a lady friend
and her little girl, and openly admired the pretti-
ness of the babe. “ Oh, no.” declared the child’s
mother, “ Peggy is not a bit pretty, really, but a
day like this does wonders for her. It curls her
hair, brightens her eyes, and gives a pearly texture
to her skin. When she grows up I intend to take
her to some climate that is always like this, and
so give her every chance to make a good marriage ”
There’s a fair emigrant maturing for a land of
warmth and everlasting mildew—a land like
(Jeylon’s perspiring isle.
“ Mario,” from Brisbane :
Much success dangled round the annual ball of the
Commercial Kowing Club on Tuesday evening, the sth
when no less than 15 duffers burst forth in goodly
raiment. Their Ex.’s arrived early and stayed late and
the aforementioned little bits of fluff were duly yarded ud
after the first dance and presented by Captain Vernev of
the twinkl.y eye. Some of them shuffled through the
ordeal, and some of them floundered. One alone got
through with absolute grace, and she was a Miss Greenup
a real “ daughter of the gods.” Bonnie Yda Buchanan
Hilda Ohmau(a real “ Kitty Asche”), and Moya Cuppaidge
were neck and neck as runners-up.
Invitations are out for a lawn party at Government
House on the 27th. In the meantime the pale but reso-
lute viceroy is “ visiting round ” among the stations, and
will probably be the guest of the Tamrookun Collins for
the Beaudesert show. Speaking of these representatives
of Ned VII., there are dark rumors in the air to the effect
that, after our next August show, the august lady will
pack up her traps and shake the Queensland dust off
what she stands upon. Ah ! well! Life is mostly parting.
We shall miss the practical commonsense and kindly
interest of Her Who Putteth Down Her Foot.
A great gathering there was entirely on Saturday last
the day set apart for the official opening of the New Farm
Bowling Green, just completed. The air was heavy with
Jerusalem. There was lashings of it, each with its nose
and its accent bigger than the last. The speech, the
applause, and the very heavy afternoon meal were right
up to standard ; but when the band let out on the solo
“ Jerusale'm ’’—well, it was too much for the few strayinv
Gentiles, and they fled for home.
The wedding of the month was that of pretty Miss
Hickson (a niece, by the way, of that Agr lady, Henrietta
McGowan) and Mr. Flower, of Warwick, which whirled in
a mist of orange flowers and rice, on Saturday last.
The moon is honey somewhere near Katoomba (N.S.W.).

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 11, 1908,
My Dear Yarrik,—
Siege and battle and clamorous strife have
raged at the Miss Sutherland Home. A rather
recent Catmittee decided to elect a new matron in
Miss Sutherland’s place. Miss Sutherland refused
to be superseded. The committee thinned out
because of resigning and exploding members ; the
remaining Cats went doggedly on, and eventually
elected a new matron and a new nurse, and
went to instal them personally. Since then
tnere have been installations and ejections
and ructions generally, and the kids have
been having a good deal of amusement.
Matters were fixed, or supposed to be. Then
they were reiixed. After that they were unfixed.
According to the latest report, some action done or
proposed by the new matron caused Miss Suther-
land to take umbrage. That wouldn’t have
mattered so very much, but she also took the
front door key. The latest arrangement was that
Miss Sutherland was to give up her old billet and
take charge of the receiving house in Latrobe-
street, anti the latest disarrangement is that the
strenuous lady is now in charge of both places.
The Catmittee apparently couldn’t manoeuvre, or
reconnoitre, or form a hollow square worth a
copper (in fact it seems unfit even for sentry
duty), and it was outflanked in an absurd manner,
and its agent is said to have found herself in the
street after midnight, with a very vague idea what
to do next, and a very clear idea that the last
tram had gone.
The trouble is that Miss Sutherland began and
wmt on and prospered without the help of a com-
mittee. Then the lately established “Board” of
women who “sat” on her doings rather ruffled
matters. Miss Sutherland was inclined to
ignore the committee. Naturally, the com-
mittee objected to being ignored. Miss
Sutherland is given to abrupt speech and is
wont to strike straight out from the shoulder —
and they are mannish shoulders, holding
a mannish head. But her heart is all woman,
and any baby will go right to her. Babies
that no one else would take, or cquld take
—poor little strays —always were packed off to Miss
Sutherland, and were never refused, though want
of space and help has meant her having half-a-
dozen wailing waifs sleeping in her own till
the day found some place for them. Now Miss
Sutherland is growing pld, and some of her
methods are old—so some think she should be
pushed to the wall, and roughly, clumsily, at the
point of the Claw. Yet she is one of the biggest-
hearted and most unselfish workers in Melbourne.
There is a boom in brides in Melbourne, hive
Toorakities have just returned from a Mediow
moon. Imagine five worshipping the same moon
on the same acre at the same time ! One youthful
newly-wed was ill all her sweet moon with an
abscess on her little face—yet she chortles
delightedly'at the elevated status she thus gained
in “fuss” and notice over the four every-day
land of brides. A dubious joy—but, mebbe,
abscess makes the heart grow fonder.
A solid wedge of a big grown-up family de-
parted from Toorak with the last crusade of Fog
worshippers, and last week a youthful member of
the clan celebrated his position as vice-Head of
the Family by sending out pasteboards for an all-
embracing dance frivol at the ancestral halls. It
was a Ball in a Thousand, and showed
masterly management. The chief decoration
in the ballroom was a huge map of the
house and grounds, with judicious nota-
tion for the guidance of dancers and sitters-
out. “ Shrubbery to the left of the lawn
path—room for six carefully assorted”; "Tool-
house, mind the lawn mower—key hangs on
left-hand corner” ; “ Rosery, standing room only
—voices may be overheard in shrubbery ” ; “ Cosy
seat under the big chestnut—may be seen from
balcony at angle of 32£ degrees ’’—that was the
kind of chart which possibly saved some girly
Columbus and her first mate from striking a reef
on the sea of love, or at least fluff. The event
slipped as smoothly as a toboggan down a
precipice, and was marred with but one blot —a
furious breach-of-guidance action against the host
by two doves whose cosy corner was pointed out
vividly by the fierce acetylene eye of a belated
motor whirring up the drive.
In uttering a plaint apropos the lobbyists
who rush up to him, and deaden his ears with the
loud voice that shows the excited mind, a member
of the House of Reps, voices a saving clause. He
mentions the Ladies of the Lobby—the encyclo-
paedic dames who battle on either side in the
War of Importers and Manufacturers. He quotes
them as notable gauges of the Australian woman’s
interest in her nation’s destiny, and the suita-
bility of the said woman to her lately gained re-
sponsibilities. One lady, in the cause of Protec-
tion, has never missed ten minutes of the
tariff debate, and is said to have carried
a dozen amendments to date. On tariff-
showing, it can be prophesied that Aus-
tralia is going to supply history with a new type
—the woman who abdicates not an inch of her
hereditary territory, the kitchen and the nursery,
yet takes her place amongst the rulers of a
continent, and guards the future of it with the
far-reaching instinct of a generous mother.
Amongst the tariff jokes perpetrated by wag-
gish Mr. Chapman is one which reflects upon
that acute person’s discretion, and relates to the
doll who shares infancy’s devotion with infancy’s
progenitors. Mr. Chapman says—with an air of
immutable decision like unto that of the young
man who bets on forgotten cricket-scores —that
dolls who wear stockings painted on their sawdust
legs are dressed. The modest dollie who wears
a little shirt over her futile little trunk is
catalogued as undressed, and gets through with a
lesser duty—and her shirt. Sisters mine, we must
reorganise our ideas! Paint on stockings, and
we could pass the Customs, with our reputations
for respectability and Mr. Chapman’s approval.
Wear a shroud over our dear contours, and we’ll
be treated as though Mr. Chapman saw through
it.
Fred McCubbin gives a show of his pictures at
the Grand Hotel somewhere in the middle of this
charming month of May. Mrs. Fred gives a
reception at the opening of the show. The whole
family will be able to hold festival after it, for
many of the paintings will be as eagerly snatched
up as unique dress-lengths at reduced prices.
“Mac” has been most subtly affected by his
European jaunt, and since his return has done the
best work of his life.
Nowadays that suave-voiced hero of a hundred
and fifty melodramas, Walter Baker, is a retired
actor. No more will he have the X-rays_ of
dramatic sequence thrown upon his theatrical
character. He has resolved himself into a man of
business, who takes a live interest in bulls and
bears and various other Apocryphal animals. And
he is restored to a state of 13-stone health.
I hear of Ruby Baxter, a delightful little lady
who once hid her light behind a bushel of J. C. W.
choristers. She is beginning to make an English
reputation as a clever character actress, an estab-
lished quantity, and is booked for the next year with
various managements. Already she has had the
distinction of being borrowed by an author for
the provincial tour of his latest success.
The Commonwealth has just issued an eighteen-
page report furnished to it by the gifted Dr.
Dunbar Hooper. It treats of infantile mortality,
and particularly of the decisions of the Inter-
national Congress upon the protection of child-
life, held at Brussels last September. The fact
that 11,627 children under the age of five
died during 1906 in Australia is an eminent
justification for the publication. _ The de-
voted Hooper describes the institutions
already existent on the European continent,
which tend the babes of the poor, and train
mothers for the earthly salvation of their off-
spring. (Health is our earthly paradise, without
doubt.) He details how pure milk is distributed ;
how mothers are given every adventitious aid in
the rearing of their infants; how even school-
girls are instructed in child-rearing by provident
optimists‘bent on humanity’s renaissance. Inci-
dentally he estimates that more than a third of
Australia’s infant mortality is preventable. It is
a document which should be printed in the shape
of a mothers’ manual,' and as widely distributed
as an enterprising tradesman’s circular. One wise
provision made in Europe is the award of sub-
stantial premiums to wet-nurses and foster-
mothers. The State in Belgium, for instance,
assumes the major part of the responsibility
for the life of every embryo citizen. The
whole report might make a text for Aus-
tralian legislation on this question. Certainly it
offers a biting reproach to the casualness of local
measures for benefiting the baby.
“A certain man,” in the Bible, at one time
raked the lanes and hedges for “the lame and the
halt and the blind,” and hauled them in to a
feast. In like wise Williamson sent out scouts to
the highways and byways, to the mayors and
councillors of poor suburbs, and to Charity Boards
and reformatory schools, and rounded up the
children, the poor waifs of the city, and gave
over to them the Princess’s, with its “ Peter
Pan,” and its Pirates, and all the other de-
lights, for a Saturday matinee. There was a
2000 child-power appreciation that would have
warmed Barrie’s heart. The play was more real
than reality to that house. Boys barracked for
the Lost Boys against the Pirates, and yelled en-
couragement to little Michael. Anxious voices
called “ Look out, Pete ! ’’ to sleeping Peter with
Captain Hook creeping stealthily upon him.
The saddest group was that of the blind
children. They knew the story, and sat still
through any pantomime, but greeted sounds with
delight, and almost came to blows in discussion
with each other. Some nurses sat in the orchestra
stalls, with bandaged babies—bound up in splints,
with nothing but hands and head free. Their free
hands clapped their belief in Fairies when Tinker
Bell was dying. There was not a doubt as to their
ideas of Fairy doings.
The Empire claimed another victim last week—
,lohn Malcolm Borthwick, son of an old Gipps-
lander, Colonel Borthwick, of the 10th Light
Horse. Young Borthwick was a bright lad, and
worked hard to obtain a commission in the British
Army, which he secured at the age of 20. A
paternal British Government sent him to Aden,
where there ain’t no Ten Commandments, and
where the fever-germs sit on the piers awaiting
new-comers. The Gippsland boy proved an easy
prey, and a month after his landing they buried
him.
Miss Emma Sugden, a youthful daughter of the
jolly Master of Queen’s at our University, is
engaged to Mr. Le Souef for a life contract.
Young Le Souef has taken this year’s Rhodes
scholarship. He is the son of the Le Souef who
is guardian archangel at the Zoo, where he acts as
uncle to the lion, and gives good advice to the
perplexed rhinoceros. Miss Emma inherits her
father’s musical bump, and does rather clever
things with her violin at the Conservatorium.
“The Scarlet Troubadours ” continue their tour
of the States in Melbourne on the 19th. Sounds
warm and vivid for this cold, grey weather. Mr.
Edward Branscombe, who ran the Westminster
Glee Company, is responsible for the Gaudy
Singers.
A gadding Australian writes:—
The Rupertswood Dame has been dazzling the crowds
at Monte Carlo just lately. She is back again in the vast
Fog, frisking while the spring cleaners chastise the cock-
roach and the moth that consumes at Park Lane.
Captain Staughton’s young widow is in the Bull capital
with a swagger crowd at Hyde Park Hotel, and prepares
her wedding ruffles and things in a delighted sort of
flurry. These widows can look so naive and debutantish
when facing the rails for a second run over the matri-
monial course. She is to marry an Armitage, of Como, a
strong- force in the social and squatting world of Victoria.
The Jack Alston Wallace couple are touring Europe
with ever-accumulating band-boxes and boot-trunks,
for the large, showy wife likes the Rue de Rivoli
label on her hat and the Piccadilly badge on ber theatre-
coat. And so the lengthy spouse has to follow the Man
from Cook’s with a Himalayan lot of luggage.
Beaumont Smith has just been attached as an
Andersonian secretary. It seems to be a fashion
for press-inclined men to enter a theatrical atmo-
sphere. Instance Darbyshire, of the Age, who
recently went over to Her Majesty’s (Melbourne),
and Claude McKay, of Sydney Telegraph, lately
annexed as Williamson’s secretary. Young Beau-
mont Smith is a budding dramatist. Anderson
has accepted a play of his (top-notch bellowdrama),
and will produce it at the New Theatre. He has
also dramatised Steele Rudd’s “On Our Selection,”
which MS. has got as far as a “favorable con-
sideration ” from Williamson.
Ernest Toy, a Bendigo youth, who has studied
“ abrawd,” and now tours his native playground,
gave two violin recitals at Glen’s Hall last week.
He is an accurate and careful player ; too careful
for warmth and vivid life —and his recitals won t
spoil Kubelik’s chance of a hearing. The concert
was a judicious sandwich of the Ernest boy and
Miss Hortense Fyfe—a solemn young lady who
warbled passionate Indian love songs with the
placidity of a gentle moo-cow.
Mrs. Walker, the plucky little woman whose
domestic finances collapsed so suddenly, is
buckling-to like a Trojan, helping the wobbly
beginner how to skate on slippery ice. She is a
dainty skater herself.
From “Rosna,” at Adelaide-in-the-influenza
State: —
His Ex. temporarily dropped the reins of office last
week, and, in company with Lady Le Hunte, Miss Clowes
and an aide, fled off'on the Rupara up our turbulent
Gulf. The Rupara is a goodly little craft, and this Gulf
trip is much patronised by the nobility generally. It
takes five days, and includes a glimpse of Port Lincoln
(which says it has the best natural harbor in the demi-
semi-hemisphere), Tumby Bay, Port Pirie, and variousother
exciting places that contain two jetties, one post-office
and a dog—especially a dog. The gubernatorial visit to
these cities was an unofficial one, so the local mayor was
discouraged from sounding the loud timbrel or flourishing
his little piece of red carpet in welcome.
Private Secretary Walter Stuart Baillie-Hamilton (no
less) came sailing back last week from a five-months’
holiday in India, where he has been pursuing elephants
and health. He brought a guest back to the Residence
with him, Captain Buchanan, of the severalth Regiment,
stationed in the Punjaub. King Edward keeps a military
force there to teach the gospel to the Hindu.
There has just passed out, at the age of 87, Mrs.
Turner, widow of the late Stipendi ry Magistrate F. F.
Turner. She was a charming and brilliant Frenchwoman,
and in her younger days a great Society dame. Her
cousin and life-long friend, Miss du Chesne, resided with
her until her death a few months ago, and the two went
everywhere together. Another recent death is that of
Miss Kingston, sister of Charles Cameron Kingston, the
statesman.
The Olympia Rink had a fancy dress and poster carnival
last week, and the populace wandered in and packed
itself sky-high to see the fun. It is wonderful how
many people seem to consider it an intellectual entertain-
ment to watch their brethren falling over each other on a
hard rock floor. Colored electric lights gave a festive air
to the rink, which is not ordinarily what you would call
decorative. The chief hilarity was caused by the antics
of a burlesque motor and its freight of lunatics ; also by a
dilapidated gentleman representing Rags and Bones, and
a white-gowned individual with a candle. He was labelled
“ Good-night.” Considerably over a hundred enthusiasts
slithered to the strains of the band, and nobody broke
three or four of his necks and limbs, though a medico tells
me the rinking fever has occasioned an epidemic of
sprains this year.
South Australia is somewhat pluming itself on captur-
ing several of the chief literary awards in the Woman’s
Work Exhibition. Mr. Barr-Smith’s £5O for a novel of
100,000 words goes to Mrs. Stone (Sydney Partridge), and
the second prize of £3O to Miss Mary Austin ; while the
whole £5 for a novel on temperance has been collected by
Mrs. H. A. Doudy, also of S.A. Mrs. Stone, by the way,
was responsible for the beauteous printing and get-up of
the Exhibition souvenir, presented to Lady Northeote.
There died in Adelaide last week, aged 90, Mr. O. B.
Fisher, one of S. A.’s earliest squatting and racing men.
He came to the province in 1836, in the Buffalo, which
carried our first Governor, Hindmarsh. And he rode at
our very first races, which were held in 1838, out in the
wilds of what is now Thebarton. Mr. Fisher was a son of
the late James Hurtle Fisher and brother of the late
Dame Morphett.
Poet E. J. Brady sauntered into Adelaide last week at
the tail end of his Lone Hand expedition, and with his
motor-boat safely stowed away in a carpet bag. He was
pursued by a long string of local ink wielders, and let fall
many pearls of wisdom on the ways of Murray Waters.
The Nurses’ ball is fixed for June 17 at the Town Hall.
Their Exes, will be present, and a strong committee is
hustling things to make the event as furiously successful
as usual. The Mayoral ball and the attendant kiddies’
spree is due to happen somewhere about a week later.
Hymen is working fairly hard in South Australia this
year. On April 25 he lit up his torch at Mount Lofty for
the knotting of Madge, daughter of the late ironmaster
,Fulton, and lawyer Charlie Edmunds ; and on the 29th,
at St. Andrews, Walkerville, he illuminated the binding
of the Barr-Smith’s daughter Jo and Mr. J. Hay Gosse.
Editor Langdon Bonython has bought H. R. Dixson’s
(now H. R. Denison’s) handsome home on the top of the
North Adelaide hill. It is the most conspicuous object
to catch the eyes of arrivals at the Adelaide railway
station, and was built out of the results of lucky gold
speculations in W.A. Poseidon was not H.R.’s only
profitable snap.
You never can tell. When the Fowell Buxtons
served out a scanty sandwich and tart at Adelaide
Guv’ment House a few years ago, nobody ever suspected
them of doing anything piore desperate than opening a
bazaar. Yet here comes news of one of them going out
in East Africa lately, and shooting three lions before
breakfast. Certainly it did prove too much for him, for
he didn’t manage to quite kill the last of the beasties —
probably he heard the sausages frying and was in a hurry
—whereupon a wounded lion interviewed him and chewed
his arm.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
Some people in S. Aus. are very much
annoyed with Chief Justice Way. The pompous
little man is torn by conflicting emotions, asR. D.
Meagher was when he got into the tram-car. On
one side he dearly loves a lord, and if there isn’t
a lord to love then he loves anything else with in-
fluence and wealth. On the other hand, he has a
pretty high regard for his duty as a Judge. Just
an ordinary man, on being lifted up to the Bench,
would go ahead and deal out justice, and it
wouldn’t occur to him whether the party in front
was rich or influential or otherwise. But it does
occur to C. J. Way; so, when a “first family”
rep. turns up, his Honor points out that he really
mustn’t be influenced by any outside con-
siderations, and mustn’t consider whether the
accused is influential, and mustn’t give a thought to
the tonnage of the prisoner’s family. He broke out
in the old familiar fashion, the other day, and thus
drew everybody’s attention to the young fool in
the box, who had written the most asinine letter
on record to a young woman (since dead—the
usual business). Of course there were scores who
had already connected the accused with one of the
biggest commercial and ship-owning families in
the State; but where scores knew it before, thou-
sands knew it after Way, C.-J., had finished
and the papers had reported how he had passed a
sentence of 12 months —“although I do it with
great distress.” Really, somebody ought to beg
his Honor to knock off what nearly everybody
regards as mere apologies to rich relatives for
doing his plain duty.

“ Stony ” sends this :—•
I haven’t been in dear did London lately, but from
what the little birds tell .me, things in the pet dog lj np
seem to be crazier there than ever they were. One of
these little birds, just out from Drizzlevill'e, says that the
City only knew three really fine days last summer, anil
that in consequence the fur-lined dog-coat has been in
great demand all through the year, which means good
money for the shops that cater entirely for the dog.
clothing trade. A late device is the linen collar and silk
tie, non-removable by the dog, though they mav be
removed by the other dog in the course of the fight
The collar is made in the correct masculine type of the
moment, and worn with tweed suitings. In oiie instance
referred to, a lady dog was dressed in a miniature seal-
skin jacket, a real lace handkerchief being in the pocket
thereof. And in the ears of the lady dog there were
diamond drops. Decadent, degenerate, decaying
London!
Journalist “ Dagonet ” Sims has started a
movement for getting British children to patronise
toys made on home ground. At present the trade
is almost entirely in the hands of foreigners.
“Form a league of Little Britons. Let every
British girl demand at least one British doll to
nurse ; let every British hoy demand that he shall
have at least one box of British soldiers made bv
British hands on British soil,” says Sims. But
what good is this passing spasm compared with
the solid, continuous effects of Protection? A
movement like that has its little day, and does its
little scrap of good, and then it goes out of fashion
like some fleeting cult of small dogs or hand-
shakes.
“ 4711 ” writes from Christchurch (M.L.):
“D.H.R.’s” statement (B. 9/4/’08) that the cotton tree
flourishes in N.Q. without much pampering, is just about
as true a statement as could be made. I had the pleasure
of seeing some very fine cotton-bushes, a few months ago,
a little higher than Bundaberg. Nevertheless, there are
stjll a few misguided State Frighters who go to no end of
bother to air their ignorance on this as on other matters.
I was at a State Frights meeting in the Perth Town Hall,
last year, and was informed by one of Westralia’s alleged
coining men, an employee, by the way, of the importing
firm of Goode, Durrant and Co., that the proposed duty
on cotton goods was robbery, “ because there was not an
ounce of cotton grown in the whole of Australia.” This
sparkling tarradiddle was swallowed by the meeting with
acclamation, and in all probability the A.C.M. felt some-
thing like Mercia looked when she waved the emblem in
“ The Sign of the Bosh.”

OLONEL LIGHT,
who was said to
have founded the
city of Adelaide
in 1836, must have
been devoted to
reg u I arity and
good order, for the city—that is
the southern or more business por-
tion is laid out with the unfaltering pre-
cision of squares on a check suit. Indeed Adelaide
testifies to the prim mathematical mind of either
its founder or its first surveyor, for its blocks,
streets, and squares were carved out of the real
estate by a person who knew his Euclid like the
back of his hand. To the visitor, this view of its
foundation will be found readily apparent.
There is taste and dignity in its arrange-
ment ; unique formality in the planning
of its squares; foresight in its situation;
and, above all, regard for posterity in its creation.
Maybe, the inhabitant of a city less regular in its
details will find the formality, by contrast, a little
disquieting—even to say, grotesque. Yet, for all
that, Adelaide is an enviable example. People
who build cities in their spare time will obtain
many valuable hints from its formation.
North Adelaide, which is less regular than the
southern portion, is, strictly speaking, the resi-
dential quarter. Between it and the city proper
stretch acres and acres of fine park lands,
through which winds the River Torrens. Formerly
this stream was the cause of annoyance and
acrid comment; but, owing to the erection
of a dam, it has been converted into a splendid
sheet of water. Its banks have been beautified ;
a band rotunda has been built; and the general
surroundings materially embellished by the hand
of man. However, where to go, what to see ?
Churches, buildings, statues? Adelaide has
them all in abundance. Where there are
not churches there are buildings—and
that is not written in any spirit of
facetiousness. As' for the statues,
their interest is perplexing to,
say, a greengrqcer. Conven-
tionality has been cleverly
disregarded. The classical rubs
shoulders with the modern. As
cases in point, Hercules com-
placently nods, so to speak, at
Queen Victoria in Victoria
Square; while a trio, com-
prising the Goddess of Love, a
but toll poet, mid an equestrian statue commemor-
a !i n f the Boer War, denote the entrance to the
official domicile of the State Governor. Such
selections may not appeal to the aesthete—but
public taste is pre-eminent in these matters.
Otherwise Adelaide possesses many objects of in-
terest. Ihe Museum has, among other things, a
collection of native weapons said to be the finest
in existence—a point for the ethnologist. The
zoologist may be animated by a survey of 1400
distinct species of animals, birds and reptiles
ror the dilettante there is the Art Gallery, con-
taining the works of many foreign and local
painters ; while the architect can arrive at some
valuable conclusions concerning the structural
qualities of the G.P.0.. or the ornate Elizabethan
style of the bchool of Mines and Industries For
the mere tripper—and the term is not applied in
any contemptuous sense—there are all these
things, and more. The small sum of five-
pence will take one to Henley Beach ; a further
but really infinitesimal, outlay will introduce one
to Burnside, or to Mitcham, or to Paradise, the
road to which is through orchards and market
gardens—as it should be. Adelaide is also practi-
cally surrounded by parks, which contribute to
tiie beauty of its environment. All mav be
reached by tram from Victoria-square, which
according to Adelaidean calculations is’
very properly, the centre of the uni-
verse. Furthermore, amusements are plentiful
Ihey range from drama to ice skating, or from
tram rides to vaudeville shows. All tastes are
provided for ; and, it is needless to add, all tastes
are satisfied. Adelaide is not moribund, as it has
been held to be. It is self-contained but cheerful
respectable but vital. That is the current tale.
It was Governor Hindmarsh who, on 28th
December, 1836, proclaimed South Australia a
British province. This interesting ceremony
took place in the vicinity of what is now
Glenelg. Ihe original tree, under which the
proclamation was made, is still preserved—now
however, decayed and bent in the form of an arch,’
drooping to the earth. It is one of the few relics
of the beginning that remain. Since that his-
torical happening, the development of South Aus-
tralia has gone on continuously—and especially
can this be said of its mining and agriculture.
I he land under cultivation is said to be, in number
of acres, second only to Victoria; while its
mineral deposits—of copper particularly—are ex-
ceedingly rich. As far back as 1838 silver lead
was discovered in the Mount Lofty ranges.
Copper ore was first discovered at Kapunda in
1843, and the same mineral found later at Burra
Bnrra in 1845, and at Wallaroo and Moonta in
1861. Copper-mining is now the staple
mineral industry, though gold, iron, coal,
platinum, other metals, diamonds and other
gems, are all known to exist. In addition, South
Australia has another important industry—that of
wine growing ; an enormous area being peculiarly
adapted to the cultivation of the grape. This
industry provides means of livelihood for a con-
siderable number of Germans, small colonies of
them having settled in various parts of the State.
Out at Tanunda, the visitor may observe acres
and acres of vines receiving the careful attention
of the Kaiser’s countrymen, and, it is not unlikely,
be greeted with “ Wie geht es?” from the
first small boy—for German hasn’t “died
graceful ” in those parts. Tanunda, indeed
the whole of the fertile country given up
to wine production, is reached by rail via
Gawler, a picturesque little town, notable
for its quaint avenue of eucalyptus trees, all not
more than abut 15ft. in height. The planting of
the eucalyptus for such purposes will inevitably
? fcr ik e the tourist as a happy departure, especially
if he be accustomed to avenues of elms and other
imported trees. A rather lengthy train journey,
but one justified under the circumstances, is that
to Me. Gambier in the remote south-eastern por-
tion of the State. This is the centre of volcanic
country, and, years and years ago, there were up-
heavals. To-day there remain dead craters, filled
with water—and caves curiously mystical in
candle-light. Fine caves there are also at Narra-
coorte; while the traveller in search of novel
sights will find a steamer at Port Adelaide, which
will run him across St. Vincent’s Gulf to Edith -
burg, where the salt lagoons, especially in the
summer, present striking effects. If there is
time to spare, Port Elliott is worth while. It is
one of the principal seaside resorts in the State
—for folk regard it like they do old hats. The
mouth of the Murray, Goolwa, and the beautiful
Hindmarsh Valley, are but a few miles distant.
Many of the spots, such as The Coorong and
Lakes Victoria and Albert, will appeal to the
tourist equipped with kodak, rod and gun. One
might also record that Port Pirie might be in-
cluded in the itinerary with profit. Scenically it
is not particularly edifying; but its huge works
where the silver, ore from Broken .Hill is treated’
are instructive* not to say, interesting. That does
not exhaust the subject of
Adelaide and South Aus-
tralia—but space is not
infinite, as it is alleged
to be. Confessedly,
much is omitted. It
is therefore desir-
able that the tourist
supply the blanks.
And the Five Com-
panies will attend to
the preliminaries!

constipation too. Don’t go on suffering. Mother Seigel’s Syrup
will cure you by putting your stomach in order, and purifying
your whole system.
