Business, Robbery, etc.
Now that the N.S.W. coastal strike is over, the
North Coast Co. is setting out after its lost monopolies.
It has had one slice of luck. The Illawarra Co., which
trades south, recently lost the Bega. Two boats which
went to rescue the Bega’s passengers were more or less
damaged. Further, the Allowrie arrived in port in such
a state that it couldn’t go out again without repair, and
the Illawarra met with an accident in the bowels or
stomach of its machinery. The Eden was getting mended
at the same time, and generally the Illawarra Co. s name
was sniffed at down the coast as signifying bad luck, the
position was urgent, and the directors hurriedly purchased
the N. Cain Co.’s Kempsey. This was where the luck of the
North Coast Co. came in. The co. had once almost
secured a monopoly of the northern trade by the
simple process of buying up the opposition when it
became dangerous. A few years ago N. Cain, a Port
Macquarie timber man, quarrelled with the North
Coast people, and started an active opposition.
He got immediate support on the understanding that
he would not sell out to the North Coast Co. Cain
flourished, and turned himself into a co., and last
vear imported the fine steel steamship Kempsey
to challenge the North Coast Co. on the Macleay Haver.
The Kempsey was a success, and was in a fair way to
acquire the best of the trade when the strike came on.
When it was over, the North Coast Co. found the Macleay
River business gone, and to get it hack meant a big
fi' r ht The River was joyful, and thought itself secure in
the knowledge that the N.C. Co. couldn’t buy the Kemp-
sev for love or money. To everybody’s rage, the Illa-
warra Co. stepped in where the North Coast agents
didn’t dare to tread, and the Kempsey left the Macleay.
Now Cain’s company has agreed to forsake the Macleay
altogether, while the old concern will clear out from Port
Macquarie and the Hastings River. Each river will thus
have to put up with a monopoly of its own. But it is
understood that Cain’s crowd intends to wage war else-
where against its rival, and it has much profits made
during the strike as the sinews of battle.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: The half-yearly
meeting of the Colonial Bank of Australasia recalls
incidents early in its career. It started business in
1856 in what had been a hotel on the N.E. cor-
ner of Little Collins and Elizabeth streets—a hotel
patronised by Lola Montez on her visit to Melbourne. In
the early ’sixties, John O’Shanassy being at the head of
the institution, the bank was attacked by the Age, chiefly,
it was understood, because it was a recognised Roman
Catholic institution. A run ensued, which lasted for one
whole day. The bank kept open as long as people pre-
sented themselves for money. In the course of the day
John O’Shanassy mounted the counter, and in
his best style and his best style was something
enormous —he said things that David Syme never
forgot The bank was the victim of two curious frauds.
On a Saturday forenoon, at the busiest time, between 11
and 12, a cheque for £7OO was presented to the paying
teller. It appeared to have been duly initialled by the
ledger-keeper, and it was paid in gold. In those days the
holder of a cheque presented the bit o’ paper himself to
the ledger-keeper, who initialled it with a flourish easy
of imitation. That expert thief was caught, or was sup-
posed to have been caught; anyhow, someone went up
for it. Soon after the culprit was released, another cheque
for some hundreds was presented in the rush. This one was
perforated, as all cheques were then by the ledger-keeper,
and it was cashed. It also was faked, and the peculiarity
in each case was that the signature was that of a person
who had no account at the bank. A lawyer’s clerk was
arrested for this second offence, and convicted, because
he could not account for his whereabouts between the
hours of 11.30 and 12 o’clock on that particular Saturday.
Amongst the founders of the Colonial Bank was “ Big
Clarke, the great landowner, and grandfather of Baronet
Clarke' who is just now assisting, financially and person-
ally, in certain theatrical ventures.
Our Pathan brethren are apparently as great a
nuisance in India as they are here. A. J. Fraser Blair
writes concerning them in the Pall Mall Magazine, and
calls them the “ Scotch drapers of Bengal.” Fraser Blair
is a Scotch name, so the. owner thereof possibly knows.
At the beginning of the cold weather the Pathans come
down from the hills and spread themselves over the lower
provinces, selling goods on the “ easy ” instalment
plan. Later in the season they go round with
clubs to collect payment. Mr. Blair says :“ A few years
ago they had established such a reign of terror among the
timid peasantry of Bengal that special measures had to be
taken to curb them. They are still an appalling nuisance,
and will remain so as long as Afghanistan marches with
India.” Deportation and long terms of imprisonment
have greatly toned down these time-payment drapers,
and the writer admits them to be fine, robust animals]
“ but from every civilised point of view the most trucu-
lent and treacherous villains unhung.”
“Stony”: The porkly propputy-owner stood
upon the new-laid lawn in front of his crimson house, and
stared up and down the street. “ It’s a reel nice neighbor-
’ood,” he remarked to me, “ an’ the ’ouse is dirt cheap at
the money.” From the pines along the Hawthorn-road,
down south, back to the curve of the quiet crescent, there
stretched a bright array of tuck-pointed brick villas, with
other green lawns about them, and with flowers blooming
down the side fences. Each house had a “ Tradesman’s
Entrance,” with a gravelled track to the rear. “ A very
nice neighbor’ood,” urged the seller. “Hi live in the
’ouse down there with th’ hornamental porch. It’s a very
select neighbor’ood ! None o’ yer riff-raff an’ rag-tail
’ere ! ” He pulled off his hat and scratched his warm
scalp, and spat heavily upon the violets in the border.
“ Yes,” he said emphatically. “None o’ yer shovin’-in
crowd up this street! We’re hall ’v a clarss ’ere all
right 1”

“Hassan”: I used to wonder where the
posterity of the pirates and highwaymen of the past had
got to. I know now ; they sell things at the door out of
barrows. There is an itinerant nurseryman in our
suburb, and his name is Morgan. He is evidently the
heir of Henry Morgan, buccaneer. He sold my un-
suspicious aunt some pot-plants the other week, and,
when they didn’t continue to exist, my Aunt discovered
that Morgan had forgotten to put any roots into the pots.
Unabashed by my remarks, when he came round again
he succeeded in selling me some border plants, which
turned out to be Bathurst burrs. I was rather annoyed,
and waited for Morgan. He came, and told me that what
I wanted more than anything else on earth was a “ nice
’edge—-it’ll make yous nice an’ private, mate.” I said
that there was only one sort of hedge-plant that was any
good to me, and he hadn’t got it. He said he could get
it if I’d “give it a name, boss.” I gave it one—he was
to bring me a dozen tum-tum trees. “ Gord bless me
soul,” he remarked, “ some bloke’s got your leg, mister—
there ain’t no such tree.” I went inside and brought out
Lewis Carroll, and showed him the verse where the tree
grows. He went off to look for it. Sometimes I hope I
will never see him again, but at others I fear that he will
turn up one day when I am out, and sell my aunt the
tum-tum trees I ordered—or something as near to them
as the vacant lot at the back of his house can produce.

For all-round handiness, the South Melbourne
Market holds a high place. On any Saturday evening a
lonely bachelor may, within its precincts, chooseacharm-
ing and useful wife from the battalions of girls who work
behind the stalls. He may purchase a comprehensive
trousseau for her, and fit himself out at the numerous
drapery and clothing counters ; buy the ring from the
“ Brum.” dealer ; secure all necessary furniture, with a
wide selection in crockery and household goods; select
the choicest plants for the garden ; stock the larder with
everything, from fruit, bread, meat and vegetables to
cheese and butter; buy pictures for the walls, and
books for the shelves ; snap up a cheap line in
footwear, and cure the resultant corns with a red
liquid prepared and sold by a “’sperienced ” man of
color ; and finally the happy couple may be married by
any one of the numerous Bible bangers who bang and
hold forth in the vacant spaces. The health of the bride
may be toasted in extraordinary penny drinks of all
colors and flavors, and the honeymoon can be spent visit-
ing the freak side-shows. Perambulators and other
neeessary equipment may also be obtained on the spot.
The only things that can’t be purchased are a house and
a divorce, but if you say something rude to the Flying
Anvels push, the Angels will probably heave half a house
at you, a brick at a time, also a few' palings off the neigh-
boring fences.


Two Hundred and Sixteen
Reasons.
There are two hundred and sixteen reasons why you, if you are about
to purchase a piano, should obtain a Beale Piano.
Look at the four illustrations herewith. Start with No. 1.
You note those rows of holes in the wooden wrest-plank of the imported
When the piano is “ strung ” each one of those holes is filled
oy a wrest-pin a piece of steel shaped not unlike a blunt nail
without a head.
Each pin is a quarter of an inch in diameter. Each is driven into its
hole with a hammer-it must fit tightly, so as to resist the pulling-
round of the wire which is wound round it.
Each pin is thereiore like a wedge in the wrest-plank, and when the wire
(or “ string ”) is tightened up by turning the pin round, and thus
coiling the wire upon it, each pin becomes in addition a lever, because
of the pull of the wire.
There are two hundred and sixteen of these pins. The total strain
exercised upon them by all the wires when at concert pitch
amounts to nearly thiyty tons.
Remember that they are only held in a wooden plank.
No one can suppose that a single wooden plank, driven full of wedges,
which are also levers, can for long withstand such a strain.
Especially in a climate like the Australian.
Whence—see Figure 2. Now compare Figure 2 with Figures 3 and 4.
There is the whole story.
In the Beale Pianos those two hundred and sixteen pins are contained
entirely in solid iron.
They will v/ithstand indefinitely any strain that may be put upon them.

frame to the wrest-plank. The wrest-pins and strings have been re-
moved so as to show the parts more clearly.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Australian eight-oared championship occu-
pied the murky Yarn, last Saturday, under subdued
weather conditions. The course was over an exact 2£
miles, from Ooode Island to the steam crane just below
Queen’s Bridge. South Aus., Queensland, New South
Wales, Tasmania, Westralia, and Victoria were in posi-
tion in said order from the rails —in a horsey way of
speaking. The three insiders had a distinct advantage
over the first mile, as a bend in the river helped them,
and their water was lively. South drifted, but Vies., on
the outside, lay like a log. Starter Enricott took con-
siderable pains, and got them away as one. Then an
awful churning of wet occurred as the boys put in strenu-
ous efforts to get going. Over the first half-mile the boats
were soldier-like, except that the Swan River men had
dropped astern. Vic. and the Ma State were shifting like
machines, t’others holding their own but raggedly. At
the junction of the old river, the Welshmen and Adelaide
were on equal terms in front, Vic. and Tassv next, and
the Bananaianders and Swanites stirring the spray behind.
The first mile was settled in smin. 19sec.—on anything
but lively water. The mile and a half saw the Welshmen,
with splendid blade work, and going like giants, lend the
locals by J-length, while the little Adelaide brigade, still
battling gamely, had fallen astern. Tassy was chasing Vic.
About the two miles, Jim Donald hit Vic. up. His men drew
on the leaders and looked something like winners, but the
muscle and science in front still kept her nose well ahead.
Meanwhile, Westralia had upset the Banana truck and
was drawing on t’others, but the paddle had long before
devolved into a Ma and Cabbage State struggle—there
was nothing else in it. On the bend for home, a poultry
incident almost occurred, but fortunately missed
the ’bus. Nutta Arnold kept Vic. hugging the shipping
on the inside running, and Donald, battling for dear life,
swelled the yell of Victorian joy. How'ever, despite said
effort, Fitzhardinge pushed the “light blues” well in
front, and was credited with a length and a quarter win
in 14min. 291-ssec. Victoria, Tassv, South Aus., Westralia
and Bananaland finished in said order. A tremendous mass
of humanity lined the banks and followed in steamers and
motor launches.
Prior to the “eights,” scullers Jack (Tas.), •
Brasch (Vic.); Towns (N.S. W.—a younger brother of the
mighty George Towns) and Callaghan (Q.), in that order
from the inside, supplied a fine struggle. At least they
all did save Callaghan, who got left early. The paddling
was over the same quantity of water as the combined
effort. Brasch was first to show' out clear from Towns,
while Jack apparently held something up his sleeve. At
the old river crossing Towns shot ahead with a vigor that
sent up Sydney stocks. The mile, reached in 6min. 45sec.,
saw Towns lead Brasch, with Jack handy. At two miles
Jack took Brasch’s water, and tackled the massive
Sydneyite and beat him for the lead. Then Towns
dropped his bundle or something. Brasch still held on,
but failed to catch the Tassy expert by three lengths.
Time 17min. 50 !-ssec. Callaghan got lost or fell out.
Ivens, the Vic. first string, didn’t turn out —a pain in his
Little Mary caused him to look on.
* * * <•
On Saturday last the All Blacks met a combina-
tion of N.S.W. and Queensland, which cilled itself the
Australian Team, at Sydney Agricultural Ground, and
defeated it by 11 points'to 10. This was due to the fact
that it was Messenger’s dull day, as far as goal-kicking
was concerned ; had the mighty booter been feeling good,
the Maorilanders might have been beaten by about 20 to
11. Many of his kicks missed points by inches only, and
although the play of the Maorilanders in the first half was
superb, it was nothing compared with their luck in the
ma'ter of Messenger’s kicks. Right through the first
half they rolled up the Australian team much as a deft
cook sweeps up prawms for the salad, and, in spite of a
fine defence right on the line, they scored 11 while Aus-
tralia scored only two. The beginning of the second-half
found the Blues still struggling like a small fly in a great
deal of soup. Messenger broke away continually, but,
through lack of combined support, vvas pulled down,
and the leather carried back to Australian terri-
tory. Long shooting by Messenger, well followed
up, compelled the Maorilanders to save the posi-
tion by forcing twice in rapid succession. The Black
forwards dribbled it back, but the Australians took it off
their toes and again got the goal in check. With a des-
perate effort the Blacks carried it out and right down to
the Australian line, where a copious and fluent struggle
took place. Amidst serious excitement the Australian
backs cleared the line, forcing the Maorilanders back a
few yards, but not out of striking distance. There the
game hung, men going down on either side in little heaps,
till Messenger secured, side-stepped the crowd that
sprang at him, and then, being seized on all hands, screw-
punted over his head, and sent the ball down towards the
middle of the field, whence he had one of his many un-
successful Shots at goal. Again the Blacks crowded the
Australian line, but Hedley drove them back by kicking
clear. Jubilation was changed to consternation as Turtill
marked, and Wrigley narrowly missed the Australian
goal. After the kick-off Messenger again got going.
His pass was missed by the man he threw it to, but
another, Lutge, was handy, and, dashing in, secured,
and scored a try which Messenger failed to convert. Almost
immediately the ball was again in play, McLean, of
Queensland, made a dashing run, which carried the game
to the Maoriland line. Another shot by Messenger at an
almost impossible angle just missed, and the Maori-
landers, trying to rush the ball out, lost it. It was
kicked here, there, everywhere, and rebounding across
the Maoriland line, there was a general race to get posses-
sion. Rosenfeld hurled himself in the van, and scored.
This time Messenger did not miss, and a whoop of con-
siderable dimensions split the sky as the scoring board
showed Maoriland leading by only one point. The tail of
Australia was exalted, and every man played hard to
score, but the Blacks put up a fine defensive game. Twice
Messenger took shots, and his last grazed the bar in its
downward curve—but on the wrong side. Then the
whistle blew, leaving the Maorilanders winner by one
point.
* * * *
It seems that in last issue this paper did an
injustice to the All Blacks in mentioning that two of the
men were ordered off the field. They were not; they
only ought to have been, and as a matter of justice
the paper cheerfully puts the All Blacks right. On
Saturday one of them was sent off for swinging his dirty
right too freely, and thereby inflicting severe injury on
Henessey, of NiS.W. Inthisease it appears to have been
nothing but infernal carelessness, and not malice. But
men who fend recklessly and without due regard for other
players’ faces, must be made to understand that it doesn’t
pay. Baskerville, the leading spirit of the enterprise,
played on Saturday, and showed that in his play he is as
perfect a footballer as his well-written book indicates him
to be. Unusually fast and full of resource, he played a
game that shone brightly by contrast in a t6am that
mostly relied upon hard bullocking for success. Over
20.000 people paid admission fees, and the new game has
received a considerable lift by the visit of the Maori-
landers. The Blacks have now gone to Queensland, and
w’ill remain there till early in June, when they will feturn
to N.S.W. and give the game another lift.
While the Rugby Leaguers were struggling
with the MaoHlanders on Sydney Agricultural Ground in
the presence of 20,000 people, one of the finest contests
ever played in Sydney that between University and
Eastern Suburbs —drew' only 7000 to the Sydney Cricket
Ground. It w'as a game of dashing individual w T ork and
splendid combined movements, all carried out at top
speed. The resurrection of the Eastern Suburbs after the
League break-upds amazing. The first half was a series
of finely-worked charges by both sides, in which the
Eastern fullback, the solid and reliable D’Alpuget, played
a star game. At half-time, University, in spite of having
been mostly on the defensive, led as regards the score
by five to three. The second half opened with increased
speed on both sides, and the game became glittersome.
Corbett of the East raged furiously, and, backed up by
Burton, Doyle, and Miller, carried the game into. Uni.
Territory. Uni. responded with well-organised rushes,
which were generally blocked before reaching striking
distance. When the whistle went, both teams were
ready to drop, and the board showed Uni. had won by
eight points to six.
• * * *
For the rest, Glebe made a most unholy show
of Sydney, taking it by the scruff of its neck, and casting
it downstairs to the tune of 34 points to nil. Western
Suburbs and Manly played a scoreless draw at Concord,
and separated with amity between them. Balmain just
managed to down St. George by four to three, and South
Sydney trod on the already much damaged North Sydney
by 14 to three. So far, North Sydney, St. George, Wes-
tern Suburbs, and Manly have not won a game this,
season, although Western Suburbs has managed to
save its face somewhat by making a couple of draws.
University and South Sydney, which now have the front
rank to themselves, with eight competition points each,
have not yet lost a game, and Saturday’s defeat was the
first that Eastern Suburbs has suffered. Newtown has
also lost one, and it and Eastern Suburbs are now in the
second rank, with six points each. Balmain is alone in the
third line with five, and Sydney and Glebe are in the
fourth row, with four each. The other three are together,
with two each, and poor little Manly is at the bottom,
with one.
* * * *
In the Soccer competition, Garden Island still
further astonished both natives and importations by
putting up a great struggle against Pvrmont, the premier
club last year. It was beaten, and fairly well beaten too,
by four to nil, but the winners had to play all they knew.
Sydney won its second game, heating the baby club,
Northern Suburbs, by three to one. Rozelle visited Glebe
at Wentvyorth Park, and got a considerable thumping,
the figures being four to nil, which is a reasonably large
score in the Sydney Soccer game. Balmain and Western
Suburbs played a draw. So Pyrmont and Svdney lead
the competition list with four points, Balmain is in the
next line with three, and Garden Island, Glebe, Western
Suburbs, and Ultimo are together with a pair each. The
rest nowhere.

In the Australian League Competition, East
Sydney, Y.M.C.A. and Redfern are together with eight
points, having each w T on the first two games of the season.
Newtown and Balmain have four each, and Sydney, North
Shore and Paddington, having- served as the anvils on
which the three first mentioned have beaten out their

* * * •
The present popularity of Yic. football.is being
well maintained bv the surprising doings of unexpected
teams ; also, it finds vent, as heretofore, in insults arid
attempted assaults on the luckless umpires. As one
courageous youth remarked to the writer, at the close of
the Richmond-Universitv disturbance —“ There were over
a hundred of us, and we were going to give him a doing,
only the Johns kept us off while they put him in a cab.
I had a rock in me pocket meself.” He wore the Richmond
colors, so there was no need to ask who won. The
scores were 14.10 to 12.4, which, remembering the
poor showing made by’Varsity against Essendon the previ-
ous week, argues an unexpected improvement on the ’Dons’
last year’s form, which was fully justified by their per-
formance against Saints. Latter had their best team out,
and played all they knew-, yet they had never a hope on
the play, and finished 38 points to the bad—l2.l4 to 7.6.
Geelong men put up a great fight against South, and but
for weak play forward might eventually have w-on. As it
was, they weren’t disgraced, the board at the finish
showing 7.9 to 4.8. The Carlton men journeyed to the
M.C.C. ground, and there found a tougher proposition
than they had anticipated, the “ Red Legs ” holding their
own for the first quarter, and part of the second. Then
condition told its tale, and the premiers went ahead
steadily and kept, there, finishing with 9'B to 6'B. The big-
gest crowd, anticipating a fierce fight, went to see those
old opponents, ’Roy and ’Wood, settle their differences ;
but ’Wood is not now the ’Wood of other days. It has
lost many of the men whose finished exposition of sys-
tematic play won the premiership, and, though it has
acquired new and good men, it has not trained them up
to the same pitch of excellence. Also it has forgot-
ten that the essence of that system is to pass the ball
to the right man with despatch and certainty ; not to
throw it wildly or anyhow, so that an opponent may
intercept it. To send the ball to the right man
it is necessary to be able to kick straight when
closely pressed. Collingwood’s kicking is shown by
its score—3 goals 14 behinds, to ’Roy’s 9 goals 9
behinds. Systematic, organised, and accurately-timed
play is pretty to watch as well as successful, and ’Wood
should forthwith set about training its men to play it.
The 'Wood-’Roy discussion also saw an attempt to rebuke
the umpire with hat-pins and other persuaders, but the
police were on hand and got him away safely.
* * * *
Like its step-brother in the League, the Asso-
ciation Esserfdon team shows improvement on last
season’s form. On Saturday it tackled the premiers,
Williamstown, when both played a fast and strenuous
game, with a finish that gave the barrackers something
to yell about. Quarter time saw the two nearly level, 20
points to 13 ; but at half time Essendon had increased its
score to 51 points while ’Town stood still. Third quarter
'Don added a goal, while ’Town put on two, latter starting
the last term 32 points to the bad. Then ’Town’s skipper
changed his formation and increased his ruck, and the 17
bogged in. They bogged to such purpose that at five
minutes to knock-off they had wiped out 31 of the 32
points. Here the Essendons made a supreme effort and
scored a goal—and the game was theirs, 10.4 to 9.3
wonderful kicking, considering the youth of the season.
The Brighton novices put up a good game against Foots-
cray, finishing with 7.7 to 9.10. Preston managed to kick
13 more points than the regrettable Ports. Northcote
gave the worst exhibition of the day by scraping together
28 points to Prahran’s 98; and Brunswick handed the
ashes of despair to North, the board showing 112 points
to 54.
* * # *
During the week the League refused transfers
to two individuals who wanted to play for Melbourne, one
because he had foolishly shown a letter from an M.F.O.
official promising him a billet, the other because he'had
demanded 15s. per match for his services from an Associ-
ation club. As this latter player’s previous club has dis-
banded, the refusal practically amounts to disqualification
sine die as far as the League is concerned. The M.F.O.
delegates were quite annoyed at these decisions, which
attitude of theirs argues a short memory. They have for-
gotten that at the annual meeting at the beginning of the
’O7 season their then secretary said it would be necessary
for them, if they were to do any good as a team, to drop
pure amateurism and do as other clubs do—find their
players billets. The Melbourne F.C. can find billets for
so many players that the League is shy of giving it
transfers.
* * * *
Certainly the Norwood (S.A.)Clubisa wonder.
It was champion of Australia in 1888 and 1907, and has held
the premiership 14 years (’7B, ’79, ’BO, ’Bl, ’B2, ’B3, ’B7, ’BB,
’B9, ’9l, ’94, ’Ol, ’O4, ’O7). And it has been second on the
list seven times, and third eight times. In 30 years it has
been unplaced only once. Every good footballer should
take off his hat to the Norwood Club.
All hockeydom was looking forward to the
struggle between Barbarians and Corinthians at Rush-
cutters Bay, Sydney, on'Saturday. And it was a great
game. Corinthians had the prestige of the past season
to back them up, but it seemed to rouse the Barbarians
to greater efforts. Corinthians did not play with their
usual reckless dash. They affected a caution which dis-
tracted from the spectacular side of the business, while it
made for safe play. As befits a champion team, Corin-
thians at once went for the Barbarian line, but
were met with a vicious counter attack which
staggered them. The Wild Men gave no respite,
and the Corinthians suddenly found themselves on the
defensive, but before they had time to fully realise
the fact the Barbarians had scored. Reforming their
shattered ranks, the Corinthians again swept to the
attack. Barbarians struggled frantically, but the ex-
quisite passing of the Corinthians baffled every attempt
to secure the ball, which was carried safely to the Bar-
barian twenty-five. As the backs crowded to the goal,
the Corinthians came trooping down, the ball flying back
and forwards across their front; then there was a sudden
drive and the sphere shot through, making the scores
equal. After that, the game settled down to a series of
splendidly ordered attacks by the Corinthians, all of
which were defeated by the Barbarian backs right on the
goal line, and the struggle ended in a draw, with one
point all.
* * * *
Hockey is making great strides in Victoria. It
was not till last season that an association was formed,
and when it started it had only 130 members. This year
it begins proceedings with a roll of 230, and fresh mem-
bers are joining every week. The premiership competi-
tions commenced on Saturday week, and the outward and
visible sign of championship is a silver cup presented by
Mr. T. G. Watson, the president of the association. Last
season the hockeyites called their clubs by fancy names,
such as lona, Waitangi, Woorigollen, etc. ; but this year,
with a view to rousing healthy local rivalry, the aesthetic
nomenclature has been gently dropped overboard in favor
of sober district names. lona has become the beastly,
common Elsternwick, while the others have changed to
Brighton, St. Kilda (that’s classic, anyhow), and Fairfield.
Rumors of a visit from the Adelaideans has vitalised the
game, and made the players feel that they have attained
an inter-State status. The chief difficulty is the lack of
good umpires, and the association is encouraging a study
of the rules by paying half a guinea for first-grade
matches and 7s. 6d for second-grade struggles. Alto-
gether, the outlook is promising.
* * * *
Victoria finished its cricket for the season, last
week, when Gold-street (Collingwood) beat North Fitzroy
for. the State Schools premiership. These little nippers
now boast two records—they have won the Schools pen-
nant four years in succession, and they have never been
beaten in a premiership match. The weather was all against
the batsmen, and the ’Woodsies, going in first, made only
68—Cole 25, Watson 13, Kelso 10. Then they put their
opponents in and out twice in short order for 20 and 45
Magrath 11, Arkinson 11 (not out). W. Bradshaw took
three wickets for eight, and five for nine; and Cole
aforesaid seven for 10, and five for 30. As very few Vic-
torian State scholars play in their school teams for more
than a couple of years, it is evident that Gold-street’s
proud position is chiefly due to its sports coach, Dominie
A. J. Lumsden, who is a fine cricket tutor.
* « * •
Brookes, the man without a smile, showed
Sydney the science of lawn tennis at the N.S.W.-Vic.
inter-State match. Or rather he showed some ball
juggling which he called tennis. The big glum man
proved himself the finest wielder of the racquet ever seen
in Australia, and his records indicate that he is the finest
player in the world. He does not play spectacular tennis—
he has got past that. His game is mathematical tennis, and
he produces startling results with strokes that look decep-
tively easy. Swishes and drives are unnecessary to him.
Rice, playing his sturdy, old-fashioned game, went down
like a novice before this exponent of the New Tennis, for
lawn tennis is now a game worked out by science.
Brookes apparently knows everything that is to be known
about the “work” to be put on the ball. He has to a
large extent discarded his famous “American” serve;
for the same reason he has discarded the swish and the
drive. He depends on a fierce, exquisitely placed serve
with a cut that, combined with its pace, makes it almost
unplayable. In delivering, he uses the full length of the
racquet, holding the end well in the palm of the hand,
and into that serve he manages to work every ounce of
his weight. Then he saunters up to the net—never
hurrying or running, and anticipating bis opponent’s re-
turn, is waiting to dispose of it with either a dainty flick
or a hard place. His variety is infinite, and his back-
hand work is marvellous, being charged with the same
electric “lift” that characterises his freehand drive.
The main features of his game are coolness and deliberate
placing.
By securing 15 rubbers to six, N.S.W. has
proved that it stands upon a higher tennis plane
than Victoria —with, of course, the exception of Brookes.
Heath, the Victorian, shows remarkable promise. (He
has the advantage of the example of Brookes.) Addison,
too, showed excellent form in defeating C. B. Jones.
Rice’s old-fashioned style made poor showing against the
world champion, but Doust’s remarkable effort in his
second set, running Brookes to five all, and actually win-
ning off one of Brookes’ serves, show’s that the New
Tennis is striking congenial soil in Australia.
* * * *
The Hog-in-the-Car is a grunting Hog at
present. One day last week eleven motorists w’ere fined
£5 each for furious driving in North Sydney. They were
all doing over 20 miles an hour. The law' says that in the
city no car shall go at over six miles an hour ; in the
metropolitan police district the limit is eight miles ; in
the Local Government districts, 10 miles. Sydney to
Hornsby is a favorite jaunt of the Hog, and if the trip is
to be run on regulation time the motor would be as quick
as a cab. Cars on the Gordon-road always hustle a bit, it
being reckoned the finest run in the neighborhood of
Sydney. Therefore, when a bobby, acting under instruc-
tions, waited one fine morning on a beautifully clear
stretch of thoroughfare near Chatswood, he made a good
bag. Recent motor-car fatalities have spurred the police
into action, and active war against the Jigger-Juggernaut
is pronounced. And w’hat is sauce for the motor-car is
also expected to be sauce for the motor-boat.
* * * *
Gotch, in his recent contest with Hacken-
schmidt at Chicago, according to the press accounts,
seems to have done everything but bite. He is said
to have used his fingers to gouge Hack’s eyes, and
to smother him by forcing them into the Russian’s
nostrils. He used his fists and elbows to batter the
Russian’s face till it was quite unrecognisable, and
generally played the wild beast and the savage. In spite
of his filthy tactics, the Yankee ruffian was unable to
throw Hackenschmidt fairly, and in the end was
tossed aside, after which the champion arose and
declined to continue under such conditions. The referee
immediately declared his wild beast to be champion of
the world, but outside his own particular cage no one
will take the decision seriously. Hackenschmidt has
offered to meet Gotch again at the Olympian games,
where, with decent referees and under proper conditions,
the struggle will be a wrestling match, _apd not an eye-
gouging wild-cat scrap. As said many times before in
this paper, the Yankee is anything in the world but a
sport.

Lured by the experience of Eight Hours Day,
when some 6000 persons swarmed on to the Melbourne
Saucer Track to view the discussion between Hock Keys
and Frank Thorn, while another 1000 evaded and defied
the parading troopers and scaled the high fence or
swarmed through gaps where sheets of iron had been
wrenched off, the same management made extensive
preparations to welcome the thousands that were ex-
pected to pay from Is. to ss. to see Peter Bannon throw
Dick Porter, last Saturday evening. As there was no
specified stake the match was apparently for the “ gate ” ;
if so it wasn’t worth the trouble. The entire portal
scarcelv equalled £5O. The match, preceded by harmless
bouts between sundry pupils of Fred Porter, resolved it-
self into a pleasing exhibition of ground and lofty “ wrast-
ling,” Bannon getting various solid holds on Porter and
then placidly allowing him to escape therefrom. He secured
the first fall, from a full-Nelson, in 29min. The pair
rested from their arduous endeavors for 15min., and
then turned, twisted and screwed for llmin., when
Porter, backing away from Bannon’s attempt at a neck-
hold, fell over the platform edge and bruised a tendon of
his leg. He then, as brother Fred unnecessarily observed,
“forfeited the match with a badly-injured ankle.”
Porter stripped at 10st. 31b., Bannon at list. lib. Before the
proceedings began, George Dinnie, a hefty-looking son of
Donald of that ilk, challenged any man in Australia for a
£lOO catch-as-can match, Clarence Weber preferred.
Clarence promptly accepted.
That tall muscle-culturist, Clarence Weber,
gave a humorous display at Melbourne Masonic Hall
recently. A principally female audience gazed, in a state
of fascination, at the statuesque form of the only
Clarence in a series of poses on a revolving pedestal
under a flood of lime or some other light. With partner
J. A. Price he gave some strength exhibitions, but the
gem of the evening ivas when he illustrated the different,
methods of breathing. With a towel wrapped round
where he puts his food he exhibited the displacement of
anatomy caused by tight lacing. He also blew himself
up to such an extent that puncture appeared imminent.
Several star pupils flung ironmongery about in a
more or less marvellous fashion, but after the
pink beauty of the undraped Clarence the girls
vot.ed it all flat, stale and unprofitable. This sort of thing
is all right for Clarence, but it is rough on the ordinary
sort of man who can’t blow himself up. All those girls,
when proposed to, will demand that the wooer shall
exhibit himself upon a pedestal, and the divinity will
regard him with a blasb, critical expression, and if he
doesn’t come somewhere near the Clarence standard he
will be turned down remorselessly. However, disappointed
femininity, like Providence, moves in a mysterious way,
and Clarence may yet live to regret his pedestal business.
It is a fearful thing to be hunted bv beauty-loving females.
Writer knows it from bitter experience—he’s been there.
* » * *
At the Gaiety Athletic hovel, Sydney, on
Tuesday night last week, Montague Andrews, of that
city, put ’em up to James Griffin, sometime of Maoriland,
and the rugged islander fell upon him, and smote him
where he resided and had his domicile. Andrews tried
to clinch, but was shoved off with one fist and smitten
simultaneously with the other something like half a
dozen times. He went down with a heavy meaty thud,
and staggering up in a dazed condition with his back to
Griffin, caught a swing on the back of the isthmus.
The crowd hooted—Heaven knows why. It had paid to
see a scrap, and it was getting the genuine naked article.
The first round consisted of Andrew's being flattened out,
and, getting up again, to be flattened out some more. It
spoke volumes for his training that he survived the
battering. His face was not all that it used to be, his
elbow had been gashed in his falls, and generally his sub-
cutaneous tissue showed signs of extensive dis-
organisation. But he pulled himself together in
a manner that bespoke the blameless life more
eloquently than many trumpets, and after a few
rounds waded in and gave Griffin some heavy deposits.
For a while the hollow reverberations from the neighbor-
hood of Griffin’s ribs suggested the Devil’s tattoo, and
Griffin revolved wildly. But the effect of much collision
in the first round began to tell on Andrews, who was
palpably a beaten man, although he fought with remark-
able skill and courage. He simply hadn’t the steam to
continue, and out of the whirl of blows that Griffin aimed
at him occasional ones got home, and then Andrews
would shoot backwards and go down with that same
eloquent thud that tells of considerable shock to the
system. He battled through fourteen rounds, and then
the accumulated shocks to the system tilted up the beam,
and he gave the signal for the casting-in of the damp
towel of the slugged-out. The distinctive feature of the
scrap was that it was the first really serious disturbance
seen in Sydney for about 12 months.

Another billiard champion is in sight in Eng-
land. Reece, who has been receiving 2000 in 9000 from
Dawson, has lately been heating him hv his points, and
has now challenged him to play level for £250. John
Roberts, interviewed on the matter, says that Reece has
improved in a startling way lately, and is certain to be
champion in a few years, but Roberts reckons that in the
present case he is making Dawson a present of £250.
It is high time that the shilling cash prize con-
sultation was smit hard where the Sunday fowl is usually
stricken, and with the same weapon. The thing has
grown to the magnitude of a huge public evil. To the
writer’s knowledge, one half-crown and six one-shilling
sweeps are raging in Sydney at the present time, with
subscription lists ranging from 25,000 to 50,000. Tobac-
conists, newsagents, bakers and grocers, and other
folk who have the run of the back door, are the most
active distributing agents for tickets. Sometimes they
bring round lists of winning numbers. Sometimes they
merely bring the laconic news, “ no luck,” by word of
mouth, and collect another set of shillings. Apparently,
Premier Wade has not heard of this huge stream of afflu-
ence, flowing weekly into the pockets of a gang °f un-
known promoters who draw their sweeps just as they
please, and of whose actions their subscribers have no
knowledge. When a man sends his money to Tattersaii,
he at least gets a fair drawing ; when he sends it to
the unknown Sydney promoter he gets just what-
ever the promoter likes to give him. People who
send their money to such concerns should be protectea
against them selves, and the best way to protect them wou
appear to be the establishment of a lottery drawn and con-
trolled by State officials. The profits would provide money
for the abolition of the Public Debt, and the esta •
lishment of properly run hospitals, and other g' ood ''’orK-.
The shilling evil is said to be rife in Melbourne, Adelaide,
Perth, Brisbane, and other cities throughout the Goin
monwealth, and the Australian Parliament might tniriK
hard over its wisdom in blocking business with latte
that it might be replaced by this sort of fraud, lort r“. B

A favorite method among gold-stealers in
X.S.W., according to Minister Moore, is to take
"P an abandoned mine and use it as a blind for
their nefarious purposes. That game has been
worked in several States, and eight or ten years
ago the swindle was especially fashionable in
A.S.W. Old mines on the Abercrombie and other
places had amazing resurrections, and every kind
and color of gold—even stuff that looked like
.jewellery boiled down —began to come out of those
long-dead shows. The game was overdone ; the
u? u e^an t 0 prowl round, badly disguised ; and
the shaft that laid the golden egg had to close up
again. Moore proposes to introduce a Bill
tor the registration of mining plants, though what
that will do Heaven only knows. It seems that
>t would be as much to the point to make a
complete list of the skull of Oliver Cromwell.

In connection with the Commission, of
Inquiry into the Sunshine disaster, Victoria is,
as usual, paying learned and expensive counsel to
appear for the Commissioners; out the lowly-paid
employees, who have been suspended without
salary ever since the accident, will have to pay
their own lawyers. And consider the wages of
these “highly-paid public servants” —Driver
Milburn, 14s. per day, after 31 years’ service;
driver Dolman, 12s. a day, 24 years’ service ;
fireman Tomlinson, Bs. fid. a day, 19 years; fire-
man Deveney, Bs. day, seven years; guard Darcy,
11s. a day, 35 years; while, after 25 years’ service,
one gold-laced officer, Kendall, has risen as far as
to have charge of Sunshine station, work the
signals, hump luggage, clean the buildings, book
parcels, sell tickets, attend two telegraph instru-
ments, and generally be the whole team and the
dog, at a little less than £3 a week. Even the
banks don’t sweat their clerks like that. And
after being compulsorily unemployed for a month
he is expected to pay his own lawyer in defence,
wmle the well-paid Commissioners, who may be
equally in fault, get one of the best defenders in
the State ,at the State’s expense. And their
salaries have not been stopped.
Mr. J. Thompson’s contribution to Aus-
tralian Defence proposals shows novelty. In what
is called an interesting lecture before the A.N. A.,
at Sydney, the ex-drill-instructor to the Highland
Light Infantry explained that 10,000 men could be
added to the Australian fighting line if, in hostili-
ties, the ambulance and commissariat work were
done by the Asiatics among us. But what if
Australia’s fight were with the Asiatics? Apart
from that, to give aliens (possibly hostile
aliens) charge of the wounded would be a horror,
and to give them custody of the victuals might
mean wholesale poison. How would Thompson
like to march against the Dowager Empress while
having to hold both hands on his stomach. If
Asiatics are to be taken into the Defence service,
it would be better to make them lecturers.

THE CLOSER SETTLEMENT ACTS.
SUBDIVISION OF THE
STAUGHTON VALE ESTATE.
APPLICATIONS are invited by the Lands Purchase Board for the unsold Farm Allotments on this Estate. Applications must be made on the
prescribed form, and be lodged with The Secretary, Lands Purchase Board, Melbourne, accompanied by the amount of deposit necessary.
The Estate originally comprised an area of 9830 acres of plain and gently undulating country. The soil varies from volcanic and strong chocolate
to clay and sandy loam. Good yields have been obtained in the past from the portions which have been cultivated. The rainfall averages
25 inches yearly, and is supplemented by the Little River, springs and dams, and good catchment areas are available. The timber consists of
box, oak and gum, of which an ample supply is available for fencing and firewood. Markets and railways are available at Bacchus Marsh,
miles/ Par wan, l]/ 2 miles; Melbourne, 35 miles; morning and evening trains to Melbourne stop at both stations.

OF LITTLE BOY
Bad Sores Spread Al I Over His Head—
Grew Worse Under Doctor’s Care
—Cuticura Effects Perfect Cure,
Bringing Relief with First Set.
FATHER RECOMMENDS
CUTICURA TO EVERY ONE
“When my little boy was about four
years old (ho is now about six and a
half years) a spot appeared on the side
of his face just under the ear. After
a time it broke and spread further
down. I took him to a doctor as soon
as it appeared. He was under his
orders for about three or four months
when he was much worse. Seeing in a
paper a Cuticura advertisement I went
and got a complete set of the Cuticura
Remedies, the Cuticura Ointment, Cuti-
cura Soap, and Cuticura Resolvent. I
took him away from the doctors and
fave him these Cuticura Remedies.
used two large bottles of Resolvent,
five tablets of Cuticura Soap, and two
or three tins of Cuticura Ointment.
From the first bottle he began to mend
and before he had finished the two he
was wholly mended. About twelve
months after he broke out again at the
back of the neck and it spread all over
the head. I had one large and two
Bmall bottles of Cuticura Resolvent,
three tins of Cuticura. Ointment, and
four tablets of Cuticura Soap before I
got it mended. I think it took me
about three months each time. It is
now about eighteen months since he
was ill, and it has not broken out again,
so I think he is all right now, thanks
to Cuticura. I always recommend
Cuticura wherever I can, and I will
always have pleasure in answering any
inquiries. R. Scarth, 25 Newport Rd.,
Middlesborough, York, June 24, 1906.”

The Inky Way.
“Cit”: The late Howard Willoughby was an able
journalist, an imperfect speller, and a shocking penman.
The compositors were constantly puzzled over his manu-
script, and occasionally fell into errors that brightened
the track of literature. The first team of English
cricketers that visited Australia commenced its opening
match on Ist January, 1862. Bennett, a notable
bowler of underhand slows, fell ill and couldn’t play in a
subsequent event, and Willoughby, then a reporter on
the Age, wrote that the Englishmen would be without
their slow bowler. And the compositor made it appear
that the Englishmen’s “ steam boiler ” was laid up.
* * * *
“K.0.K.”: Re “Sandra’s” par (9/4/’08), one of the
Perth (W.A.) morning papers achieved a cable howler
that simply loses any he quotes. It was at the time Gully,
ex-Speaker of the British House of Commons, was made
a peer. Contemporaneously the Pari Mutuel was estab-
lished on all French racecourses, and the bookmakers
were warned not to cry the odds. The message from
Reuter came through this way: “ Gully Viscount Book-
makers warned French racecourses.” Which the W.A.
cable genius proceeded to interpret: “ Messrs. Gully and
Viscount, the well-known bookmakers, have been warned
off French racecourses.”
* * * *
A feature of Sydney Telegraph is its hankering
after a word or phrase. Last year it was “ disgruntled.”
Everybody it didn’t like was disgruntled. The Federal
Premier was disgruntled, and so were the State Opposition,
Balfour and Chamberlain, the Continental anarchists and
the English cricketers. Lately the Telegraph has been
“linking up.” Broken Hill, Barren Jack, Riverina and
the Canberra Federal Capital site are all to be “ linked
up.” It even proposed to “ link up” the Sydney Show
Ground at Moore Park with the Coogee tram line. To-
day the word is “ aftermath ’-’—the “ aftermath ” of the
Federal crisis, of the test matches, of the Yankee money-
market collapse, of the banquet to Carruthers, and the
Lord knows what. And of course, the Braybrook horror
had its “aftermath.” The Telegraph shows signs of
wanting a new word. This writer suggests “ transmag-
nificandanbanuarity ” to go on with.
* * * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph made a diverting break
(5/5/’08) when, on receiving a letter from the Earl of
Meath—the noted Empire Day fanatic—on the only
topic this particular Earl ever writes about, it treated the
gorgeous one’s historic moniker to the inverted commas
of a pen-name. There will be a shocked Imperialist when
the following—the Telegraph’s page-index—gets to
foreign parts:—
In Defence of Contractors (John Ahearn). 9
Australia’s Opportunity at the Franco-British Exhi-
bition (Daniel O’Connor) 4
The Empire Movement (“Meath”) 4
Rating on Unimproved Values (“E.S.D. and “Tax-
payer ”) 4
The late Earl of Glasgow had something the same sort of
experience once. He was a shabby, dirty-looking old
peer, and on presenting a five-pound note at a London
railway station, he was asked to endorse his name on the
back. He wrote “Glasgow.” “I didn’t harsk fer the
place yer want to go to,” hissed the official, irritably;
“ sign yer name, yer old blank.” But there should be a
distinction between an ignorant Ma-land railway olficial
and an informative Ma-State newspaper.
* * * *
Broken Hill has a new paper, which comes out when it
can—The Flame. There is, on the Barrier, a little band
of whole-hog revolutionary Socialists who can’t stand
the slow trot of the Labor party. So they have broken
away, and with R. S. Ross (late editor of Labor Truth) at
their head, have bought an ancient newspaper plant that
has for years lain idle on the old Mount Brown goldfield,
and they make it shriek their creed once a month at least
for a start. Ross is a sincere apostle anyway ; he fell into
a good, “ stiddy job ” after leaving the Truth, but
chucked it to preach revolution on about a pound a week.
* * * *
“Bob” Sievier, racehorse-owner, betting-man (he was
the first to introduce ready-money bookmaking to Aus-
tralia), and, latterly, editor and newspaper proprietor,
lately bumped the first snag of his journalistic career,
when a Bow-street magistrate fined him £lO and £5 costs
for printing indecent matter in his Winning Post. Since
his libel fiasco, a few years back, Sievier has been
coining much pelf from his paper. It is a weekly
devoted to veiled (and occasionally unveiled) porno-
graphy, and to turf and sporting matters generally.
Sievier’s counsel, with his tongue stored away in his
cheek, contended that the indecencies complained of
wouldn’t do any harm, since the readers of the paper were
almost exclusively “bookmakers and others whose morals
were not likely to be corrupted by reading such para-
graphs,” etc. As a fact the Winning Post, like the Pink
’Un (on which the Winning Post is slavishly modelled),
penetrates to most wealthy British homes. There is no
affluent club in the British Empire which does not take
its Sporting Times (and most of them take their
Winning Post), and the average member endures
the red-hot japes on the front pages of these sheets
with equanimity. There is but one trait that will
keep a newspaper out of those places where well-to-do
Anglo-Saxons congregate—viz , political Radicalism.
Which is why the rabidly Tory Pink ’Un and Winning
Post are greeted with open arms by stout English-speak-
ing people, who would swoon helplessly away if you
handed them a copy of the Daily News.
* » * *
Albert Dorrington has been unburdening his soul
in London Daily Chronicle on the subject of Australian
literature, and quite evidently his liver was troubling
him when he was writing his article. According to Dor-
rington, the newspaper owner in Australia regards the
literary contributor on the same plane as “ the vegetable
hawker, or the collector of old bottles or scrap lead.”
Another cause operating against the growth of Australia,
according to Dorrington, is “ the flash-and-fade tempera-
ment of the native-born literati, the literary twilighters
whose careers resemble the brief light which shimmers
between the Australian sunset and dark. ... It is not
uncommon to discover among the vast army of Aus-
tralian writers the names of mere boys and girls . . . the
bands of young scribes who appear at regular intervals on
the literary horizon. The greenest and freshest are im-
mediately chosen from the ranks, and, like damp, im-
mature cigars, are handed round to a waiting public.”
Those are some of tne expressions in the article, which is
far from being a just statement of the Lets, though it
does contain a considerable element of truth.
A man in India, having received, by request, specimen
copies of an Adelaide paper, emphasises the fact that he
does not intend to become a subscriber :—“ Sirs, I beg to
request you that kindly do not send to me as a subscriber
a paper. You have registered my name for the paper in
the list of subscribers, but I request to not to send it. I
have received your fine paper, but when I received one
copy as a sample (specimen) in payment of that one
copy postal-stamps had been sent for payment. If
you have not got please state the price of four
copies of the paper. If you will send a list of the
papers , which have been received by me, I will send
stamps for the four copies. I can’t send the whole
account of the paper for one year, because I only asked
for a specimen copy, but you have registered me as a
subscriber. Now please do not send a single copy of
paper again. I will be pleased to send a account pay-
ment for only four copies which have been sent by you.
Your paper will not be successful to me in Bombay, and
therefore I request you not to send paper again to me as
subscriber.—Yours obty. ——
THE EDITORIAL WE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are the corpse behind the walls
That writes of sea and sky ;
We are the individuals
Who obsoleted “ I.”
We are the men who turn the wheels—
Cohorts of Destinee 1
We are the saints whom no one paints—
The Co-essential We !
We’ve hitched our dreams unto the stars,
But, oh ! the plural curse
Has quite absorbed our brightest pars,
And commandeered our verse !
We are the sullen slaves who write
That all men may be free ;
But, as I said, we’re really dead—
Our coffin’s labelled “ WE ” 1
N.B.W.

“THE CHILDREN'S FOOD.”
Bung isn’t having a very cheerful time in
S. Australia. Another local option poll (this time
in the big Sturt electorate, covering all the south-
western suburbs) has given a vast majority in
favor of the time-reduction of one-third of the
licenses. Still, in spite of oceans of talk, only
a fourth of the electors voted. The busiest man
m the whole, campaign has been S. J. Jacobs,
chairman of the S.A. Brewing Co., who has been
on the stump for weeks past. But the Methody
parsons beat him. In most things the Methody
parsoti has S. Australia in the hollow of his hand.

On which Side of the Line
Do You Buy
Credit is a Commodity —just the same
as goods, and a very expensive Com-
modity. A Merchant who .buys credit
as well as goods, sells you his expen=
sive Commodity of credit, goods,
and profit combined.
You too may buy credit, or you may
be paying Cash at a credit-buying or
credit-giving establishment, but in
either case you are paying the credit
high price. The point is, if you can
pay Cash do you prefer to pay for these
credits, and for those losses of non-
payers, for that is what it means, or just
to buy the goods without these extra
charges attached. If you do, come over
the line!
Stewart Dawson & Co. manufacture or
buy from the actual Makers, Jewellery,
Precious Stones, Silverware, and fine
Leather Goods for Cash and sell them
to you for Cash. If you buy at Stewart
Dawson’s you will not have to pay
inflated credit prices, nor will you have
to pay the bad debts of others', but you
will purchase at prices 10 per cent, to
20 per cent, lower than at an average
shop—whose business and selling prices
are built up, not on a foundation of
value, but on credits and losses thereon.
Call at the Stewart Dawson Showrooms,
or write us, and realise the revelation
in price-saving.

An American paper recently devoted half a
page of its valuable space, and about a furlong of
headlines, to President Roosevelt’s method of cloth-
ing himself. It seems that Roosevelt is one of those
men who can t be bothered changing his clothes
four or rive times a day, and he .cheerfully
ignores all the established rules about wearing a
sac suit in the morning to the office, followed by
a sort of Beaufort, then a frock coat for afternoon
wear, and evening dress after six. His worst sin
—from the dress-expert’s point of view—is that he
will not conform to the fashions, and persists in
having h i garments made according to his own
ideas of comfort. He wears the wroner sort of
collar and the anathema sort of tie, and he has
buttons wherever he wants them, and his out-of-
date, straight-up-and-down frock coat is a service-
able garment, and makes him look like a man, in-
stead of having a waist and making him look
like a bad imitation of a monkey’s wife. Accord-
ing to the excited journal that criticises him so
minutely, Roosevelt gives tailors and clothes-horse
men a fearful amount of pain by his lack of taste.
This, however, should not lie an insuperable
obstacle. If Roosevelt is too busy to change his
clothing all day, let Congress create the office of
National Clothes Horse, and appoint someone to
the position to whom Rags are a Serious Matter.
The Public Clothes Horse might be made Vice-
President.

BRONCHITIS
CURE.
The Famous Remedy for
Coughs, Bronchitis, Pneumonia,
Pleurisy, Asthma <fe Consumption
BRONCHITIS and PNEUMONIA
Cured by
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. W. G. Hearne. Dear Sir, —From a strict sense
cf duty I feel that I should publish the following state-
ment, so that others may know what to do when the
awful fact is evident that a life is in danger of being
lost: —In September, 1906, my little girl, aged at that
time 3 years, contracted Measles, and in the following
October was attacked by Bronchitis, Pneumonia and
Congestion of the Lungs. She was attended by a
legally qualified doctor of high standing, but his treat-
ment was not successful in arresting the progress of the
illness. On Saturday, the 21st October, 1906, he said
that her life was in danger—that there was very little
hope for her. For eight days and nights she had been
prostrated by Cough, Pain and Fever, and was lying
like a statue, unconscious. At this stage I was per-
suaded by a friend to obtain Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure,
with its auxiliary medicine for the Fever and Conges-
tion of the Lungs, as directed in the Catalogue of
Medicines which accompanies each bottle of the Bron-
chitis Cure. I gave the medicine as directed, and there
was an improvement from the first dose of Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure. The improvement continued after
each dose of the medicine. In a week she was perfectly
free from the Pneumonia, Congestion, Cough, Pain and
Fever, and was well, except that she was still weak. In
a fo-tnight she was quite recovered, and is now in
splendid health, and stronger than ever. Any person
asking for information about this grand medicine can
be supplied by me, or by any of my neighbours who have
witnes-ed its wonderful effects. It absolutely snatched
my child from an early grave.—Yours gratefully,
„ . D. GARDINER,
Police Station, Geelong East, Feb. 5, 1908.

Are We Becoming
Bald-Headed ?
Baldness More Prevalent Abroad
than in England.
Are we becoming a bald-headed race ?
That is the question which thrusts itself into the mind of the inquiring observer after
an evening at any place of amusement or public assembly. Baldness is undoubtedly
largely on the increase, and the number of bald heads to be seen is quite alarming.
Somewhere in the Western interior of Australia, the Scottish explorer McKay says
that there exists such a race of people. They are not only devoid of hair upon their
heads and faces, but there is absolutely no trace of hirsutic growth upon their bodies.
1 hey are, in consequence, a feeble people, stunted in stature, and particularlv liable to
bronchial and chest diseases.
The fact of the matter is, that when abroad we do not, as a rule, devote the same care
and attention to the cultivation of a good head of hair as the average foreigner, and we
think that if we dash cold or hot water over it regularly, we are doing everything that we
can do to preserve it. But this is wrong. Except in a few cases, where there is a
naturally vigorous growth, the hair requires quite a different style of treatment. There is
no doubt that the lime in ordinary hard water is a depilatory, whereas what the hair
requires to keep it healthy is a non-harsh preparation (like “ Harlene ”) that will prevent
the scalp from becoming heated and dry, and also prevent the hair falling out. There is
no better preparation of this kind on the market than the many-years-old, tried-and-true
iavounte of Royalty, namely, “ Harlene,” which is very similar in quality and character
to the natural oil of the hair, and the absence or poverty of which is the direct cause of
nearly all baldness.
Harlene ” is both cleansing, tonic, and preservative, and may be used safely by
people of all ages. It may be obtained direct from the manufacturers, Messrs. Edwards’
Harlene Co., 95-6 High Holbom, London, W.C., and from any chemist or stores in the
Colonies. Furthermore, in order that “Harlene’s” merits may be tested before
adoption the proprietors will forward a free sample supply to all who send three penny
stamps (to cover postage) addressed as above.

EIGHT YEARS SUFFERING
A PERMANENT CURE.
THE CASE OF MRS. A. WEAVER.
BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.
No one can tell the self-denying work which
good men have done in the early days of this
country, neither can we exaggerate the nobleness
of spirit which animates present-day people in a
desire to ameliorate distress as much as lies in their
power. Therefore, the public should be informed
of some of these efforts, one of which was made by
Mrs. Alice Weaver, of No. 7 Louis Street, Redfern,
who replied to the reporter by saying :
“ About this time last year I was living in
Telopea Street, and it was just before I left there
for this place that I was fortunate in casting aside
a complaint that had worried me for no less than
eight years. What a fearful life of suffering mine
had been all that time, to be sure, and to think that
nothing in the world could benefit me made my
spirits droop lower than they will ever be again,
I hope.”
“What made you think that nothing could
benefit you ? ” queried the writer.
“I arrived at that conclusion because nothing
had done me good so far, and I believe I tried
every medicine going. At the time my second
child was born I was under a doctor, who did
everything he could for me, but still my health re-
mained as bad as it could be. Indeed, at that
period I was nearly dead with indigestion, al-
though I dieted myself and did all that could be
done to keep my torments in check. It was of no
avail, though, for when I ate anything I could
hardly breathe owing to a feeling which came like
a lump in my chest, from which place a sharp pain
went right through to the centre of my shoulders.
I then remained in the utmost agony until the food
was vomited up again, after which I felt so ex-
hausted that I could not think of doing a thing in
the house until I had had a good rest. It would
surprise you if you only knew how poor my appetite
was. You would wonder how anyone could keep
alive with it, and as for my head, oh, my sufferings
with it were awful. The most piercing pains at
onetime, and themost depressingachesat another.
Really, I think it was a marvel how I kept my
senses with the agony I endured in the head ; and
it’s strange that I did not meet with many a nasty
accident through the giddiness which used to over-
take me. I was a misery to myself and everybody
around me, and to tell you the honest truth I never
thought I would live through it all. My eyes used
to feel heavy enough to drop out of their sockets,
and I was affected in the mouth by such a bitter
taste that it made me feel sick of a morning, after
putting in the usual restless nights that had done
much towards making a wreck of me. Even in the
daytime I wasalwaysimagining something terrible
was going to happen, and these were just the very
things that Clements Tonic, which I commenced to
take, showed itself to be the master of ; for it sent
such a vigor through my frame, when I had been
taking it a couple of weeks, that I could not help
feeling joyful in the belief that at last my health
had taken a beneficial turn. I got more Clements
Tonic, and as each bottle was finished, so did my
prospects improve, for I commenced to take my
meals with a degree of heartiness which astonished
my people as much as it pleased them ; and to my
own satisfaction anddelight theformer discomforts
attending my meals were quickly out of existence.
Oh, dear, what a changed woman I am now to then.
My limbs do not ache from weakness like they
formerly did, and I can go about my work as
cheerfully and as actively as any housewife in the
country. Ido not spend any restless nights now;
never dream during my sleep, and the pains that
used to abound have long since been driven away
from every part of my body. Well, Clements
Tonic made me the healthy woman I am, and for
the sake of other sufferers I trust you’ll publish
this interview in any way you think fit.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Alice Weaver, of Fo. 7 Louis Street, Redfern, in the
State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, and that it contains, and is
a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by
Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to
publish in any way my statements, which I give voluntary,
without receiving any payment, and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true,
and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1000.
(Signed) ALICE WEAVER.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern, this sixth day of
September, one thousand nine hundred and four, before
-ene, L. BREARLEY. 4,».

And brows of summer cloud,
And Auckland girls are stately,
And Auckland girls are gay—
But it’s not the girls of Auckland
I’m thinking of to-day.
()h, lass ies of Dunedin !
Your eyes are just as briyht;
They ylow with happy beauty,
They shine with so fter light;
And, lassies of Dunedin,
The gentle southern sun
Has touched your cheeks with color
Of blushes half-begun !
In Wellington are ladies
Not lacking queen-like grace
For poise of head and shoulders,
And clear, wind-freshened face —
Full-breasted like an empress,
With straight limbs swinging free—
Yet little is the heart-call
Of Wellington for me.
Oh, lassies of Dunedin,
Through all your veins there thrills
A blood made warm with climbing
The stiff Dunedin hills !
Therefore no city’s women
Can match your necks and hips.
There are no lips to challenge
The freshness of your lips.
The girls that live in Christchurch
Have glorious waving hair,
And some are dark and regal,
And some are sweet and fair ;
And Christchurch girls are merry,
And Christchurch girls are bright,
But it’s not a Christchurch maiden
I’m wishing for to-night.
Oh, lassies of Dunedin,
Your laughter runs like gold.
For you, the joy of living,
And hearts that grow not old.
To lightsome tunes are beating
The staunch, good hearts of you ;
And, lassies of Dunedin,
Your lips and hearts are true !
For we have gone by Roslyn,
And over Maori Hill,
And by the hawthorn hedges
That lead to Roslyn Mill,
And climbed to Cargill’s Castle
A long December day,
And the lassies of Dunedin
Went singing all the way.
Oh, lassies of Dunedin,
I hear your laughter yet,
The elder days are calling.
And I shall not forget.
Oh, home of silvery laughter!
Oh, house of honest speech !
To you come all contentment,
And one true heart to each !
How through the winter gaslight,
Along the snow-hushed street,
We went to merry dances
With gracious girls and sweet!
How, girt in dainty vesture
And filled with warm delight,
The lassies of Dunedin
Waltzed through the winter night!
Oh, lassies of Dunedin !
When shall I see again
The tender eyes I once saiv
Like bush new-washed with rain !
Whatever land I come to,
Whatever doom I dree,
It’s one Dunedin lassie
That holds the heart of me I
maoriland.
T.P.
The Farmer’s Daughter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TMHE cow-bail was built of bark, and was
large enough to accommodate six cows. In
winter it was up to the knees in mud, in
snminer over the ankles in dust, in both seasons
Mdescribably filthy. But these things were of
die least concern to Mary Gunter and her father,
lhey were used to them.
Mary was finishing the last few cows late one
simmer evening. Her father was up at the dairy,
which explained the familiar attitude of Bill
reenleaf, who leaned comfortably against the
Post and watched Mary’s skilful fingers
squeezin’ the cow juice,” as he put it. All
the time he kept a furtive eye on the door of the
•htiry- Mary was leaning against the flank of the
a,v dreamily. One of her eyes had a squint,
sad seemed to be watching the restless tail of the
while the other looked clean at and through
“I- Her face was slightly freckled, and her nose
seemed to have been flattened from constant
Pressure against the ribs of innumerable milkers.
Just now her cheek was against the cow, for she
''shed to look at Bill. Her ear seemed to tickle
I''eo 1 '' eow ’ a nd it swung its tail round so much that
1 1 began to speculate when it would catch Mary
!" “ er Btra ight eye. He became so interested that
almost lost the thread of his talk, and gave
the impression that he was absorbed by her
' ufy, so she stared harder and tried to raise a
“y blush.
u *^ re y’on fer riskin’ me ? ” said Bill.
Will yer risk the dad ? ” she replied, and
to wink as the tail swished past her eye.
I don’t think ’e\counts so very much if you’ll
take me.”
“Then yer really and truly loves me, Bill.”
ine milk gurgled excitedly into the bucket, and
the cow kicked towards Bill, as if it suspected he
might be tickling its flanks.
Yes, Mary, I trooly loves yer, and wants yer
ter marry me.” The sparkle in Mary’s straight
eye deepened, and Bill watched her anxiously,
to see if there was a gleam of love in it, and at
the same time he saw the tail give a preliminary
swing. His eyes lighted with interest, and left
Mary s face to watch it. With a sudden twisting
swish it flung round, and for a brief instant Mary’s
eye vanished.
“ Got yer,” he said.
' I’ll be dommed ef y’ave, ’’ said Mary rising in
sudden wrath, and before Bill could turn, she
flung the whole bucket of milk, bucket and all,
over him. Even the plainest girl hates to be
thought cheap.
It took Bill a month to explain that he did not
mean what she thought he meant. But her final for-
giveness was sudden as unexpected. It was on
the day of the arrival of her cousin, Kate Willow.
Bill had often heard Mary speak of her cousin ;
but not taking any particular interest, he never
remembered what she had said. He only retained
an impression that Mary considered her a
very pretty girl. When he saw her, he came
to the conclusion that Mary had very much
understated the case; or he had forgotten most
of what she had said. There was no hope of
meeting Kate in the house ; for Mr. Gunter re-
garded every young man as a prospective thief.
Most particularly was he suspicious of Bill. So
was Mary, which perhaps justified her misunder-
standing his remark at the cow bail.
Kate Willow was riding into the township
beside her cousin when Bill first saw her. He
said afterwards that he nearly fell off his horse,
but that was probably an exaggeration. Mary
said he looked like a fool when she introduced
him. Which was probably true. Kate was a
fair girl of 18. Her hair was still down, though
her figure was already that of a woman ; her skin
was fresh, her eyes blue and bright as good
health and simple-mindedness could make them.
She appeared shy at first, and Bill found her shy-
ness fascinating. After going to the township he
felt that there was only one girl in the world for
him, and that was Kate Willow. Mary was quiet
at first, and seemed distressed. She cheered up a
little by the time they reached home, and dis-
missed Bill with a curtness which he didn’t see.
As they were undressing that night, Mary looked
at herself in the glass, and came to the conclusion
she had no show against Kate, which was curious,
after the bucket incident. Then she asked :
“ What do you think of Bill ? ”
Kate giggled. “’E’s a gawk,” she said, and
giggled some more. Mary understood.
“ Anyway, ’e’s a fool,” said Mary. “’E wanted
ter marry me.”
Kate shrieked with laughter, till Gunter
thumped on the wall and bade her be quiet.
Mary’s freckled face turned scarlet. She began
to think that she didn’t like Kate. “ I wouldn’t
’ave ’im at any price,” she continued. “All’e
wants is me money. At least dad thinks so.”
“ Is ’e poor ?” asked Kate.
“ Poor as eight brothers and sisters and a mort-
gaged farm can make ’im. Still ’e’s strong, ’an
can work.” She said this very thoughtfully.
Bill brought up the cows the next evening. He
used to do it to see Mary. This time he hardly
looked at her, but watched the house. Mary buried
her face in the cow’s flank.
“D’yer think Kate’s pretty, Bill?” she asked
carelessly.
“A bit,” he answered casually, still eagerly
gazing at the house.
“She’s cornin’ in for a good big farm some
day,” said Mary, softly. This was an invention.
“ Blow the farm ! ” said Bill, looking quickly
down at her. Her face was still hidden, and
Spotty’s tail swished her back hair unreproved.
“I mus’ be goin’ now, Mary,” he said, reluc-
tantly. Then he bolted. Mr. Gunter had caught
sight of him, and was rushing to the cowyard
bellowing wild threats.
■***■»
A month passed, and Kate was still with her
cousin. By this time they had discovered they
had very little in common, and Mr. Gunter dis-
covered that his house was under some kind of
surveillance. Several times he had seen a shadow
fitting out of sight when he went out for wood after
the girls were in bed. He suddenly grew suspicious,
and, sneaking to the door of Mary’s room, he
softly opened it and peeped in. There were two
figures in bed. Mary didn’t think it worth while
telling him that one of them was built out of a
pillow. She was loyal to Kate, though she had
begun to hate her badly. Kate was always letting
her see that she would never have a chance of
getting a husband, and saying things which re-
ferred to her plainness and its drawbacks. It
requires a lot of patience, or a lot of sense, for a
girl to put up with that sort of thing, particularly
from one prettier than herself. Mary must have
had a great deal both of patience and
sense, for she listened with downcast eyes,
yet with that appearance of interest which might
have warned a less conceited girl than Kate.
When Bill first sneaked up to their window, and
whistled, Kate giggled and nudged Mary
vigorously, giggled again, and pulled the clothes
over her head. She asked Mary what she ought
to do, but was out of bed and dressing before she
could answer. In a week Kate did not trouble to
undress, and the pillow was sufficent evidence
for Mr. Gunter’s casual eyes.
* * * *
Kate had taken to going into the township,
ostensibly for the mail. Bill tried to go for the
mail too, but his dad had something more useful
for him to do, so it was one of Bill’s younger
brothers whom Kate always met. She was
learning to dislike Bill’s younger brother.
One morning Kate was standing on the plat-
form waiting for the train, when Mrs. Jones, who
lived on the side of Gunter’s farm nearer the
township, came along with two of her daughters,
bovine-eyed lasses of sixteen and seventeen. Mrs.
Jones didn’t see Kate till one of her chicks went
to speak to her. Then she broadened like a
flustered hen, and uttering “ Cissie ! ” in a tone
exactly resembling a hen calling her chickens to
cover, she passed on with an outward thrust of
her chin and a tilt of the nose that made Kate
turn scarlet. She crawled to the end of the plat-
form, her ears singing and her heart bursting
with wrath, and with a face which tried its best
to appear coldly indifferent.
An hour later she burst into the kitchen where
Mary was busy making bread, and amidst angry
tears and angrier questions she related what had
happened. Mary listened silently, as usual, her
unexpressive features struggling hard either to
show or suppress sympathy.
“ Some o’ the Jones kids must ’a seen yer out
with Bill,” she said at last.
“There wasn’t no ’arm in that, was there? ’
and Kate tossed her head.
The Jones kids had seen Bill vigorously kissing
Kate behind the barn. Mary had told them there
was a native cat in that barn, which might be

caught about 10 o’clock. She did not tell Kate,
