* * * *
“ Bill,” said Mary next evening, when she was
making the milk hiss and bubble into the pail,
“ Dad’s goin’ away for a couple o’ days from
ter-morrer. ”
“I ’opes yer won’t be lonely, Mary,” said Bill,
eagerly.
■‘l’ve Kate,” she answered, pressing her nose
deeply into the cow’s side. The cow kicked
against the leg rope fretfully.
“ There’ll only be the two of yous,” said Bill,
hopefully. He just missed a kick from Spotty’s
free hind leg, as he leaned against the post behind
her.
“Kate’s thinkin’ o’ goin’ ’ome soon.” Mary
turned and looked at him from between the cow’s
hindlegs and tail as she spoke, and saw his face
fall.
“ Anythin’ wrong,” he asked.
“’Er father’s lost ’is farm,” she said sadly.
(This was also an invention.) Bill’s face was a
study in conflicting emotions, mostly fright.
“ What ’orrible luck,” he said.
“ Who for ? ” she asked, innocently.
“’Er father,” he answered, quickly, trying hard
to read the differently directed eyes of Mary, and
remembering that to see a squint was unlucky.
That night Kate lay on her bed dressed. At
nearly midnight she grumpily dragged off her
clothes. Mary said Bill was a low down sort of
sneak. Kate agreed; then she defended him.
Mary agreed with the defence and invented
further excuses for him, which Kate said were
rubbish. Mary thought they might be, too. But
there was no pleasing Kate, who sat up at every
sound, only to drop back with a sigh. Mary got
tired of it, and told her to go to sleep. She wept
instead. In the morning she got up with a hard,
set look on her face, and announced briefly that
she was going for a long walk.
Mary sang at her work all that day. There
was the making of a fine cynic in Mary, for she was
pleased to think that Kate was a bigger fool
than herself. She felt no real malice towards
Bill, though she had told her father to call at
Mrs. Jones’ for a pattern on his way back.
* * » *
The sun had just set when Mary was finishing
her last cow on the following evening. The day
had been hot, and she was unusually slow. Bill
was leaning against the post, carefully watching
her straight eye. The other eye was intent on
Spotty’s vindictive tail, and he was not curious
whether the tail caught it or not. Mary was
becoming a puzzle; a man always gets interested
in a woman when she puzzles him. He had
blurted out that he wanted Kate “to explain
things to her,” he said. Mary didn’t even smile.
The puzzled frown had deepened on his forehead,
and he was just beginning to think he ought to
explain things to Mary, remembering suddenly
that he had said he loved her. He began to
sweat, and had already mopped his forehead
several times. Then he saw Mary smile. The
sound of a quickening footstep reached his ears.
The smile deepened. He turned lazily, and
found himself looking into the angry eyes of Mr.
Gunter. There was no time to walk away, and
he would not run. Mr. Gunter was a big
brawny man, with heavy eyebrows that met, and
gave him a peculiarly savage aspect when he was
angry.
“So you’ve bin ruinin’the good name of me
niece, Kate, have yer, yer unlicked whelp. Seen
kissin’ her behin’ me barn, eh ? S’pose yer think
she’s cornin’ irt fer money, too, eh?” His voice
rose. With a fine assumption of indifference
Mary released Spotty, and, picking up her pail,
walked slowly to the dairy. There she hastily
emptied the bucket and went to a small window.
She saw two figures in a cloud of dust. A large,
thick one was blindly butting and hitting; a long,
thin one, which was Bill, was blindly defending
itself and being forced back to the fence, where it
got a blow that sent its head with a bang on to the
high rail. Mary ran swiftly back to the yard.
Bill was on the ground now, vainly struggling to
get up under a rain of blows from a split picket.
His nose was bleeding, and one eye was black.
She did not see that both the eyes of her father
were black.
“ Stop it, dad ! ” she screamed.
“ I’ll teach’im ter play the blackguard about
me barn !”
“Stop it!” screamed Mary more loudly. He
took no notice. Rushing to the bail, Mary ripped
off another picket. Mr. Gunter gasped as a
whack on the chest knocked the wind out of him.
Dropping his stick he turned on Mary furiously,
to be checked by another on his head. Mary was
Dutch-built, with plenty of breadth in the beam,
and long in the arm. Luckily her dad had on a thick
felt hat. As he raised it to feel his head, he saw
his undutiful daughter throw herself beside Bill,
and begin to mop his face with her skirt. Then
she took his head on her lap and kissed him.
Mr. Gunter walked away in a state of helpless
bewilderment.
• * * *
When Kate returned she was tired out and
snappy-tempered. After tea she said she would
return home in the morning, and did not speak
again till they were in bed. Then Mary spoke
softly:—
“ Kate.” she sighed.
“ Kate,” insisted Mary.
“ What is it ? ” mumbled Kate, surlily.
“D’yer remember me tellin’ yer Bill ast me ter
marry’im?” There was a dreadful silence. “’E
ast me agen ter-night.” Kate sat up with a jerk.
“ And what did you say?”
“ Nothin’ much —only ‘ yes.’ ”
Kate lay down and wept for the rest of the
night.
W. BRENTA.
The Goat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN Newcastle (N.S.W.) the rank odor of
the male goat mingles in some quantity with
the heavy, coal-laden atmosphere. The
Queensland towns may boast of more numerous
herds, but for smashage of the Ten Command-
ments the jam-tin goes to a herd of rank and
truculent venison which haunts the Newcastle
wharfs.
We were coming down from the North, and had
to lie up for repairs to the stomach of the ship.
The time hung on our hands like a curate who had
committed suicide, so to lighten the dead weight
of the evening the second engineer suggested that
we should sally forth and capture a few goats.
The goats just then came down and tried to eat
the ship’s repairs, and the perfume of the Head
Goat was lofty, even as the crest of Chimborazo.
The captain exclaimed that, if he owned half
of that Billy, he’d kill his half. We took this for
a command, as King Henry’s hangers-on did
when the monarch said he could struggle through
without T. A’Beckett. It was raining as the Devil
reigned in Heaven before he was bounced from
the ranks of the Seraphim. We told the captain
we would be back before that chaliced hour when
the dark mantle of night is drawn from off the
golden tresses of morn. Then we stepped ashore
and buffeted our march towards a dark, for-
bidding bump in the landscape, behind which we
had previously investigated the enemy preparing
for the night’s restlessness. The second mate was
first, and the first engineer came last. The first
mate was in the middle, and the third
mate was fourth. Behind the fifteenth engi-
neer marched the hordes of Small Boy,
and Chips, our Chinese ship’s carpenter.
The Chinese crew was with us to a woman—they
couldn’t be called men by reason of their skirts.
Chips was clad in all the glory of the Orient. He
had upon him a blue blouse of eatable cut, a
passionate pair of baggy pants, and a pair of fried
leathers.
Despite the opposition of a reptile press and
the machinations of the Labor party, we mo-
bilised ourselves and rushed upon those goats.
Immediately the Chief Goat put his head down,
and butted Chips right in the forecastle. Chips
went down like the Polish monarchy in 1793 or
thereabouts, and fell into a state of profound
reticence. He was aroused from deep stunned
thought by the Chief Goat eating his clothes,
which, as already mentioned, embodied all the
glories of the East and vaguely suggested what
the Taj Mahal would have been if it had been
painted bright red and had cost 3s. fid. He leaped
up, and kicked the animal on the thickest part of
its fragrance, and bounced right up into the
atmosphere and again dropped temporarily dead.
While he was dead the animal made a champagne
supper off his boots. It was devouring his pigtail
when Chips awoke some more, and in frenzy he
again kicked the loftiest pinnacle of the William’s
odor, and sank down with a shriek, a broken toe,
and a heartrending cry of “ Whaffor ? ”
Just here the general battle had deployed, and,
getting mixed, had retired in disorder to the
original scene, leaving behind it its artillery and
most of its prestige. The engineer said that
if Australia had a system of compulsory military
training this disgrace would never have fallen
upon us. He was moved to tears, and recited ex-
tracts from “Grey’s Elephant in a Country
Churchyard.” We rescued Chips. Then we re-
turned to the ship carrying one nanny as the
plunder. The captain concealed his joy at seeing
us, and said he would stop the goat out of our
wages, which he did. And what I want to know
is : Have we a good ground of action in the High
Court ?
THE DRUNK.
The Seeker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE warm air blew against my face,
Its heavy rose and lily scent
Slow-pacing up her starlit halls,
Unto her throne the Queen-Moon went.
Far down, lit by a million lamps,
The town lay dreaming at my feet,
And, close at hand, a singer trilled
A happy love-song, old and sweet.
The music set my heart on fire,
I, whom no man had ever kissed,
Whose youth had been a barren thing,
Who had life’s sweetest glory missed.
My dying youth awoke again,
My old heart flamed with discontent,
And down into the dreaming town
In search of love and joy I went.
But, blinded by the myriad lights,
I stumbled over ev’ry stone ;
The laughing crowd in groups went by,
But I passed on my way alone.
Then one came near with godlike steps,
With glowing face and careless eyes,
A happy song upon his lips,
A song of spring and sunlit skies.
I barred his way with outstretched arms,
And cried of love and longing heart,
And my lone bower upon the hills,
From all the sordid world apart;
There we would dream through summer days,
And when night came with shadow deep,
Beneath the glory of the stars
My lips should sing his soul to sleep.
He looked me, scornful, through and through,
He said, “ Though you are passing fair,
Your eyes have autumn’s fading tints,
And winter’s snows are on your hair.
My godlike youth is in its prime,
My heart must find a spring-born mate ;
For autumn’s but a mourning time,
And winter nights are desolate.”
So, crushed and shamed, I passed along,
My erstwhile joyous hopes laid low,
Till one came by with snow-white hair
Through all the bustle limping slow. s
Then thought I, to Midwinter’s chill
Dull Autumn’s days are Summer’s prime,
Though we may think the wind blows drear,
They call it Spring in northern clime.
I laid my hand upon his arm,
And cried, “ Oh, surely we may love,
We both have lived and suffered long ;
Come to my hill-set bower above.
Within that sunnier land may we
Find Happiness and Youth again,
And Love may come to dwell with us,
And Joy be born of years of pain.” 1
He stroked my whitening red-brown hair,
His trembling voice was strangely kind.
He said, “ Go, seek your hill-set bovyer
And leave the noisy town behind.
Dream not of blissful joyous hours
That still may be your heritage,
For Youth’s the only time to love,
And madcap Joy’s not born of Age.l-
- sad grey dawn was in the sky,
As back unto my bower I went,
My heart’s red fire to ashes burnt
With wisdom grown of discontent.
And but a string of words have I
My lonely longings to assuage—
“ Youth is the only time to love,
And madcap Joy’s not born of Age.”
N.S.W.
R.B.

(To the Editor of the “ Star," Sydney.)
Sir,—Under the above heading-, your Saturday issue of 2nd inst.
states that the men engaged on sewer works “contend that the smoke
from rackarock, which is largely used, is specially irritating and
injurious.”
I introduced “Rackarock" from America in 1885, and until the
patents expired, in about 1894, I manufactured every ounce that was
used in Australasia, amounting to some hundreds of tops. Since that
time some brands have been placed on the market at prices with which I
could not compete without lowering the quality, which I would not do.
Unfortunately, many people consider an article at, say, 4s. must be
cheaper than ahother at 55., whereas, in actual work,'the low-priced
stuff may be 50 per cent, dearer.
With regard to my “ Rackarock,” I would state that if the holes are
Sewer Construction a Menace.
properly charged and tamped the combustion is complete, and there are
practically no fumes.
A good explosive saves cost in drilling, as less of it is required to shift
the burden, as it can be put into the bottom of the hole and leave plenty of
room, for efficient tamping, whereas a larger quantity of the poorer quality
is required to shift the same burden, and ifry often there is not sufficient
room left for good tamping, consequently the combustion is imperfect,
and the drive is filled with the smoke and fumes to which the men object.
Blasting is a talent, and I may safely say that in about every SO
miners one man would have the “ instinct ” which would enable him to do
at least 25 per cent, more work ivith the same amount of explosives than
anyone of the remaining 19. This man would know' to an ounce the
quantity of explosive to Use to shift a burden, consequently he would never
overload a hole, which always results in Jumes and extra cost on the
explosives bill.
If I had a contract only employing .four men, I would find one of
these men better at blasting than the other three, and in my own iterestt
I would place all the blasting in his hands, as I know my expl five bill
would be far less, the men could work in the headings under better condi-
tions, and with one man in charge of the explosives, which should a l be the
very best of their kind, the risk of accident would be almost nil. .
In the Sydney sandstone “Rackarock” is by far the most effioien
explosive, and if properly used, would cause the men much less discom
fort than any other class of explosive.
There is no doubt in my mind that every man engaged on the sewer
jobs will join me in heartily thanking you for ventilating this matter as
you have done, and which I trust will result in better conditions an
round.—Yours, etc., R- A. HERVEY.
Sydney, May 4, ’OB.
